THE BED PAGE.
N.S.W. Art Society.
THE Art Society of New South Wales is
luring interested Australians to its twenty-
first annual exhibition at 70 Pitt-street,
Sydney. From that catalogue where so
much humour is subtly hidden, one learns that the
Society has sold at its exhibitions pictures priced
at £7469 ; that its subsidy from Govt, is expended
entirely in art education; that it will award a
travelling scholarship next year if Govt, provides
the money ; and that among well-known artists
educated by the Society are F. P. Mahony B E
Minns, Percy Spence, C. Conder, A. J. Fischer’
A. ,T. Hanson, J. S. Watkins, A. R. Coffey!
J. Wohnski, J, Salvana, C. E. S. Tindall, Sid
Long, G. W. Lambert, H. Garliclc, &c.
The pictures now shown form an interesting
collection ; and compared with last year’s they
are more various and more charming. Given that
Art in Australia is pursued in hobbles ; that the
majority of the painters are still learning the rules
of the game, that attempts at sculpture rarely pass
beyond clay modelling; that the force of developed
fen ius is nowhere manifest as yet;—then the Art
ociety’s exhibition does the members credit.
The most noticeable exhibitor this year is A. R.
Coffey, who has worked : succeeded as far as his
present talent permits ; and painted better pictures
than in, any previous year. He exhibits both
landscape and portrait work ; and in a large
composition called “ Curse the Gold ! ” he tries his
imaginative wings, and falls without hurting him-
self. He is decidedly to be congratulated on his
pluck and achievement. The chief fault to be
found with him seems to lie in a certain hardness
of temperament which prevents personal
emotion from showing in his pictures, and
which leaves them rather in the category of
clever pictures than in that of fine art. His
large landscape, “Under the Ti-trees,” *
purchased by N.S.W. Gallery, is un-
commonly well painted. It is well seen,
truly realised, and transfers to canvas a
beautiful glimpse of Nature. There is
velvet shade in the foreground, the dense
branches of the trees overhead, and be-
neath the branches a bright daylight vista.
The stray sun-ray which shines through
the branches to the foreground gives the
illusion of real sunlight. Yet the picture
is a little hard, a little garish, a little too
dazzling. Even the shadows are not cool.
One admires—and turns away with relief.
What does that mean ? Either, if the glare
was not in the scene the painter saw, that
he has missed some subtle tones of shade ;
or, if the glare was in the scene, that he
has painted too well, lias not subordinated
a natural crudity to his artistic effect. An
artist should never forget that he must
transfuse Nature through himself to obtain
the highest result: even her miracles he
must merge in his own mood. There was
never a spectacle of Nature so grand that
the spirit of Man could not rise above it,
grander.
In “ Curse the Gold ! ” A. R. Coffey re-
presents two men in a desert apparently
meant for Westralian. One is reclining,
sick or dying, near an empty water-bag :
the other with the left arm supports him,
with the right thrusts to Heaven that
accusing fist which is the popular embodi-
ment of a curse. An exploring person who
has been there complains that the shadows
are too light for the strong sun overhead :
it might be added that there is little energy
in the thrust arm, no grip in the accusing
fist—and so on. But details like these
matter little in view of the main fact that
the picture does not arrest, does not con-
vince. It takes a commonplace idea,, the
most obvious pose for the idea ; and
there is not yet force enough in the artist
to justify the idea and the pose. The
men hardly live, and the curse is dead. Never-
theless, this is a class of picture one feels grateful
to our artists for attempting, whether they fail or
not.
Throughout A. R. Coffey’s work comes the same
defect. His colours are warm ; his pictures are
cold. Now a good picture, like a good poem, is
not an exercise in manualor intellectual dexterity:
it is a manifestation of forcible emotion. And A.
R. Coffey exhibits a hard brilliance without any
emotion worth mentioning. It is so in his clever
study of rock and wave, “A Rugged Coast.” It
is so in his “ Moonlight on the Hawkesbury.” ' In
the pretty watercolour, “Two Ketches,” and the
attractive pastel, “The Lady with the Veil,” hi»
energy seems to want just a touch of softness, a
glimpse of feeling, to make its result perfect. Bub
he goes through all media with the same rigid
barrier between himself and his subject. He has
done good work ; but if he can break down that
barrier he will do great work.
* • • •
Pass to A. J. Daplyn. It is a fashion among
some of our younger artists to pity “poor old
Daplyn ; ” —and some of them would be fortunate
if they could compass the sympathy between
Daplyn’s brush and his subject. In the present
exhibition, his talent is perhaps shown to greater
advantage than in any exhibition previously.
His worked is keyed as low in colour as Coffey’s
is keyed high; and he shows some of the
emotion which Coffey lacks. But his energy
“ The Return of the Pearl-
Fishers .”— A. G. Plat.
does not rise to
the level of
Force, and his
emotion doesnot
rise to the level
of Passion. It
is rather senti-
ment—a little
tricked, maybe;
a little faded;
but tender and
charming with-
al. And Daplyn
knows his limits,
and paints with-
in them and
up to them.
Read an old
love’s letter and
stroll with a
mild cigar and
a memory un-
der a shaded
moon, and you
will receive
something like
Daplyn’s im-
pression. He car-
ries his senti-
ment into his
titles. “The
Moon is up, yet ’t is not Night”—the
picture which N.S.W. Gallery bought—is hinted
by the drawing on this page. It has glamour, but
the glamour is a trifle elderly and familiar. For
myself, I would prefer ‘ ‘ The Lagoon, Narra-
been,” bought for 40gs. less by some discriminat-
mg person. It is a villainous simile, but “The
Lagoon ” seems to me a fresher infusion than
“Ihe Moon”—where the tea-leaves might have
been dried and decocted over again. And
Daplyn keeps on. Not so well in his large
“Shades of Evening”—is the lighting bad ? —but
m his “Marsh-land” and “Where Creek and
River Meet ” —and in others in a lesser degree.
He is selling, too ; the public have sense, after all
somebody picked up Sophie Steffanoni’s plea-
sant glimpse of “ Mount Keira from Wollongong
Beach ” cheaply at four guineas.
* * * «
Lister, the solid man ? He slogs away. With
a vast of labour, a deal of skill, and no soul worth
speaking of, he covers his huge canvases year after
year with Nature as he sees her ; and he sees her
weH. She shines for him ; but. she never glorifies
him, never transfigures him, or he her. There is
some impediment to their marriage. Sometimes
his skill imitates soul so well (in his smaller
pictures, usually) that you are almost deceived.
Never quite. The hem of her garment he clutches
often; sometimes the garment; never the god-
dess wholly. Always she herself escapes. And he
follows, follows—through good sales and bad sales
he follows. I admire that man. And I wish, I
wish
* * * *
.Albert Hanson appears in -some, dozen land-
scapes. He has skill; he has emotion ; neither in
surpassing degree. He knows what to jjaint, and
he paints well; but fails in the force to send his
work past pretty-good into very-good. Yet his
“ The Moon is tip, yet Hi's not Night.”—A. J. Daplyn.
work is attractive : everybody should have one
Hanson on his wall. There are “ A Bend in the
Teremakau River”; “ Otira Gorge’’—priced
high : “ The Oaks by the Lake, Narrabeen ”;
“ Nafrabeen Lagoon ’’—priced low.
Horace Moore-Jones works; he is improving
his work : it is still a little meretricious, a little
flashy; wanting simplicity, sincerity, good man-
ners.
(This is not in order of merit; the all-but-best
picture is to come.)
Robert M. Ronald, a young man from Armi-
dale Technical School, shows promising work : it
has delicacy, syinDathy--and weakness still.
This space represents Frank Mahony, who is
practically Unrepresented. About five years ago
somebody sent to The Bulletin fr Jm Queensland
a poem about a dreamer, and in the last line “ The
Dreamer lay with the unknown dead.” I don’t
know that he wasn’t just as well off; but when
one has talent one might just as well be better
off. However, there is a big picture at home—for
next year. And, of course. -. . . Still
Ekeu, fugaces ...
* • • •
Nerli is unfortunate m bringing his
talent to several pictures which seem un-
likely to sell in this community. His chief
picture, an idea -of “ Cosette,” has a
striking face (though it is rouged enough
for lan tine), and the figure stays in
memory; but, once the Gallery has passed
who is there to pay the 100 guineas which
are not an extravagant price—if one wanted
it ?
Dattilo Rubbo’s excellent painting of the
figure of an old woman seems in the same
class: there is no market for old women.
A much more attractive picture comes in
ms heads of three old smokers, types of
‘Rose, Thistle, and Shamrock’’—full of charac-
ter, well painted, and with a good human interest
m them.
Clement Blackman sends one Tasmanian land-
scape-^-well conceived and well executed—one
mood of Nature caught and fixed.
Anybody with a guinea and a place for a- small
panel of red roses should buy Hetty Dymock’s
No. 78 —just as good as some priced at five times
as much.
Toni Roberts gives one picture—a lady in “A
French Hat,” dexterous and dainty. Worth, on
a , c , om P^ ruson of prices, 15gs. ; priced, lOgs.
sold. (An old dog for the local Art road.)

Arthur Oollingridge, like the others, is not to
be valued in proportion to price or size of canvas.
Somebody was lucky enough to get the best ex-
ample of his art on show—“ Clearing ” —for sgs.
, Simihriy with C. 11. Hunt. His large picture,
Strayed ” —counts for nothing. A nice little
girl m a nice little gown and hat, holding a nice
little flower in a nice little way', is pleasant to see
against a background of bush and rocks; but she
does not look in the least “strayed” or worth
lOOgs. And there is a small water-colour at 3gs.,
“Near Woy Woy,” which holds the value of Art.
and which should in due proportion be priced at
250 gs.
“ The first sail in the offing of Port Jackson,
6th May, 1770,” by Blagdon Chambers, may illus-
trate some artists’ point of view. The conception
is excellent. You have to show the surprise and
terror of the Australian aboriginal at the onset of
this strange harbinger, animal, maybe gckl. What
is the chief point of your picture? The abori-
ginal and his emotions, of course. The scenery
and the sail are incidentals—to be indicated with
greater or less fullness as the picture will permit.
So you bring barbarism close to you—its gesture
of. agitation, its play of feature, its attitude
of fear. Highly dramatic, this clash of old
and new ! A fine opportunity for a study,
of aboriginals, a display of human emotion,
with the underlying idea to point it all. But
Blagdon Chambers does not see things that way.
His blacks, his scenery, his sail, take nearly equal
importance. The result is that the ideh i 3
frittered away. The blacks are brought close
enough to suggest, but not to display emotion (the
male lying down looks about nine feet long, by
the way—and the cliff to the right may be rising
from the water or lying nearly flat uponit); so the
picture is wasted for want of a key, a climax.
“The literary idea of art” is decried ; but in
imaginative subjects a notion of literary perspec-
tive (which is one with all imaginative perspective)
would a”"- 1 - help ~sme artists ?
» » • *
J. WoMaski’s picture, “After life’s fitful fever
he sleeps well,” comes up for criticism under,.dif-
ferent conditions from those which held at the
students’ competition. It represents a man just
dead in bed, with a bowed and weeping wife,
a hardly-understanding little girl like “The
Widower’s,” and a sleeping infant in a cradle—all
the material for a grief-impression. And by dint
of very careful, very complete painting, it does
leave the impression of death and grief
(though it is only the title which assures you
the man is really dead). Further, the painting
is so good, the detail so complete, the figures so
well posed, that the impression deepens with
acquaintance. It is a picture which reflects great
credit on the painstaking talent of the artist. But
it is not a picture on the high plane of art. Wh‘y ?
Firstly, because it deals with a hackneyed idea in
the most obvious vJay. You imagine the artist
saying, “ How should the woman be posed ?—Just
so”—and posing her just so. “ How would the
child stand?—Just so’’—and she stands just so.
Of course, every artist does that—he has to do it ;
and if he leave the obvious attitude for
the extraordinary, ten to one he will
lose his impression. But a figure should
neither be commonplace nor outr6— it should be
lifted just sufficiently from the commonplace to
give a result of freshness, of “ slight novelty
lifted not far enough to make it improbable or
not-plausible, but far enough to remove the touch
of the prosaic, the mechanical, which clings to
familiar objects or ideas. Except in the case of
the little girl, Wolinski does not do this. His
picture composes mechanically, but not artisti-
cally ; with the result that his achieved
impression seems rather the result of artifice
than of art. This may seem hypercriticism;
but (if the competition judges were similarly
impressed) it may explain why work inferior in
the totality of result was preferred above Wolin-
ski’s picture; It is not meant to detract from the
cleanness, the simplicity, the surety, which are
such fine qualities in the work, and which have
earned its place in a gallery of Australian art.
And, ag Wolinski goes to Paris with the proceeds
of his picture, one hopes that he will soon be able
to add the original flavour, the flavour of dis-
tinction, which are,all that is lacking to make his
picture a masterpiece.
* • . • •
The black-and-white section is not large or
very attractive. J. Mather’s etching and “fumo-
graph ” are noticeable: the latter is especi-
ally to be prized. There is a panel in Ameri-
can walnut by Louise Hunt. Remain some 200
paintings in oil or water, beyond (or below)
decided praise or blame. The one picture
which' should have eclipsed competition is
suggested in the third drawing on this page
(these three are chosen for publicity because
they occupy least space and least-misrepresent the
“ Sunshine after Rain.” — T. Dean.
paintings)— “ Sunshine after Rain,” by T. Dean.
Here is a man, a railway fettler, who nourishes the
young plant of Art in a. hard environment, and
with a little more science would culHhe best blos-
soms of them all. He paints landscapes which he
feels, and shows that he feels, and by dint qf feel-
ing his paint becomes light and atmosphere. The
effect of rainbow on mist, of sun on wet trees,
which he puts into his picture is artistically the
highest effect in the exhibition. The cattle
in the centre are not good, the nearer figures are
bad, and the foreground in which they stand is
clumsy and badly-painted, and spoils the picture.
If it were simply taken away tlie remainder would
stand, admirable. If it were improved, as con-
ceivably it could be improved, the picture would
rank very highly indeed 1 . The same artist’s
power over atmospheric effects is seen in the sky
of “ Out Back,” and the personality of this picture
also is noteworthy, though less marked. A “Sun-
set ” scene has less of Dean’s peculiar power.
_ Well,. ! did my best to lookat things in a dry
light, through spectacles of sympathy. And those
are the impressions the Art Society's 21st annual
exhibition made upon me.
A.G.S.
“ Henslowe’s Annual ” is issued by Gordon and
Gotch, at Sydney, in a dashing cover. The
literary and artistic contents are not of great
merit; but the publicatioTi seems well designed- to
secure popularity and a sale.
JR. H. Matthews forwards a pamphlet on
Divisions of the South Australian Aborigines,”
reprinted from a recent volume of proceedings of
the American Philosophical Society. It should
have been made clear that the compilation does
npt relate to the present day: the conditions of
tribal distribution and existence having greatly
changed since they were studied, for example,' by
Schiirmanii in the seventies and Curr in the
eighties.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Frog-farming is a coming industry of the
U.S., which territory is said to grow a finer na-
tural article in Bana esculenta than Canada;
though, owing to a superior method of prepara-
tion, Canadian “ frogs’-legs ” have hitherto been
most in demand. In San Francisco frogs’ legs are
largely consumed at the restaurants.
Yic. C.J. Madden recently delivered a
learned dissertation on the asininity and
absurdity of the law as it concerns divorce. The
alleged man in the case had frequently beaten his
wife, so severely on one occasion as to temporarily
deprive her of the sight of one eye. Also he
became a more or less habitual drunkard and
entirely neglected to maintain her. Nathless the
learned C.J. refused the decree nisi, ruling that,
“as he (the husband) had called at her mothers
house as late as Dec. last, he had, in his own miser-
able fashion, recognised her as his wife. Ihe
woman who had united her life to that of a thrift -
less and good-for-nothing fellow had married him,
and if he turned out to be by no means what her
fancy had painted, she could not hope to escape
on that ground. If he desired, even in the most
shiftless, half-hearted fashion, to live with his
wife, he could not be said to desert her. Also,
though Ins disposition was so brutal that she
might readily regard him with some mistrust, as
far as her future safety was_ concerned, the
violence alleged was not sufficient to bring it
within the legal definition of cruelty, ’’ Madden s
definition of desertion is tiuly amazing, A man
may wholly cease to support his wife and even
cease to live with her, but if he calls on her once
in eight months or so (possibly to threaten her
with further battery) it isn’t desertion within the
scope of Madden’s law !
The Exhumation.
“ I was practising at Cobar,”
Said the lawyer-man to me—
And I know that he was sober,
For we both were drinking tea—
“ I was practising at Cobar,
In the year of ’93.
“ Cobar girls have lips of coral;
. Cobar girls are charming sport;
But one cannot long on moral
Kisses hold the vital fort —
And the folk there seldom quarrel,
And they rarely go to Court.
“ How I yearned to make my pile and
Leave this place devoid of sin 1
Life upon a desert island
Would have more of joyful din—
My sole fountain in that dry land
Was the Copper-Miners’ Inn.
Black Bad Luck, with grim derision,
Came and sat upon my knees,
And dropped hints that an incision
In the neck would bring me ease,
When —a sweet celestial vision !
Came a squad of eight Chinese.
And they brought some Chinese toddy,
And they asked me what would be
Cost of leave to raise the body
Of their countryman, Ah Kee.
‘ Eight pound ten,’ I said —no shoddy
Pettifogger’s price for me.
Well, they planked the money down—and
Oh, ’twas good to see the gold !
Then I wrote straight off to Town, and
Got a permit to unmould
Dead Ah Kee for half-a-crown, and
Twenty-seven-and-six, all told.
Then I offered Michael Carty
Half-a-sovereign to exhume
That decayed old pagan party
From his long-neglected tomb;
And his answer it was hearty,
Though he had an eye of gloom.
Now, there was a dissipated
Corpse, by name of Fogarty,
That in ground unconsecrated
Had been buried privately ;
And they thought it would well-mated
With another heathen’s be.
So Ah Kee’s remains they planted
In that grave, without a wreath,
Never fearing to be “hanted,”
The white spectral moon beneath,
By a spook with eyes that slanted,
Or a ghost with hairy teeth.
That was long before, but Carty
Had a row with Fogarty ;
And he cherished still a hearty
Hate against the enemy;
So, instead of the Chow party,
Carty dug up Fogarty.
With a grin he said, confound him !
‘ Think of Fogarty the Chow
With the other Chows around him
Bumin’ joss-sticks ! Troth I vow
Though in life I often downed him
I have downed and damned him now.
Well, the thing could not be mended ;
So the coffin took the road,
And in war it nearly ended,
For each Chow would bear the load;
I was near with laughter rended
At the reverence they showed.
Did those cheated Chows divine a
Reason for the change absurd ?
Faith they never made a sign, a
Faintest sign, nor said a word,
But they shipped the corpse to China,
As I subsequently heard.
Sir, I sometimes can’t help sighing
As I think of poor Ah Kee,
And his bones near Cobar lying,
While in far-off Pe-che-li
All his folk are softly crying
At the shrine of Fogarty.”
N.S.W.
CREEVE ROE.


The Best
Literature.
“ THE TIMES ”
REPRINT
OF THE
ENCYCLOPEDIA
BRITANNICA.

In practice, the best literature is that from which the reader derives both pleasure
and profit, the joy of reading apt and sound English and the sense of enrichment that
comes from gaining knowledge. That the English should be apt, that the treatment be
fitted to the subject matter, is a requirement based upon the utilitarian foundation of all
literary principles, and the tediousness of hack writers arises from their obligation to
write indifferently well upon all subjects.
THE LATE ROBERT LOUIS STEVENSON
used to say that the most difficult of literary tasks was to describe a game of cards so
clearly that a party could sit down and play at it by book, and to write upon science and
philosophy for the unscientific and unphilosophical reader is a task beset by kindred diffi-
culties. A specialist may so master his subject that it ceases to present to his mind the
bristles of its technicality, but it is only the most profound students who can achieve this
happy result. The best treatises in every branch of learning must necessarily be written
not by “ popular expositors,” but by men who live so entirely in a world of their own that
they lose their sense of aloofness. In such an atmosphere
CLEAR THINKING IS AS HABITUAL
as is asceticism in a monastery. To Lord Kelvin, Lord Rayleigh, or Sir William Crookes,
for example, the physicist’s point of view must have become second nature. Heat and
cold, light and darkness are phenomena which, to such a scientist, present each a code of
action. He recognises the vibrations of light and sound in the air as plainly as a child
sees smoke; there is to him no vagueness and
MO NEED FOR VAGUE WORDS
he has a plain tale to tell and tells it plainly. To an astronomer like Sir Robert Ball the
paths of the stars and planets seem altogether simple, they move to and fro as perceptibly
as the steamers that ply on Sydney Harbor. To a child the mere thought that the world
spins on an axis is so bewildering that it accepts the statement as a savage accepts a
myth, and even to the* average adult the effort to conceive unbounded space results in
little else but headache and confusion. ~ Special training is necessary before one can even
receive a clear impression, and to impart that impression to others is an art in itself.
There are, indeed, in each field of study but a few specialists who can tell what they
know so that he who runs may read with understanding, and it is hardly too much to say
that these few are almost all to be found in the ranks of the Encyclopaedia Bfitannica’S
contributors.
The Ninth Edition of the ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNICA,
which “The Times ’ offers to residents of New South Wales, is
universally recognised as the most perfect of works of reference.
Completed nearly ten years ago, it at once assumed and has firmly
retained a position of paramount authority. “ The Times ” Reprint
is in every respect the same as the copies already sold by Messrs. A.
and C. Black, the Original Publishers, at the higher prices; it is
what booksellers call a new impression, not a changed edition. There
has been no condensation, no abridgement, no omission. Every
word, every illustration, every map, every element of value is
preserved intact, there is not the most minute cheapening of
the product to offset against the remarkable diminution of
the price.
But your order must be booked at once in order to secure the
work at the present favourable prices.
The greatest bargain in the world of bookselling; the great
national work for a preliminary payment of
ONE GUINEA.
To be followed by 16 Monthly Payments of One Guinea each.

A BARGAIN FOR BOOK-BUYERS.
TO DELAY IS DANGEROUS.
“ A LL good things must have an end,” says the adage. Had the sage who first propounded this
jr\ maxim been of a less pessimistic turn of mind he might have observed that evil things, as
well, are not always eternal; but, be that as it may, the adage that he made fits the case in hand
exactly. We refer to the London “Times” ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNICA ENTERPRISE.
For, first of all, it is an essentially good thing, since it has already placed the great international
standard library of reference in hundreds of homos in New South Wales—placed them at only half the
usual cost, and "yet permits the subscriber to pay this reduced price at the easy rate of only ONE
GUINEA PER MONTH. Then it is transitory, too, for on SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 15, the
opportunity it embodies will pass for ever beyond the reach of those who have failed to avail them-
selves of the benefits it offers.
We have but one word of advice for all our New South Wales friends, and that is : Seize the
opportunity while it is yet within your reach. Cut out the order form printed below, and post it; but
be quite sure it is posted on or before. SEPTEMBER 15. If there is any doubt about it, make sure of
your set by telegraphing us as follows:—“The London ‘Times,’ Sydney, Reserve set Britannica,”
giving your own name in full; then follow your telegram immediately by an order form properly filled
out, and a remittance of the first guinea.
REMEMBER that the withdrawal of the offer terminates the, arrangement which made this
extraordinary offer possible in New South Wales. ON SEPTEMBER 15 the offer must be withdrawn
permanently, and it will not be renewed at the prevailing prices or at any other price. It is a case,
therefore, of—NOW OR NOT AT ALL.

THE SPECIAL BOOK*CASE
The Offer—Good until Sept. 15th Only.
The sensation of the bookselling season in London, the remarkable offer of “THE TIMES” Re-
print of the ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNICA (Ninth Eedition) is already known to residents in
New South Wales who read “The Times.” No fewer than 15,000 copies of the massive work were
printed, but applications were received in rapid succession for more than 25,000.
Widespread disappointment was occasioned by the enforced refusal of a great number of orders,
and residents in New South Wales were, owing to delay in communicating with London, peculiarly
unfortunate in this respect. .
What “ The Times ” did for residents in England, it is now doing for residents in New South
Wales. Arrangements have been made for a limited number of copies for distribution in New South
Wales. The price and terms upon which these copies may be obtained are stated in detail below, and
immediate application should be made by persons who desire to avail themselves of this extraordinary
opportunity.
An Admirable Investment.
The ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNICA is an admirable investment for three excellent reasons.
The three excellent reasons which combine to recommend the “ Encyclopaedia ” are these _ ar-p. TA
In the first place, it is the very best thing of its sort to purchase. The ENCYCLOPAEDIA
BRITANNICA has been recognised, ever since the first edition was published in 1771, as the very
best work of reference in the world. It is easy enough to find more costly books, to purchase a thou-
sand volumes instead of only twenty-five, but that thousand would be ill chosen if the twenty-hve
volumes of the ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNICA were not among them. It is not, indeed, too
much to say that no thousand other volumes contain so great a proportion of the whole sum of human
knowledge as do these five-and-twenty. Here, then, is one excellent reason why the “ Encyclopaedia
is the very best thing of its sort. , ,
In the second place, the ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNICA may at the moment be purchased
upon remarkably advantageous terms. The established publisher’s price for the twenty-five volumes
in cloth binding is £37. “The Times” offers precisely the same work, unaltered and unabridged, tor
seventeen monthly payments of one guinea each, or for £l7 in cash —less than half-price. Ims is
another excellent reason, for everyone must wish that the pounds and shillings one spends should do
their utmost in adding to the accumulated wealth of the investor.
In the third place, the purchase of the ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNICA does not involve, on
the one hand, ready money, nor yet, on the other hand, the incurring of a vague debt which the debtor
may be called upon to pay when it is least convenient for him to do so.
A preliminary payment of only one guinea in cash will secure the delivery of the volumes. After
the delivery has been made other payments of one guinea each become due at monthly intervals. ** e
purchaser of the “Encyclopaedia” who adopts this novel and convenient system of paying for books
knows precisely when his payments should be made, and knows too that each one of them is so incon-
siderable as to make hardly any strain upon a month’s income.
The Popularity of the Work.
The popularity of the ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNICA is sufficiently shown by the fact
that in less than twenty months more than twenty thousand copies of “ The Times” Reprint of the
“Encyclopaedia” have been sold in the United Kingdom alone, in addition to copies supplied by
“ The Times ” in India, South Africa, and Australia, as well as to the United StAtes, where this com-
plete and authentic Reprint of the great library is eagerly welcomed notwithstanding that the country
has, owing to the faulty condition of the copyright laws, been flooded with three or four hundred
thousand copies of piratical and mutilated editions.
The aggregate sale of “The Times” Reprint of the “Encyclopaedia” has been, at i Lome ana
abroad, over twenty-five thousand sets of twenty-five volumes each, a total of six hundred and twenty-
five thousand volumes. The figures in each case are of interest, not only because they show that the
“Encyclopaedia” is a general favourite, but also because they describe an unprecedented success m
the dissemination of really good literature; and go far to -prove that the public taste is by no means
so debased as some alarmists believe it to be.
They are figures, too, so astonishing that they cannot readily be grasped.
Every copy of the “Encyclopaedia” contains twenty-two thousand pages, and this means that
over five hundred and fifty million pages have been printed and sold. Large figures of this sort pro-
duce upon the mind an effect which a contemporary foreign writer has aptly described as a
“brain-blur.” One cannot easily picture priuted pages floating through the air and lying like snow
upon the ground, but a simple comparison will assist the imagination. Each of these pages is 11
inches in depth by 8| inches in width. Their aggregate sqperfices would therefore be, if the front a,nd
back of each leaf could be split apart and all the pages spread out, no less than fifty-two thousand nine
hundred and thir.ty-seven millions five hundred thousand square inches, or 367,621,527 square feet,
or 8,349 acres, or nearly 14 square miles. Conceived as one gigantic sheet of printed words, having
its centre at a given point, the whole would extend nearly two miles north, nearly two miles south,
nearly two miles east, and nearly two miles west. On this enormous sheet there would appear no
less than 750,000,000,000 words, for there are more than 30,000,000 words in each copy of the
“Encyclopaedia.” Using another familiar illustration, 550,000,000 pages each 11 inches in depth
would, laid end to end, make a ribbon more than 95,486 miles in length, reaching nearly four times
round the earth.
Each copy of the ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNICA, packed in a box for delivery, weighs
about one hundred and ninety-six pounds. The whole would therefore weigh over two thousand tons,
as much as can be carried by a dozen ordinary goods-trains.
No other book devised by human ingenuity has ever had a sale which can be compared to this, and
after the recapitulation of these figures the acceptability of the ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNICA
as a timely investment can hardly be questioned.
The Forthcoming “ Times ” Supplement.
A Supplement to the Ninth Edition of the ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNICA is now in course
of preparation, and will shortly be published by “The Times.” When completed it will be supplied
to the purchasers of “ The Times ” Reprint at a lower price than that at which it will be obtainable by
the public at la rye.
It is impossible to give a work so large and so comprehensive as the ENCYCLOPAEDIA all the
minor advantages possessed by an annual, such as “ Whitaker’s Almanac ”or the “ Statesman’s Year
Book.” The scholarly care with which the ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNICA was prepared
serves to make the progress of its issue so slow that its volumes must inevitably cease to be “ up-to-
date ” before they leave the printer’s hands. The value of such a work is quite unaffected by the
changes of the moment. It has none of the perishability of a directory Or a year book, because its
editors were wise enough not to attempt to give it the advantages and disadvantages of an annual.
Information that is truly of an encyclopaedic nature does not indeed accumulate in any great
degree in a year, or even in a score of years ; it is only in the case of a few of the more rapidly-advanc-
ing sciences that more could be said than is now contained in the treatises of the ENCYCLOPAEDIA
BRITANNICA. The subjects which have been seriously affected by the progress of the past few
years form so small a proportion of the contents of the ENCYCLOP.ASDIA BRITANNICA that the
great bulk of the work will probably remain the same even when the tenth edition is issued to the
next generation.
No steps will be taken towards preparing the tenth edition for many years to come, and when a
new edition is undertaken it will require at least twelve or fifteen years to complete it. The present
edition (the ninth), with the Supplement now in preparation, will therefore be the national standard
work of reference for at least twenty years to come.
' The editdr-in-chief of the Supplement is Sir Donald Mackenzie Wallace, in collaboration with
Prof. A. T. Hadley, President of Yale University, U.S.A., and it will contain new articles dealing
with the various inventions, discoveries, &c., which have been made since the ninth edition was com-
pleted. The Supplement will also contain biographies of distinguished men and women who have died
since then. The various treatises of the ninth edition and all the scientific and historical information
which these treatises present will be brought up to date so fully that the edition of the Supplement
will, to all intents and purposes, make all the volumes of the 9th edition as recent as if they had been
prepared this year.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 8, 1900.
Price 6d.
Some brilliant genius sug-
gests that the police go up in
a balloon and search for the
Breelong blacks with a spy-glass.
Another equally brilliant suggestion is that the N.S.W.
Govt. Analyst be instructed to hocus a lot of whisky to be
left promiscuously about the bush for the Governors
to get hold of and thus facilitate their capture.
Whoever suggested this last is no friend of the police.
“At any rate, Sullivan,”
says Sergeant Dooley, “ they
can’t lave no insoolting mes-
sages fur us oop here.”
No doubt a policeman’s boot dropped
from a balloon at a high altitude upon a
Breelong black’s head would
make him sit up.
Jimmy Governor . “For two pints I’d'
go to Sydney, and become the
Governor of Australia.”
NS.W. Minister for Works O’Sullivan recently paid a Ministerial'visit to the Breelong Black country, armed to the teeth and
under strong, police escort. The above is what Ministerial journeys up country are coming to.
TOUCHING THE BREELONG BLACKS.

Special Notice
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence" column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that toe
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient,
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 8. 1900.
The Federal Land and Income-Tax.
The Parliament shall, subject to this Constitution, have
power to make laws for the peace, order, and good
government of the Commonwealth with respect to :
11. Taxation, but so as not to discriminate between
States or parts of State.— Commonwealth Constitution,
clause 51.
During a period of ten years after the establishment of
the Commonwealth, and thereafter till the Parliament
otherwise provides, of the net revenue of the Common-
wealth from duties of customs and excise, not more than
one-fourth shall be applied annually by the Common-
wealth towards its expenditure.
The balance shall, in accordance with this Constitution,
be paid to the several States, or applied towards the pay-
ment of interest on debts of the several States taken over
by the Commonwealth. — Commonwealth Constitution,
clause 87.
Chiefly owing to the hard lying practised
by the anti-Federal press, much misconcep-
tion exists as to the financial situation
created by the Commonwealth Bill. Despite
a large amount of misinformed public opinion
to the contrary, that document does not
commit Australia to either Protection,
Revenue-Tariffism, or Freetrade ; it leaves
the whole question as open as it is now. It
does not commit the Federal Government,
as so frequently alleged, to “ raise four
times as much revenue as it requires.” It
does not commit the Federal Government
to raise any customs and excise revenue at
all; if the Government does raise any
customs and excise revenue it has to refund
three-fourths of the sum to the States, but
the Constitution leaves the Commonwealth
absolutely at liberty to pay its way by
direct taxation, and to wholly and entirely
abolish the tariff if it feels so inclined. The
only reasons' why it will impose a tariff that
will afford Protection and raise a large re-
venue at the same time lie in its own common
sense and in the necessities which the States,
by their great debt-accumulations, have
created. The Commonwealth Bill did not
create either of these reasons.
* * * •
Further, the Commonwealth Govern-
ment is not bound to impose only such
taxes as it requires for its own
use. It may impose others that it doesn’t
require, and hand over the proceeds to the
States. Though it is only pledged to hand
over to the States not less than three-fourths
of the tariff-revenue, this is merely a mini-
mum ; it can also hand over anything else
it pleases. And herein lies one of the Com-
monwealth’s grandest opportunities to pro-
mote prosperity, discourage land-monopoly,
break up absenteeism, provide land for
closer settlement, and otherwise do a great
and noble work in Australia. For the Com-
monwealth can impose heavily-graduated
land-and-income taxes and absentee taxes
throughout its territory, quite irrespective of
its own financial needs. It can do this sim-
ply because Australia requires these things
ior the discouragement of the great and in-
solent oligarchy of land-grabbers, and to
suppress the absentee, who make this coun-
try a slave and tributary to foreign States.
In one or two States the oligarchy
of land-stealers is so impregnably entrenched
in a property-vote Upper House that the
people can’t carry out these urgently-needed
reforms through their State Parliaments.
They are suppressed and over-ruled by aprivi-
leged and immovable handful. Therefore
the natural and obvious way out is for the
people to seek the line of least resistance,
and effect their purpose through the Federal
Parliament, where no privileged handful will
be allowed to block the road.
• * * *
The Federal land and income and absentee
taxes would not be additional upon any such
taxes levied by the States, therefore any cry
about double-banking the burdens of the
people would be wholly irrelevant. It is not
proposed that the Federal Parliament should
retain the money for its own use, but
that the entire net proceeds should be
handed back to the States, or that
the Federal Government should balance
things by taking over State liabilities
and responsibilities sufficient to equal the
amount derived from the land, income and
absentee duties. Therefore those States
which have such taxes now could be relied
upon to abolish them with all possible speed.
South Australia is the only Australian pro-
vince which has a scientific system of direct
taxation, and its system, with a few improve-
ments, is the one which should be followed.
N.S.W. and Tasmania have a miserably
unscientific and undemocratic land - tax,
which falls about as heavily on the small
owner as the large one, and which does not
discourage the accumulation of large estates,
or facilitate closer settlement. Victoria has
a still worse system, and the privileged
House of 48 Rich Landlords blocks, the way
like a vast Boer entrenchment, and makes
improvement impossible. Westralia and
Queensland have no land-tax. Neither
have they an income-tax ; the great land-
lords and great financiers run these States
with a regard for their own interests which
is deeply touching. S.A., Tasmania, and
Victoria have a reasonably scientific income-
tax system which varies according to the
amount of the income, and according to
whether it is a precarious revenue derived
from personal exertion and liable to stop at
any moment, or a reasonably permanent one
derived from investments. And N.S.W.
has a hopelessly unscientific one which falls
on the income derived from personal exertion
and the income from property alike. S.A.
is the only Australian State which has an
absentee-tax, and its absentee-tax is much
too small for the purpose it is intended to
serve.
* * * *
The purposes of the land and income tax
should be to raise revenue, to raise it where
it can be best afforded, to encourage the
settlement of land by small landholders,
to discourage the formation of large
estates by taxing them so heavily
that a £200,000 estate in one lot will
be worth only half or thereabouts of
what the same land would be worth if cut
up into holdings small enough to be below
the taxation limit, and to make the exist-
ence of the absentee landlord wholly unpro-
fitable. The absentee, by taking his rents
out of the country, is a constant drain on
the national resources. The owner of
£1,000,000 worth of property can, at any
moment, by permanently leaving the
country, convert that asset into £1,000,000
of debt owing by Australia to a foreign
State. The fact of Australia having to send
abroad the rents on £1,000,000 of property
places it in exactly the same position as if it
had borrowed £1,000,000 at 6 per cent, or so,
and had to send the interest abroad. Therefore
it is most essential that the absentee should
be specially included in every scheme of
direct taxation. If there is enough of him
he is about the shortest possible cut to ruin.
But there is little hope that the slow-
going and undemocratic State legislators
of New South Wales will recognise
these facts. There is less hope as
regards the Boodle Government of Queens-
land, and the Forrest gang of Westralia,
both of which are abject slaves to the fetish
of foreign capital. There is quite as little
hope with the land-shark Legislative Coun-
cils of Victoria and Tasmania. There is no
reason, however, why the Democracy should
grieve over these matters any longer.
Nothing can be gained by spending years
and years in weary effort to bring the local
Parliaments of Property to a sense of their
backwardness and incompetence, when the
Progressive party, by throwing its weight
into the truly democratic Commonwealth
Legislature, can accomplish all that is neces-
sary without the least difficulty. If it likes
to do this the local property-vote Upper
Houses and the local Boodle Ministries will
be of as little account in stopping the way as
the dead leaves of last year. And even if
the State Houses of Property were willing
to reform, of which they show no signs
whatever, it is better that the work should
be done by the Commonwealth Parliament,
if only for the sake of uniformity.
So The Bulletin proposes that the
Federal Parliament should, at the earliest
possible date, impose taxes on these lines :
All incomes not exceeding £240 a year to be
exempt. From that point up to £6OO the
tax to be 4|d. in the £ on incomes derived
from personal exertion, and 9d. in the £ on
incomes derived from investments. From
£6OO to £2OOO the tax to be 6d. in the £ on
incomes derived from personal exertion, and
Is. on incomes derived from property.
Above £2OOO the rates to be 9d. and Is. 6d.
With regard to land tax, every holder of not
more than £5OO worth of land (unimproved
value) to be exempt. From £5OO to £lO,OOO
the rate to be Id. in the £ : from £lO,OOO
to £20,000, ljd. in the £; and an addi-
tional Jd. in the £ for every additional
£lO,OOO up to 6d. in the £. Also that
every absentee—thereby meaning a person
or corporation that has been more
than two years -continuously out of the
territory of the Commonwealth, or that
resides out of it for more than half the
time, averaged on a term of four years or
more, even though there may not be any two
years’ continuous absence, shall pay fifty per
cent, extra both in land and income tax—
holders of State or Federal Government
securities that were originally issued abroad
being alone excepted.
* * * *
This is placing the absentee-duty higher
than either Maoriland or S.A. have done,
the tax in both cases being too small to be
efficient. On the other hand, it is bearing
more lightly on the small landholder than
any of the land-tax systems now existing in
Australia. It is specially devised to give
every possible assistance to the small farmer ;
to make land worth more and more per acre
as it is subdivided till it gets down to
the exemption limit, and less and less per
acre as it accumulates in huge estates.
At the same time, it is more merciful to the
large landholder who is willing to cut up and
sell his huge holdings than any land-tax now
existing in Australia. It gives an extra
value to holdings worth £5OO or thereabouts
by relieving them of all taxation. Under
the proposed new taxation policy a single
estate in S.A. or N.S.W., now worth
£lOO,OOO or so, would be diminished in
value by reason of the extra tax on large
properties. At the same time, if the owner
subdivided that property into 200 small
farms worth £5OO each, and sold them
singly, he would get more for his property
than he would get rfnder the existing law of
either S.A. or N.S. W. Small farms would
be more valuable by reason of the extra
exemption allowed; under the proposed
Federal taxation system a farm worth
£5OO would be free of tax, whereas it
now pays £1 Is. Bd. in N.S.W. and
about the same in S.A. Therefore the pro-
posed extra exemption would add £2O a
year to the selling value. By making the
large estate less profitable and the small one
more profitable than under any existing Aus-
tralian land-tax system the establishment of
a class of small independent proprietors
would be greatly accelerated, and the growth
of the obnoxious classes of “ squires ” and
“ tenantry ” would be stopped short.
* * * *
Also the plan outlined is specially designed
to reduce the exhausting tribute which this
unfortunate country pays to foreign lands
more particularly the interest, rents and
dividends exported to maintain people who
are away hunting for titles and decorations
in London and elsewhere. It is devised
with the intention of steadily diminishing
the hold which the innumerable foreign
mortgagee has on Australia. And incident-
ally the policy here suggested would supply
S.A., Tasmania and N.S.W. with as much
revenue as they have now, and the other pro-
vinces with more. It would, to a great extent,
fill the gap created by the abolition of
Border duties. It would turn the en-
trenched position of the Victorian House of
48 Rich Landlords, and the Tasmanian
House of Proprietors, and the Boodle Gov-
ernment of Queensland. It would transfer
the fight on the most essential of all demo-
cratic questions from the Parliaments where
class privilege is strong and the people are
weak, to the Legislature where class privilege
is nil and the people are all-powerful. So
far it doesn’t appear that the Australian
Democracy has seriously realised the splendid
chances that lie before it, if it only uses
wisely the powers conferred on it by the
Federal Constitution. That Constitution is
the greatest opportunity ever offered to this
country, and one of the greatest ever offered
to any country at any time in history.
The A.M.F. Extension Joke.
After a weary and lonesome fashion the S.M.
Herald and the Sydney Telegraph keep pound-
ing at each other with blunt weapons re the pro-
posal to give the A.M.P. directors power to extend
the Society’s business all over the British empire.
The Herald struggles along under a great weight
of statistics to prove that there is no chance of
getting much new business in England, and there-
fore it isn’t worth while to extend. Tlfe Tele-
graph labours heavily over the theory that there
is plenty of room for the A.M.P. to do a very
large new business in England, and therefore
maintains that it is worth while extending the
A.M.P.’s business, and heaves things like this at
the public’s head :
But the argument put forward in that way yesterday
was designed to prove the A.M.P. Society, if it went to
London, would only secure about one-eighty-eighth part
of 24,753 policies, and as it can get 15,00 n policies a year
in Australasia, the extension was useless from a business
point of view. Why, then, make all this fuss about it?
To some, with less of a bent for declaring business in the
old country as used-up and inferior, it may seem odd that,
if the'A.M.P. can secure so many new policies out of the
4,000,000 people in Australasia, its expectation should be
only about 280 policies a year out of a population of
40,000,000. The Mutual Life of New York managed last
year to pick up 3959 policies, assuring £1,843,11 9, in that
much-used-up country, and, in spite of their vast inferi-
ority, went on piling up its funds in a remarkable fashion.
What are the managers of that office thinking about to
persist in such a suicidal policy as to take these not only
worthless, but, as we are assured, positively harmful,
British lives in its keeping? There would be no difficulty
in picking out any number of offices doing what they con-
sider a thriving business in the United Kingdom, but is it
worth the trouble ? When the A.M.P. is also securing its
4000 policies a year in the United Kingdom, we may re-
turn to the subject.
Why these two papers should hammer away
thus wretchedly and irrelevantly re the size of the
probable business to be done in England passes
comprehension. The mere size is a question of
no interest whatever, except to the staff which, if
a very large new business opens in a new country,
will have many new and improved billets in
prospect. But to the Australian members of the
A.M.P. it doesn’t amount to a single straw
even if the A.M.P. can get £40,000,000 of
new business in London. The question is—
how will that new business pay ? If the
English business is quite as cheap as the
Australian business, and the death-rate is quite as
low, and the interest earned on investments is
quite as high, then the new English business,
even if it amounts to £40,000,000 a year, won’t do
one penny of good to the Australian policy-holder.
It will leave him exactly where he is. tlis only
satisfaction will be in knowing that the English
policy-holder is getting as large bonuses as he is
getting himself. But if the new English business
is • more expensive to get, or the death-rate is
higher, or the interest realised on investments is
lower, then it will be a dead loss to the Australian
policy-holder, and the larger the English business
the greater the loss. The English business may
be a splendidly profitable one, but if it isn’t quite
as profitable as the Australian one then it is still a
dead loss to the Australian policy-holder. A
business that yields 20 per cent, on capital is
doubtless a splendid one, but if it coalesces with
another business, having exactly the same capital,
and yielding 30 per cent., the chances are that the
average profits are 25 per cent. And the second
business, by pooling with a most sound and profit-
able concern, only loses money. That is the pro-
bable situation as between the Australian policy-
holders of the A.M.P. and the prospective English
ones. For there is no reasonable prospect that
business will be cheaper to get in England ; no
English office doing the same proportion of new
business as the A.M.P. does, works any more
cheaply. And it is dead certain that the
mortality in England will be higher; the
average Australian death-rate is 124 annually
out of ’every 10,000; in England it is
177. And the expectation of life for an English-
man aged 30 is fully two years less than for an
Australian aged 30 —in other words he pays two
less premiums before collecting the amount
of his policy. As for the rate of interest
which can be earned on money invested in England,
that is notoriously less than the Australian rate,
and no ordinary life office in England shows any-
thing like the A.M.P.’s interest rate. Therefore
The Bulletin maintains that the extension of
the A.M.P.’s business to England will raise the
average death-rate (which means less bonuses all
round), and reduce the average interest rate
(which again means less bonuses all round). And
the more business is obtained in England, where
death-rate is higher and interest is lower, the
greater will be the loss in bonuses to Australian
policy-holders.
* * * *
The Mutual Life of New York’s extension to
England, which is so vigorously quoted by the
Telegraph as a reason in favour of the A.M.P.’s
extension to England, is as irrelevant as the rest
of that paper’s reasoning. When the Mutual of
New York extended from the U.S. to England it
extended to an equally healthy country, so far as
the imperfect U.S. statistics make it possible to
judge—therefore, its action might be wise enough.
If the A.M.P. extends from Australia to England it
extends to a less healthy country—which is un-
wise. A man with 15s. a week is advantaged by
having his wage changed to 205., but it doesn’t
follow that a man with 255. a week is advantaged
by having his wage changed to 20s. The Tele-
graph holds that he is, but that is merely its
want of reasoning faculty.
* * * *
Moreover, there is another aspect of the case
quite apart from the English aspect. The direc-
tors ask for power to extend the business all over
the British Empire—power to fool away the profits
m the fever-haunted swamps of the West Coast of
Africa, in plague-stricken India, in the unwhole-
some wilds of British Guiana, and all the other
lazar-houses of Victoria’s dominions. If that
power is given them it can’t be taken away again
except by a two-thirds vote of the policy-holders ;
and before the policy-holders were sufficiently
aroused to the nature of the situation to secure
that two-thirds vote, the A.M.P. might easily have
struck the biggest rock in its history. The
directors will probably say that they don’t
intend to_ use this power of extension all over
the empire. Perhaps they don’t, but, if
so, why are they so desperately anxious to get it ?
People don’t, as a rule, strive so hard after a
tmng which they are not going to use, and whicli
they don’t want, and which will be of no kind of
service to them. And even if they don’t use to
the full the amazing power that they ask for, to
‘blew” the A.M.P.’s funds in the swamps of
Africa, what guarantee is there that no future
body of directors will so use it ? Why should any
body of policy-holders deliberately give any board
the power to wreck their sources of profit for the
benefit of outsiders ? The man who gives another
a blank cheque, merely on the other’s assurance
that he won’t use it, and who never thinks of
asking why he is so anxious to have it if he does
not mean to use it, is many kinds and varieties of
a fool.
The Assessment Insurance Eelusion.
Ijie Bulletin .has drawn its own attention to a
lengthy advt. in the Review wherein the manager
of the Independent Order of Foresters (a Canadian
assessment life assurance institution which re-
cently arrived in these parts) replies to this
papers criticisms on his institution. He states
that he was driven to this course because The
Bulletin refused to insert his lengthy
letter in its entirety, which is quite true.
Ihe Bulletin offered him for his reply a larger
space than was occupied by its attack, and all the
real subject-matter of his discourse could easily
have gone into the space offered, but the rhetorical


frills wouldn’t. The disagreement arose over the
frills.
* * * •
And, after all, the answer hardly touches at all
on the points raised by this paper—the points
which made it grievously doubt the solvency of
the 1.0. F. and urgently call for legislation to
establish a reliable standard of solvency among
life assurance offices doing business in this mis-
managed, neglected, happy-go-lucky land. The.
1.0. F. publishes tables in which it sets forth that
its allegedly very low premiums (the alleged re-
markable lowness of its premiums is the means,
above all others, by which it attracts business) are
quite sufficient, provided not more than 5 per
cent, of the premiums are absorbed in expenses.
The 1.0. F. has the gall to declare that it doesn’t
spend over 5 per cent, of its premiums in expenses.
And, in his reply to The Bulletin, the manager
says distinctly :
The 1.0. F., too, makes a deliberate inclusion of ex-
penses in the premiums, but the amount so taken is
ABSOLUTELY LIMITED TO FIVE PER CENT.
And then this same 1.0. F. deliberately publishes,
in Sydney, its revenue and expenditure account
for the fiye years 1893-’94-’95-’96 and ’97. And
some items in the five years’ accounts read thus: —
Receipts. Expenditure.
Premiums .. £1,039,792 Commission .. £1402
Expenses of Man-
agement .. 96,275
Legislation .. 1406
“Expenses of management” alone run to over
nine per cent, of premiums, and if the other items,
which are also expenses, are included, they run to
nearly 10 per cent. What is the good of saying
that the expenses are limited to 5 per cent, and
publishing tables to prove that everything is well,
provided the expenses are kept down to 5 per
cent., while documents like these are on record '!
* « * •
Again the 1.0. F. issues tables of figures whose
purport is that the allegedly very low premiums
which serve as its great advertisement and stock
argument are quite sufficient, provided these pre-
miums are invested so as to bring in 4 per cent,
interest—that if they are invested to bring in 4 per
cent, interest regularly they will accumulate to a
sufficient sum to pay its policies when the said
policies fall due. And these, according to its own
published returns, are the interest rates actually
earned in its life department on the average funds
for each year: —
Interest on Average Funds of Each Year.
1893 .. £1 16 6 per cent. 1896 .. £2 2 4 per cent.
1894 ..3 3 4 „ 1897 .. 4 12 2
1895 .. 3 18 4 „ 1898 ..4 2 8
Therefore it is certain that the 1.0. F. doesn’t
always average the 4 per cent interest which it
assumes to earn, and which, by its own figures, it
needs to earn. And even if it did always earn the
full 4 per cent, that .would leave no margin of
safety to cover possible losses on investments
(even the 1.0. F. is not free from that risk), or for
a future drop in the rate of interest.
* * • *
But the most crushing thing of all is a certain
actuarial report issued by the 1.0. F. itself. This
sets forth that, at the end of 1897, the present lia-
bilities were £7,159,342. That is, assuming that
every policy-holder then in existence kept on
paying his premiums the same premiums
which are quoted—until his policy became a
claim, the amount then on hand should have been
£7,159,342. And, instead of this, the sum it then
had on hand was £518,724, so on this basis there
was a fearful and wonderful shortage of £6,640,018.
Assuming that it was called upon to pay its
policy-holders what it had pledged itself to pay
them, the 1.0. F. was apparently then good for
about one shilling and sixpence in the £. For
every £ it should have to be a solvent, substantial
office, on the same basis as the best offices doing
business in Australia, it had eighteenpence.
* * * *
To make good the difference, it apparently
trusted : (1) To Providence; (2) to the power
which is reserved by the management of increas-
ing the premiums—that is to say, the 1.0. F.
attracts business by announcing very low pre-
miums, but there is always ahead the pos-
sibility that after the policy - holder has
joined the 1.0. F. because the rates are
lower than those of the A.M.P. or National
Mutual or Maoriland State Department, and has
been in a few years, he may find himself confronted
with the alternative of paying far higher rates
than these offices charge, or else losing his insur-
ance and all the premiums he has paid. And (3)
the 1.0. F. apparently depends largely to keep
itself square in the future on the fact that many
policy-holders drop out and as it pays no surrender
values all these are clear profit. But though it has
been many years in the business, and members
have been dropping out all the time, all these
droppings out still leave a deficit of 18s. 6d. in the
£ according to ordinary insurance ideas. Is there
any evidence that they are going to drop out
faster in the future ? Further, if the insurers
really believe that the 1.0. F. is quite able to,
and quite certain to, give them so many
advantages as it alleges at so low a
rate, they will do their best not to drop
out. Further, the fact that the insurer,
by dropping out, loses every penny of the money he
has put in is another strong inducement for him
to stay in with far more pertinacity than he does
in offices which give surrender values. And yet,
if they stay in too much it looks as if it might be
bad for the office, and bad for the people who
stay in.
* * * *
The Independent Order of Foresters, according
to its own actuary’s report, conducts business on
these lines:
Every certificate and policy issued by the society shall
contain a promise to pay the whole amount therein men-
tioned, out of the mortuary funds of the society, and out
of any moneys realised from assessments to be made for
that purpose, &c.
This looks all right, but what the insurer needs to
remember is that assessments are simply calls to
be made on him and others like him. When he
gets his £IOO policy, he really gets it with this
condition :
We don’t undertake to pay this policy out of the pre-
miums charged, but only out of these and any further
premiums (amount unspecified) which we may find it
necessary to charge you later on. If you don’t pay these
extra premiums (which may raise the cost of your policy
to far more than the A.M.P. would have charged you)
you can drop out and lose your insurance, and lose all
the premiums you have already paid. If you do pay
them that doesn’t necessarily make your policy safe ; its
safety also depends to some extent on how the other mem-
bers pay up.
This isn’t a very satisfactory-looking situation,
but it is about how things stand.
In some of its printed literature the 1.0. F.,
drawing a parallel between itself and the A.M.P.,
claims that it, like the A M.P., gives bonuses to
members. This comes as near to flat misrepresen-
tation as anything well can without quite getting
there. The principle of giving bonuses to mem-
bers is recognised, provided their policies have
been seven years in force, and provided a
;wo-thirds vote of the Supreme Court is cast
’or giving bonuses, and provided the bonuses don’t
•educe the funds below £5 ss. for each member —
there is something very beautiful about the system
which empowers the management to distribute as
profits everything above this amount, and to keep
m hand a reserve of only £5 ss. per member, when
it agrees in its policies to pay each member sums
varying up to, say, £IOOO. But the principle is of
no special value without the cash, and the 1.U.-t.
has never yet paid any bonus, and there is no
proof that it ever will. In fact the actuary says :
“ The policies of the Order are non-participat-
ing in all cases.” Therefore, when it classes
itself as a bonus-paying institution alongside the
A.M.P. it is getting pretty close to straight-out
misrepresentation. And when it speaks of its
total fund as its surplus (as if it was a sur-
plus over and above liabilities instead of being not
nearly enough, on ordinary assurance principles,
to cover liabilities) it again gets pretty near to
plain misrepresentation.
♦ * * *
The much-advertised fact that the 1.0. F. has
hitherto met all claims with ease counts for
absolutely nothing. The A.M.P. last December
had £16,i00,000 of assets, which covered its then
liabilities and left about £600,000 for bonuses and
reserves. But the A.M.P. with its £15,500,000 of
liabilities, could also have met every claim with
ease even though, instead of having 16 millions,
it hadn’t had a penny accumulated. It would in
that case, of course, have been utterly insolvent and
good for almost nothing in the £, and the collapse
would only have been a question of time, but the
new premiums coming in would have more than
covered all claims and expenses. A reconstructed
bank with one million assets and ten millions of
liabilities to depositors might also meet all cur-
rent claims for 20 years if the deposits were
fixed for 21 years. The mere fact of having been
able to meet current claims proves nothing. It
certainly doesn’t prove that the 1.0. F. can keep
solvent on present premium rates, or that it won t
be necessary to make heavy calls on policy-holders
some time in the future. And when a policy-
holder goes in because rates are low, and yet with
the knowledge that a great part of the security
for the payment of his own policy consists m the
institution’s reserved power to charge him very
high rates hereafter, he is a very foolish policy-
holder.
* * * *
Moreover the 1.0.F.’s wonderfully cheap pre-
miums are not all a member has to pay ; he is also
liable for “court dues” which make a serious
difference, and for entrance fees. Then the 1.0. F.
in all its comparisons compares the with-bonus
rates of the Australian offices and its own without-
bonus rates —it has never paid a bonus and there is
no visible prospect of one. Comparing the 1.0. F. s
rates with “court dues” added, and_ the without-
profit rates of two Australian offices (which is
putting them all on the same basis) the quarterly
premiums on £IOO at various ages are :
Age 1.0. F.
Last including
Birthday. Court dues. A.M.P. City Mutual.
20 ' 8s ljd 7s 6d 7s 6d
30 10s 4Jd 9s lid 9s lid
40 13s 7Ad 13s lOd 13s ltd
50 21s 4Jd 20s 8d -20 s 8d
Out of the 1.0.F.’s Bs. l|d. at age 20 “court dues”
and expenses absorb about 3s. 6Jd. so there is
about 4s. 7d. left to accumulate in order to pay the
assurance at maturity. In the A.M.P.’s case
expenses absorb about Is. Id. out of 7s. 6d., leav-
ing 6s. sd. to accumulate towards meeting the
policy at maturity. Therefore the chance of the
policy being paid at maturity is vastly better.
That is the situation as regards the small insurer,
on whom “court dues” fall heavily. Things
don’t pan out so badly for the large one. Then
on the one hand the 1.0. F. promises benefits in
case of total permanent disability, and relief from
all premiums at age 70, and certain old age benefits,
which the A.M.P. doesn’t. On the other hand
the A.M.P. gives surrender values, which the
1.0. F. doesn’t. It charges no entrance fee, which
the 1.0. F. does. Its policies carry no risk of a
huge increase of premium hereafter as the 1.0.F.’s
do. And its security for payment of the policy is
immeasurably better, for it has sufficient funds to
pay everybody in full without increasing its rates
even if all its insurers hang on till death, while
under these same conditions the 1.0. F., at the
time of its last valuation, was apparently good for
about Is. 6d. in the £. And all the other leading
Australian offices are in the same situation as the
A.M.P.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The North Queensland Syndicate
Railway Job.
If North Queensland is successful in getting its
great syndicate railway, 435 miles long, from the
Norman to Cloncurry, it is reasonably safe to
assume that North Queensland will be, for many
years, the sorriest place in all Australia. It is
especially and carefully stipulated, as one of the
conditions of the work, that the syndicate is to
have power, for 50 years, to charge one-and-a-lialf
times the rates now charged on the Queensland
State lines. The rates charged on the North
Queensland lines are already high, and one-
and-a-half times these rates will be an appalling
burden. The North Queensland lines about pay
their way on present rates—the great loss is on
the Southern system—so if the syndicate is as
economical in its railway building as the State has
been its profits will be enormous. As population
increases in the North, and business improves, the
State’s railway rates will doubtless be lowered,
and in course of time the syndicate railway will
probably be charging three times the State tariff,
for there is no provision for lowering its charges.
Then there will arise a demand that the State will
buy up the dreadful incubus and reduce the rates.
And the syndicate will demand to be compen-
sated for the sacrifice of the enormous profits
which its charter allows it to exact for half-a-cen-
tury. And if it is so compensated the price of
the line to the State will be so vast that it will be
impossible to reduce the rates to the level of the
other State lines without making a very heavy loss.
And yet if the State doesn’t resume the Cloncurry
private line the district will remain for 50 years
subject to exactions which will be little short
of ruinous. It is a disastrous policy for any
district, merely for the sake of getting its line a
year or two sooner, to sell itself into commercial
bondage for 50 years, or even half that time. It
is especially ruinous just now, for, even if the
Queensland Government can t build the railway
as alleged, it will be possible in a very few
months to appeal to the Commonwealth to do the
work, and, if the appeal is successful, North
Queensland would probably get its railway years
sooner in that way than by the lingering opera-
tions of a syndicate. At all events, until that
rational expedient is tried and has failed, it is
utter madness to resort to the suicidal one of
giving private speculators the power to oppress
and loot the country for half a century. And, in
any case, the plea that the Queensland Govern-
ment can’t find the money is utterly untrue.
Queensland could find the money for railway con-
struction in the first place by imposing a
land and income tax, neither of which exist
at present in that State, and could set
its railway-extension business going again out
of the proceeds. If it hasn’t the grit and sense to
adopt this expedient it could borrow the money.
It is only a question of paying enough interest.
It could probably raise enough to build the Clon-
curry line six times over if it offered 4 per cent.
And if it charged one and a-quarter times the
usual fares and freights, that would far more than
cover the extra interest. The existing North
Queensland lines pay their way reasonably well,
yet they have to meet the interest on money
borrowed at the average rate of 3| per cent. If
the Cloncurry line started with a 4 per cen t.
interest burden that would be only one-fifteenth
more, and if the rates were put up to one-quarter
more that should cover the difference several
times over, and yet that arrangement would
supply the district with vastly cheaper means
of communication than the syndicate offers.
Borrowing is unspeakably bad, but syndica-
lism is a great deal worse. And the wildest
humor of the situation is this: The other
North Queensland railways were built with
money borrowed at over £3 15s. per cent.,
and they practically pay their way on
present rates. The result of the last loan
shows that Queensland can borrow even
now at about £3 10s. per cent., or £3 12s. 6d. at the
outside, at which rate it should be possible to
build the Cloncurry line more cheaply than the
other Northern railways, and make a profit out of
it on the existing scale of freights. Queensland s
last big loan, disappointing as it might be, was
actually the cheapest big loan Queensland, has
ever issued. And yet because money can t be
obtained more cheaply still, the Philp Govern-
ment claims that State railway construction is
impossible, and that it is necessary to let the woik
out, on the most atrociously usurious terms, to
private grabsters. Where is the necessity ?

Industrial Arbitration.
One result of the visit of M’s.L.A. Trenwith and
Best to M.L. is a Bill now before the Victorian
Lower House for the abolition of strikes and lock-
outs, and for the better protection and organiza-
tion of employers and employes. It is entitled
an Industrial Arbitration and Conciliation .Bill,
and proposes, much on the lines of the Maoriland
measure, to enact that a registrar is to be
appointed to register companies or associations of
employers, and trade-unions or branches of unions.
Subscriptions are made recoverable and penalties
imposed for non-payment of same in due time.
Agreements may be made between unions and
associations or employers, which shall have the
effect of an award of the Court of Arbitration.
The Court is to consist of two paid arbitrators,
nominated by. employers and employes, presided
over by a Supreme Court Judge ; and is to have
power to hear and determine any industrial
dispute, to dismiss such as trivial, to dismiss with
a view to amicable settlement, to award costs and
expenses (with the special exception of costs for
counsel, solicitor or agent,) to summon and deal
with witnesses, to grant injunctions and pro-
hibitions and issue writs of mandamus, and
generally to exercise the powers of the Supreme
Court in the administration _of the Act. The
Court is also to prescribe a minimum wage, and
a direction is to be given for preference in
employment to members of industrial unions
or trade unions. Provisions are also made
for the enforcement of decisions and . the
recovery of penalties. Altogether it is a
most comprehensive and discerning measure, and
one that the House of 48 Rich Landlords will
either alter out of all semblance to its original
purpose, or reject with enthusiasm. And the
Assembly knows that the 48 Rich Landlords will
do this. Nine times out of ten it knows, when it
introduces a progressive measure like this, that it
is only wasting time, and that it would be just as
much' use if it introduced its proposals in the
Zoological Gardens or the Waxworks. The right
end to begin at is by shifting the House of Land-
lords first, and there are two visible ways of doing
that. One is to have a coup d’etat and shift the
Upper House with an axe. The other is to send
up a Bill for the establishment of a new Upper
House on the lines of the Federal Senate, and
tack on to it a clause giving a handsome life-
pension to each of the existing 48, and thus pay
them to go out. Until they are got out in one
way or other, democratic legislation in the
Assembly is mostly the purposeless digging of a
big hole, and the purposeless refilling of the same.
