A Blank Form.
An interesting letter from Maoriland :—
Tasmania is in trouble through (in the depth and vast-
ness of its grovel) sending most of its locally-subscribed
Patriotic Funds to England and leaving itself in the
curious position of having to beg for some of its own
money back to relieve its own war-widow with. Maoriland
Premier Seddon must also have seen trouble looming
ahead when he tried recently to get all the M.L. cities to
“pool” their funds and appoint the Public Trustee to
administer same—first taking into consideration local re-
quirements. Unfortunately local jealousies set in, and
the desire of each big business man on the local com-
mittees to stand well with his customers at ’Ome, and top
the score with his own Separate local fund, came out on
top—result, huge sums remitted to the Lord Mayor of
London, and very little kept on hand. It was certainly
understood that the Maoriland Boer-hunter was to receive
the same help from the big fund as his cockney brother,
but the reply received from London to the first M.L.
request for help, and read in the House by Seddon, would
seem to dissipate that impression. It is about three
months since the application was sent in, and after going
through the red-tape departments the substance of the
reply was somewhat as follows: —“ The Commissioners
are —haw —sensible of your generous subscriptions to the
_er —patriotic fund, but we—er—understand from the
Lord Mayor (salaam) that a certain portion of the money
collected for these funds was kept—er—for your own
beastly widows and orphans, don’teherknow. We would
have sent some—er —money to Mrs. B. but for
this information. You can —er—however, give the
woman something on account, if she needs it and is
—er —troublesome, and to enable you to deal—er —
adequately with her case we enclose you a—er —BLANK
FORM ! which please fill up with the —er —creature’s
worldly circumstances, and return it to us with another
BLANK FORM which we enclose for any additional infor-
mation you may be able to—er—give us. We also enclose
SIX spare BLANK FORMS in case there should be any
other beastly widows in your contingents in the future,
and — er — remain, yours, etc., llaw ! ” Poor Seddon read
this document out, and in a most pathetic manner added,
“ We sent them over £30,000, and this is the reply to our
first application.” • The woman’s husband was killed in
action, and this is the experience of Maoriland’s first war
widow while the enthusiasm is still warm. How will the
last war widow get on when the excitement has frozen
hard ? Application three months going, fill up a form,
wait three months more for a chance of a favorable reply,
then perhaps have a few shillings doled out (Maoriland’s
own money, too). Then the blank form to be filled up
with “ further information.” But the climax is reached
with the enclosure of SIX extra blank forms in case there
are any more widows and orphans left by the men M.L.
sent to S’African abattoirs. There are more likely to
be 160 blank forms wanted. Luckily some of the com-
mittees, warned in time after the first crisis in the fever
was over, did keep in hand a certain portion of the sub-
scriptions, and the wisdom of this course has now been
amply proved. But for that forethought there would be
nothing for the Maoriland war widow except a blank
form.
The Victorian Government has a measure in hand
prohibiting a politician holding a seat in the State
and the Federal Parliaments simultaneously. It
it passes Victoria will be able to whip up all its
Federal members when they are wanted for an
important division. . N.S.W., if Lyne’s proposal
to allow the holding of two seats is not promptly
suppressed, mav often be unable to bring moi
than half its Federal members to the scratch
when they are urgently wanted, the rest being
away rolling an important log m the local
Assembly. If N.S.W. allows the same man o
sit in both Parliaments, it is quite probable
that at first, till the electors drop to the
disadvantages of the arrangement, lil ;.
more than half, its federal re P rese ”* a^
will be dual seat-holders, They will
strong democrats—under the Federal Constitutio
it is hardly possible for any other class to be
elected to the Commonwealth Legislature. po
they attend properly to their Federal business it
will mean, to a great extent, leaving the lory
minority to run local affairs. And if they attend
properly to their State business it will mean
largely disfranchising N.S.W. in the Federal Pai-
liament. The logical process is to enact that a
State member may stand for the Federal Parlia-
ment, but that, if elected, he must, within seven
days, resign one of his billets. There is no sense
in making him resign his State billet before he
gets his Federal one, and leave the bone for the
shadow. But he should be compelled to choose
between the two bones.
The Wilkie case, which lasted for 11 solid days in
Melb. Divorce Court, is one more instance ofthe
Law’s splendid capacity for piling up costs Mrs
Wilkie, the petitioner, sued for a divorce from
her husband on two grounds—firstly, habitual
drunkenness and cruelty, and secondly,
“alleged adultery and cruelty, concerning
which the evidence was purely medical. lhat
is to say, it didn’t involve any specific
charge of ad Wery. There was no co-respon-
dent named in the case. The whole of the
evidence bearing on the inferential giddiness of
husband Wilkie, and the physical effects thereof
upon Wilkie’s wife, might have been put through
the Court in one day. Ninety, per cent, of
the evidence related to the ridiculous charge
of habitual drunkenness and cruelty. Day
after day this farce was permittee! to proceed tor
the benefit of lawyers and barristers engaged.
Every man in the street who read the printed
evidence knew that Mrs. Wilkie was wasting
dine and money in her attempt to prove Wilkie
the navvy (as she called him) guilty of habitual
whisky whilst employed in making £lOO,OOO profit
on Westralian railway contracts, and habitual
cruelty whilst he was giving her tens of thousands
of pounds for her own personal use. In granting
a divorce to the well-gilded spouse of . s } lc "
cessful navvy, Chief Justice Madden decided
that she had “failed on the first ground of
drunkenness and cruelty, but had succeeded on
the second ground of adultery and cruelty.
To use a racing simile, Mrs. Wilkie had started
two horses for this legal event. The one horse
was, in Judge Madden’s opinion, a certain
winner, whereas the other was so obviously weak
on all four legs that it ought not to have been
started. And yet the “ wrong ’un ” was allowed
to go fooling round the course for ten or eleven
days at vast expense to the petitioner and
respondent. -A. word from the Judge could have
stopped the hopeless gee-gee at any moment, but
he let it go on and on for all it was worth to
Ikey Mo, the man who lives by the game.
Griffith, M.L.A., recently (28th Aug.) dragged
into the half-light of N.S.W. Assembly what looks
like a most revolting ease of injustice. His story
was to the effect that a contractor habitually sup-
plied to a certain public hospital food of the worst
and cheapest description—putrid fish, decayed
vegetables, and vermicelli so weevily that the dead
insects floated on top of the soup. These horrors
were accepted by the responsible officer despite
the protests of cook, matron, and others, and the
public is left to draw its own inferences as to why
they were accepted. Presumably, there was some
reason, political or otherwise. It is also alleged
that rations were drawn for dead or discharged
patients, which, if true, must have been a source
of handsome profit for somebody. Appeals to the
officer in charge being useless, one of his subor-
dinate's at last drew the attention of the authori-
ties to the matter. The authorities did noth-
ing, and made no inquiry. They simply
allowed things to go on, and they allowed the
man who drew their attention to the scandal to be
promptly sacked for his officiousness. He has
been out of employment ever since. Then the
discharged subordinate besieged the Public Ser-
vice Board and other functionaries, and after long
delay and intense pertinacity, he at last moved
somebody to an inquiry. The result bore out all
his accusations—and what does this free country
suppose followed? The man who was sacked for
doing his duty—for calling attention to a hideous
cruelty practised on the sick and suffering—was
not reinstated. He didn’t get paid for the time
when he was unjustly deprived of his employment.
He wasn’t compensated. He wasn’t given a
better billet, as a man who did his duty fearlessly
and was worthy of trust. He was simply left in
the same state of “sack” as before. And the officer
who neglected his duty so shamefully and
allowed the hospital patients to be treated in this
unspeakably disgraceful fashion—well, he wasn’t
sacked. According to the Parliamentary state-
ment he was only “ transferred.” It doesn’t even
appear for certain that he was transferred to a
worse billet; he may have been shifted to a better
one. And as for the contractor, Premier Lyne
says he thinks that when he heard of these things,
he either cancelled that man’s contracts, or refused
him further contracts, or struck him with a feather,
or something. No attempt was made to deny
Griffith’s story—which may or may not be
correct in all details. If this story, as would
appear, has at. least a substratum of
truth, it is a gruesome reflection on
the incapacity and small meanness of the
Government that it required a Parliamentary
debate to impress on its alleged mind that the
prompt discharge of the man who did his duty,
and the retention in the service of the man who
disgracefully neglected his, wasn’t quite a satis-
factory ending of the difficulty.

Alex. Hutchinson, the ex-N.S.W. M.P. who
has been making several country newspapers’
lives a burden to them with libel-actions, failed
in his case against Singleton Argus last week.
The case was curious enough. There are three
Alexander Hutchinsons, two of them ex-M’sP. ;
and another Hutchinson was imprisoned in
1891 for stealing spoons. A paragraph reporting
the occurrence was printed in several country
papers, adding that the guilty Hutchinson
was an ex-M.P. Then Alex. Hutchinson,
formerly M.P. for Glen Innes, brought actions for
libel apparently all-round. At first he was suc-
cessful ; but in the last case Judge Stephen
summed up in favor of defendants, who printed

The recrudescence at Sydney Royal of the
peaceful, pastoral Tank is probably a sign that
the war is nearly over, and that the blistering and
explosive war drama is off. Also, its contents are
a symbol of the cold water that is being thrown on
all proposals for the prompt payment of the war-
bill. The Tank is cool, homely and placid, and
the olive branch of peace grows by its side ; and
the disinherited Bland Holt, who was cast off
before the tale opened through the machinations
of Arthur Styan re a forged bill, floats on its
surface with three other newsvendors, who all eat
’am sandwiches. Whatever incoherence there
may be in “How London Lives” it will be
remembered by its tank, which is one of the most
beautiful on record. All round it stretches the
property of the man who threw Bland Holt out,
and of that man’s depraved second wife; and
the second wife’s paramour is engaged thereabouts
as secretary. Most of the drama works itself
out beside the smiling Tank. When Miss
Frances Ross is told by her father that she must
marry the secretary and must not wed Albert
Norman she refuses there, and when he bids her
enter his doors no more she casts herself into the
Tank, and Bland Holt fishes her out. When she
has been properly dried and blotted she naturally
marries Norman, and as they were both depen-
dent on the old Tank-owner they sink into great
poverty and naturally have a baby in an attic.
Norman, who is a weak sort of hero, grows weary
and deserts his wife, and walks right out of the
drama—he is supposed to be afterwards killed oy
a Boxer. Wherefore Miss Ross and the baby sink
to deeper depths of poverty, and Bland Holt, who
stands by them to the last, even pawns his hat
and goes home with a newspaper round his
head, in order to get the money to shift a bailiff
named Sandy out of the attic. The old man is
frequently on the verge of relenting and sending his
discarded daughter 6d., or even more, but is
always dissuaded by his second wife and her para-
mour, the secretary. At last he comes so near re-
lenting that the secretary finds it necessary to
murder him in order to save the property, and the
murder is comfortably saddled on Bland Holt, and
the second wife and the secretary see before them
a life of love and affluence. And just here the
secretary’s one good quality is his undoing. For
years he has had a tenderness for the disinherited
daughter, and when Albert Norman was killed in
China he honestly offered her his black heart and
his red hand, and desired to make her a good hus-
band. He did it in a crowded street, in presence
of four newsvendors, just when the speshul edition
was coming out. And the second wife, hearing of
this, casts away the great property and the Tank
for the sake of vengeance, and tells how she and
the secretary did the murder. The story ends
with Frances Ross and her child inheriting the
Tank and the other assets.
* * * m
It’s a somewhat unfinished story, inasmuch
as Albert Norman, who should be the hero, vanishes
early. Also it is never clearly explained how
Bland Holt, who is nephew to the Tank, was
betrayed and reduced to poverty before the drama
Opened. The moral is bad, too, for the secretary
falls over the one redeeming feature in his char-
acter, and if he had had no redeeming feature
Bland would have been hanged, and Miss Ross
would have been left to struggle with the world
and bailiff Sandy. A very long cast does honor-
able service in this drama. Bland Holt is hardly
up to his usual standard, but Mrs. Bland is
pleasant to look upon as a sunbeam kind of
creature in a print frock, which makes the balance
even. Misses Frances Ross and Harrie Ireland,
as the good daughter and the bad step-
mother, are both satisfactory. Also they are both
beautifully dressed in the first act, but
whereas the bad woman remains a dream of
lovely frocking all through, the good one
has to pawn everything with three insulting Jews
named Moses, Isaac, and Abraham, and at last
her only decent property is a leather waistbelt.
Arthur Styan, who is generally successful as a
murderer, or a fraudulent secretary, or some other
lost soul, is commendable as usual. Albert Nor-
man, on the other hand, has been training for
many years as the dramatic bad man, and fills the
part of the disappearing husband rather uneasily.
Among the casual multitude who come and go a
few leave behind a large enough footprint to be
worth mention. Harry Norman’s bailiff is one of
them, and Charles Brown, Max Maxwell, and
Miss Lucy Coppin are three others. The Tank
has already received honorable notice.
“ What Happened to Jones,” or rather the
violent happening of Jones to other people, is
making strong progress at Sydney Palace.
Serious-minded people may complain that no real
family would stand the riotous invasion of Jones,
of the firm of Smith and Co., but in comedy the
rights of Property are suspended, and any substan-
tial citizen is liable to have a strange bagman come
into his house from inadequate motives, and borrow
clothes, and hide his own previous pants in the
piano. The pathetic little play re a small girl’s
“ Empty Stocking ” is meanwhile getting a power-
ful hold on a section of the public to which the
riot of Jones is only a vociferous mystery. There
is much human nature in its four characters. The
patient and hungry little girl who is waiting sadly
for an unpunctual Santa Claus, the writer whose
manuscripts won’t sell, and the awful girl from
downstairs, are all very real; and the unex-
pected father, though less lifelike than the rest, is
good enough to pass muster. Miss Dot Frederic
never did anything better than the offensive girl
from downstairs, and little Edna Arnold is so
many miles above the usual child-actress that, if
she fell, the result would probably be fatal, or, at
the very least, she would bounce severely.
m* # *
The beautiful opera of “ lolanthe ” is to
be revived at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, on Satur-
day. The cast is mostly new. George Lauri is
to be the Chancellor who gives agreeable girls
away, and a community which is accustomed to a
long thin Chancellor must learn to accept a short
thick one instead. Wallace Brownlow will be
Strephon, he of the airy legs and the frivolous
hop, whereas the community has grown accus-
tomed to seeing its beloved Brownlow in heavier
characters, with a crown on his head, and ordering
people to be executed or otherwise discouraged.
Jennie Opie will be the Fairy Queen, pretty
Carrie Moore is cast for lolanthe—in fact, barring
Earls Tolloller and Mountararat, almost every-
body will be fitted with a new part. Probably
Lauri will be the most difficult to accept. A long
and bony Chancellor is one of the local traditions
of “lolanthe.”
** * *
The incident on which Marie Corelli
founded her “Vendetta,” now being played as
“ The Power of Wealth ” at Sydney Criterion, is
unreliably alleged to have really occurred at some
Italian city, and Marie, like the good, dear
woman that she is, wrote her story as a warning
to her widowed friends not to speak slightingly of
the deceased till he had remained dead at least
five days. Three days, she discovered, are not
sufficient proof that gentle woman is really in
that condition in which women wish to be who
don’t love their lords, but are mashed on some-
body else. This Corellian version of the swell
that came back just as well as ever causes great
thrills at the Criterion. And when the Old
Clothes man tells the hero how the Judge exon-
erated him for killing his Flossie wife because he,
the Judge, reflected that he had a wife himself,
and might require to kill her some day, it gives
a glimpse of society as it is in stage Italy,
wherein men descant publicly on their wives’
virtue, and make casual after-dinner remarks on
the purity of these same goods and chattels. When
a (stage) Italian woman has virtue it is necessary to
mention the fact. The Cri. d.c. hears those
views of matrimonial severity with interest, and
the small husbands of large, plain women who
wear their hair straight and have long Roman
noses, inwardly resolve that they will follow the
old-clothes philosopher’s plan if they ever come
home and find, &c. The Criterion earthquake is
not much of a warning to would-be erring wives,
as the pillars merely fold over quietly like so
many yard measures, while the great, hard
boulders from Vesuvius or somewhere bounce up
from the vault when they touch it and do no harm
whatever. But as the “Power of Wealth ” ends
on Thursday night, “Hamlet” following on
Friday and the gruesome “Drink” on Saturday,
this frivolity on the part of the boulders is of little
importance.
** * *
By a person who was there :
A wild rumour went about that the Illingworth Benefit
was to give a representation of the Latin Quarter in Paris,
with wild-eyed Australian geniuses wandering about,
dropping fragments of poetry and sublime thoughts in the
gay and giddy atmosphere. Why the real affair was
called “A Night in Bohemia” no one can tell, as any-
thing more like a respectable concert was never seen.
Some of it was good, some of it was bad, but the long-
haired geniuses were quite lost in the crowd of short-haired
folk who had come to gaze upon them, and could only gaze
upon one another. During the first part of the programme
the look of stolid bourgeois endurance on the faces of the
would-be Bohemians was conspicuous, not because the
programme was a bad one, but because the bourgeois per-
sonage always takes his pleasures that way. The idea
was to have a promenade concert, and one 10 minutes’ in-
terval was announced to be given up to fraternal convi-
vialities, but most of the people stuck to their seats in
case they shouldn’t get them again, and merely
glared at one another by way of friendship. The
men were allowed to smoke after 9 o’clock, and
the noble artist-soul did not prevent them spoiling one
of the best effects on the organ with the universal
crackling of matches. George Rignold gave the hack-
neyed “ Oration of Marc Antony,” and when he said he
wished he lay with Caesar most of the audience wished so,
too. He was in splendid form, however, not forgetting
his part more than twice. C. W. St. Julian, and what
somebody called the Mustard-Slingers, sang the never-
stale “ Funiculi Funicula.” Mr. Sun Johnson, in his ren-
dering of a song, showed the difference between English
and Chinese ideas of music ; the complications in his
native land seem to have got on his brain. The items of
the programme were given erratically, a good number
that were in being left out, and another lot that weren’t
mentioned being interpolated. The song by Nelson
Illingworth, jun., was a happy inspiration. At 11 p.m.
the authorities ignored the other items as yet unsung,
and the alleged Bohemians went home decorously. And
now the world knows at last how they amuse themselves
in Bohemia.

Fitzgerald’s Circus, well and honourably
known in these parts, and including the Dunbar
Trio, Mademoiselle Margharetta, the Cinnezallas,
the Jandaschefskys, and others, is to open at
Sydney next Saturday. It will be found on the
old stand, near Redfem station, and conveniently
adjacent to the Labour Bureau, the Benevolent
Asylum and the Cemetery.
m* * *
New songs are scattered up and down
Sydney Tivoli programme this week. Mr.
Arthur Crane comes out with “ A Father’s
Advice; ” while Billy McClain warbles agi-
tatedly of his Black Baby. Miss May Lewis
generally favors us with ditties wherein the chief
sentiment is a request that she may be his. If it
were put as a gracious permission for him to be
hers, it would be more suitable to these days of
Woman’s Rights. This week is stated to be the
last of the Coon Ballet. Its departure is some-
what of a relief; while the Coon Ballet faced the
audience it was fairly right, but when it turned
its back . Arthur Crane and Wallace King
have started duetting concerning the classic young
man who climbed up a mountain for no purpose
discernible, with a banner in his hand, and a re-
mark in bad Latin floating from his mouth at the
end of every verse.
** * *
The Crane-Power Co. made a first Mel-
bourne appearance at Her Majesty’s on Saturday,
starting its short season with “Trilby.” In
some respects the present performance is in
advance of the one that flourished at the Princess’
four years ago, but in other respects it isn’t. The
stage pictures are not so well displayed in the
oval-shaped theatre, and the deeply impressive
voice of Regina Nagel is not there to give a sound
of truth to the name part. On the other hand,
the burden of the old Little Billee has been lifted
from the show and replaced by a young man
whose head is unobtrusive and general appearance
not altogether aggravating. The old Billee had
a head of the pumpkin order, and a habit of crying
“O Gard!” and “Tarfy ! Tarfy ! ” which was
fatal to the illusion. So taking one thing with
another, and allowing for the fact that a number
of people will now see “ Trilby” for the first time,
the management has nothing to fear from com-
parisons.
* • » *
Svengali now-a-nights is a less impish
creature than the Jew that Reuben Fax intro-
duced to us of yore. Tyrone Power makes his
own music, instead of pluggingaway at a dummy
piano in a wild, irresponsible manner. There is
some dignity about the greasy man of talent when
he goes to borrow five francs. He appears to
think more and laugh less than the previous
Svengali, and he sacrifices melodramatic points in
the interests of realism, but perhaps Fax’s per-
formance was all the stronger for its extra lurid-
ness. Svengali is bound to look as though he had
escaped from a ghost story. His influence over
the young woman with the naked instep and trans-
cendental toe-nails doesn’t work by the rules of
hypnotism. The idea of a hypnotist walloping
his tame prima-donna because she jibs at a top-
note is quite unreasonable, for if he pours his own
soul into the lady she naturally must do her best
to carry out its instructions. No matter. Miss
Edith Crane’s handsome Trilby has improved
during these four years, also the new Laird
(W. F. Grant) is a very satisfactory stranger, and
Cyril Keightley, as Taffy, wears his long whiskers
as though he had grown them himself. Gecko,
in the first two acts, is a limp reminder of our
previous Gecko, but subsequently he turns
upon Svengali with unexpected vigour, and
may be said to fill the bill. Mrs. G. B. W.
Lewis, it goes without saying, makes a wel-
come re - appearance on the stage in the
part of Madame Yinard. Her French accent
was rather rusty last Saturday night, but she
comes as a boon and a blessing after the inferior
specimens of “ old woman ” acting that Melbourne
has lately endured. Oily Deering scores as the
oily shepherd who gets reconciled to Trilby’s
moral character on discovering that the Prince of
Wales has made inquiries after her health. Cecil
Ward is a pretty fair Zou-Zou, and the very small
parts are neatly filled. But “ Trilby ” it seems,
is only a six-nights show. On Saturday the
company will play “ The Christian ” for the first
time in Melbourne, or mostly the first time.
Half an act of the drama was produced at the
Princess’s on the night of J. C. Williamson’s
farewell, just before he immediately came back
again.
** # #
On Saturday night the deceased Svengali
committed the grievous error of a speech to the
audience after the third act, reminding one of the
new warbler at Rickards’s, who usually thanks his
applauders kindly when he has got through his
third ditty and hopes that his fourth and last
effort will give them further satisfaction. Luckily
Tyrone Power’s oration was brief and neat.

** * *
At the Bijou, the same programme as
before is just now appealing to the sentiment of
Agricultural Show-week, and the temple of
Rickards resounds with the gusty, lusty laugh of
the occasional visitor from up-country. It is
understood that Paul Martinetti and Co. will pack
up their pantomimes and depart at the end of this
week, unless they stay till Monday. Anyway,
they must be gone from Melbourne ere the red
dawn of the next Bulletin, and Emmonds and
Emerson, or somebody else, will fill the gap.

Definitely stated that George Musgrove’s
reign at Melbourne Princess’s and Sydney
Lyceum will open in Melbourne with a short and
quick-change season of classic opera in English.
“Faust ” is almost certain to come first on the list
(a fine organ has been shipped from London for
“Faust”), and the after productions will be

some comic opera, with Nellie Stewart to the fore.
When this second co. moves along to Sydney in
its turn Nellie will stay behind to play principal
boy in a panto, at the Princess’s.

Fatal Thirteen ! In Sydney harbour, on
Sunday last, a sudden south-westerly squall cap-
sized an 18-footer sailing craft, containing 13
young men, of whom four were drowned, though
neither of the only two inferior swimmers of the
party perished. The same squall “turtled”
another craft of the same size, but all ten occu-
pants escaped.
Bressi, the late King of Italy's slayer, has
been sentenced to life-imprisonment. They never
were too hard on homicide in Italy.
The necessity for public inspection of all so-
called private orphanages and charitable institu-
tions has received striking exemplification in the
case of Stoke (Nelson, M.L.) monastically-con-
ducted orphanage. What is wanted is not the
common or garden prurient-eyed old-maid inspec-
tion, but inspection by competent Govt, officials,
well-versed in the horrors of philanthropy. The
evidence evoked by a recent Govt, inquiry into
the ways of Stoke Orphanage shows a state of
affairs "of the Middle Ages, and the Marist
Brothers who conducted the institution are
just now at a considerable discount. Latest
news is that the brothers have been
“ removed ” by whom is not stated.
A prominent medical man asserted that the in-
mates of the school were stunted, insufficiently
fed, and vermin-stricken. Evidence was given
asserting that several lads were starved, kept in
chains for a month, and beaten on the bare flesh
with supplejacks, and that one was kicked on the
head by one gentle Brother, while another struck
him in the mouth and face with a bunch of cell
keys! The boys marked for ‘ ‘ punishment ” used at
night, it was deposed, to be forced to lift their
shirts whilst leaning over a desk as a preliminary to
receiving as many as 30 strokes on the bare flesh.
The lads suffered agonies through their filthy gar-
ments festering in their wounds, one boy being
quite unable to sit down for a fortnight. At
Stoke the lonely orphan seems to have been used
as a sledge-horse, and his time, so said the evi-
dence, was spent in carrying loads of heavy posts
from the bush to the Home. The simple rule
seems to have been that those who didn’t carry
enough posts got no tea. The reward of those
who were strong enough for the work proved on
one occasion to be a piece of festering cow that
had died in a neighboring gully —which, it must be
admitted, reads like a rather tall yarn. Anyhow,
Stoke orphanage seems to have been simply a place
where certain persons who were too lazy to carry
their own wood, and too mean to buy a horse, got
the work done gratis by orphans. Otherwise it
appears to have existed chiefly in order to let
off the bile of certain Brothers —the kind of
brothers who like to flog something and who want
to have something small and convenient, and
plenty of it, to practise on.
The Wild Cat Column.

Apropos of the Philp Government’s announce-
ment that Queensland can’t borrow any more at present
for railway construction, and the consequent proposal to
hand over the building of the big Cloncurry line —the
first section of a trans-continental railway—to a syndicate
with power to charge, for 50 years, 11 times the fares and
freights now charged on the State lines—just consider
the matter in the cold light of arithmetic and see its out-
rageous folly! The existing northern lines practically
pay their way on existing charges, and yet they are bur-
dened with interest on money borrowed at a fraction over
£3lss. per cent. Now, the last loan, floated a few weeks
ago, showed that Queensland could borrow money at just
about £3 10s. per cent. Therefore it is fair to assume
that it could get it at £3 15s. per cent, with reasonable
ease. That is a high rate, but still, if the Cloncurry
line was built with money borrowed at £3 15s. per cent,
it would still be built on cheaper principles than the
other North Queensland lines, and as it is a more promis-
ing line than niost of the others, the reasonable assump-
tion is that it would pay at ordinary fares.
And instead, by its syndicate proposals, the un-
speakably foolish Philp Government proposes doing some-
thing which is equal to borrowing at £8 ss. per cent. On
a North Queensland line which just pays its way, about
7d. out of every shilling paid on the present scale of
charges, by the people who use the railway, goes in work-
ing expenses, and about sd. in interest on capital— i.e.,
money borrowed for construction at £3 15s. per cent.
The syndicate proposes to charge Is. 6d. where the State
charges Is. Working expenses need be no higher ; the
same 7d. will cover them. That leaves lid. for interest
on capital, so the tax on the people of Queensland will
be the same as if the Cloncurry line was built with money
borrowed for 50 years at £8 ss. per cent.! The burden on
the people is the same whether the money goes in fares
paid to a foreign syndicate of railway-owners, or in fares
paid to a foreign syndicate of money-lenders. The in-

North Chillagoe Mines : The prospectus of this
group is out, accompanied by reports from Jack, ex-Q.
Govt. Geologist, and Dunstan, assistant ditto. The latter
says the group undoubtedly bear a most favorable com-
parison with any other in Chillagoe district; that they
have been selected simply on their own merit, and their
value is not influenced bv their proximity to high-priced
mines. And the promoters claim that this is a large
development company, in which shareholders will partici-
pate in the profits made in the development, as well as
profits likely to be obtained by flotation from the English
market when the twelve properties have become suffi-
ciently worked to justify their being placed into inde-
pendent co.s. Already one-half of the issue has been
subscribed, and the placing of the balance is practically
The Austral Otis Engineering Co. have just
completed a concentrating test on the Wilfley Table in-
stalled at their South Melbourne works of a parcel of
crushed ore representing the second product from the
concentrating Mill at the Conrad Mine, owned by Mr. J.
Howell, which hitherto has all been stacked on the mine
waiting the advent of a machine that would profitably
treat it without re-crushing. The result of the Wilfley
test is a high-grade concentrate, and tailing waste show-
ing very little values, and the Otis Co. have despatched
the tables for erection on the mine.*

credible Philp holds that because Queensland can barely
manage to borrow at £3 10s. per cent, and might have to
pay as much as £3 15s. for the money to build the Clon-
curry line (the money to build all the other lines being
borrowed at an average of rather over £3 155.), therefore
the only thing is to commit the country for 50 years to
what is exactly the same as borrowing at £8 ss. per cent.!
Another strange point is this : The cheapest big loan
Queensland ever floated before this year cost £3 11s. 7id.
per cent, in interest. This year Queensland floated a big
loan which cost it ahout £3 10s. or £3 11s. per cent. And
having floated the cheapest individual big loan Queens-
land ever got, Premier Philp declares wildly that State
railway construction must be abandoned, for further bor-
rowing is evidently impossible. So just when Queens-
land’s loans are at their cheapest he falls back on the
syndicate at £8 ss. per cent. Barring two little borrow-
ings of about a quarter of a million, the loan which broke
Philp up, and made him throw State railway construction
overboard, was the best on Queensland’s record. Queens-
land’s credit may not be Al, but just let Philp try Sydney
and Melbourne with a 4 per cent, loan before he casts
himself on the bosom of his £8 ss. per cent, shind.vkit 1
Query : What is Ph Ip’s motive ? Is he only a tremendous
fool in matters financial ? Or has he been deluded by some
very clever people who are grinding the biggest axe in
Queensland behind the scenes ?
Further, Premier Philp stated as a reason for
handing over railway-construction to private syndicates,
that the State railways have run Queensland into a loss of
£2,428,114. Well, they wouldn’t have done that if the
State had charged even 25 per cent, higher fares, not 50
per cent, higher, as the Cloncurry syndicate is going to
do. As it is, by charging unprofitably low fares the
people paid in taxes instead of in railway rates, so it is as
broad as it is long. Also Premier Philp might have added
another thing. The southern railways have lost about
£2,200,000 ; the central and northern lines have only lost
about £225,000, and now these latter are practically pay-
ing their way. Therefore l’hilp’s argument is, by his own
mode of reasoning, good cause why the State should build
northern and central railways itself, and let syndicates
try their hands at the work down south. To abandon
State railway construction in the profitable region,
because it is a loss in another part hundreds of miles
away—to give the profitable end of the work to syndi-
cates and keep the unprofitable end for the State —is true
Boodle Government logic.
* * * '*
The Victorian Government’s offer of £500,000
worth of 3 per cent, stock locally at a minimum of £96
brought tenders for about £900,000. The loan fetched
about £96 65., so it is again worth remembering that this
local loan realised a better price than Victorian stock ever
realised in London. In fact all the inscribed stock which
the Victorian Government has sold in Melbourne has
brought a better price than the very best ever realised
in London, and all the bonds sold in Melbourne have
fetched a far better price than the bonds sold in London.
The only question is whether the local market is able to
take up big loans. Tub Bulletin reckons it is able to do it
six times over. If the Victorian Government had offered
in Melbourne a £2,000,000 loan at the price it would have
to pay in London it would probably have got offers of
£5,000,000. Of course while the Australian Governments,
in their painful and habitual grovel to England, will offer
the Australian investor much less than the English one,
th, v won’t get the offer of very large amounts. But that
is no reflection on the capacity of the local market.
South Australia, by the way, recently floated a
one-million 3percent, loan in London,and thenet proceeds
appear to be about £9O 12s. per £l9O. And Victoria had
offers of 1900,000 in Melbourne when it fixed the minimum
at £96. On this basis it seems reasonably certain that S. A.
could have got its million in Melbourne and Adelaide
with perfect "ease at £94 and thus saved over £30,000 and
a good deal of interest, income tax, expense in remitting
interest, &c. But it has never offered its stock locally at
anything like the price it was glad to accept in England,
and because the Australian market won’t tender money
as freely at £97 as the English one does at £92 it is
alleged that it is “ unable to absorb large loans.”
Apropos of these two loans and many other
loans The Bulletin rises to repeat that the practice of
floating loans at a discount is pure swindle. S.A., for ex-
ample, raises £906,000 by incurring £1,000,000 liability.
It will never have more than £906,000 of reproductive
works to show for the money. Its credit was only good
enough in London to enable it to borrow at about 3J per
cent. Instead of paying 3i per cent, honestly, and get-
ting full value for its securities, it pays less interest, and
to do so adds £94,000 to its permanent debt. That
£94,000 is really part of the interest, paid in a dishonest,
surreptitious way out of loan moneys. When a State
whose credit is only good enough for 31 per cent, floats a
3 per cent, loan at a discount it is as truly using loan
moneys to pay part of its interest bill as if it floated a
loan expressly for the purpose. That is what S.A. has
done, unless it adds £94,000 from revenue, and thus
makes the full £1,000,000 available for the purposes for
which the loan was floated—and when was any Australian
State honest enough to do a thing like that ?

The Federal Palace Hotel, Limited (Melbourne),
shows remarkable signs of improving fortunes. In the
boom days this concern had a paid-up capital of £123,584,
mostly represented by building and ground, then valued
at £151,082, and burdened with a mortgage of £70,000.
With the great slump in property about £llO,OOO of
capital was written-off, and it now stands at £13,485
paid-up. The hotel and ground is described as worth
£66,500, subject to a mortgage of £59,000, the other
£ll,OOO having been paid off by careful management.

The Australian Newspaper Co. (Sydney), the
concern which publishes the Australian Star, is an
enterprise which seems to make little progress in a
financial sense. For the year ended 30th June, 1900, the
net earnings are set down at £1963, and a balance of
£l5O brought forward from previous year makes £2113.
A very moderate dividend of 5 per cent, absorbs £2OOO
(£37 more than the earnings), so the balance carried for-
ward is reduced to £ll3. This £ll3 is the only semblance
of a reserve fund which the co. possesses, and even its
existence is less certain than might be. The chief asset is
“ Premises, plant, furniture, stationery, and goodwill,
£31,783.” How much of this is goodwill doesn’t appear,
but it is reasonably certain that the goodwill is set
down for more than the £ll3 which forms the sole visible
reserve, and the surplus, whatever it may be, represents
a shortage in the visible assets as against the liabilities.
It is questionable if an enterprise which earns barely 5
per cent, on its capital has any goodwill—whether it can be
said to have any selling value apart from the value of its
visible assets. Certainly it can t have much. At
events the amount of the alleged goodwill should be
separately stated. The shareholders ought to know how
they stand, and a balance-sheet which doesn t ™
much of the assets are real and how much is the more or
less dubious value set on the business, does not tell them
how they stand. The accounts for the last two years can
be lumped together thus o aoa

. £72,650 £69,533
On paper, at all events, the Cascade Brewery Co. has im-
proved its position to the extent of £3117 in eight years.
It has a paid-up capital of £168,750 and total reserves (on
paper) of £16,522. But the Cascade Brewery Co. is
one of those remarkable concerns which square their
accounts with a paper item of “goodwill.” This is set
down at £25,000, so the visible assets are less than the
visible liabilities by £8478. Goodwill is in some cases
a less papery item than in others ; the pathos of
the thing is that it is generally the concern which
has no visible goodwill which puts the item among
its assets most assiduously. In the present case,
a brewery whose visible assets are, even on its own
valuation (which may, or may not, be a greatly
over-stated valuation), £8478 less than its liability to
shareholders and others, and which is only paying 3 per
cent, on its capital, asks the public to believe that the
goodwill of its business is worth £25,000 —-this at a time
when investors can get about 3J per cent, in State securi-
ties. The effort is too great a strain on the imagination.
The Cascade Brewery Co. should write off that goodwill
item at once so far as its reserves will allow, and then
extinguish the balance out of earnings with all possible
speed. After that it should lay the foundations of a real
reserve fund. A reserve fund which is all represented by
goodwill is very unreal.

The big Sydney stock, station, and wool firm of
Pitt, Son and Badgery, Limited, shows net profits for the
last four years as follows :
30th June, 1897 .. £1,841 I 30th June, 1899 .. £4,043
30th „ 1898 .. 2,731 | 30th „ 1900 .. 6,555
In each case no dividend has been paid, the profits being
carried to a special reserve to meet bad and doubtful
debts. There is a material improvement in earnings,
which are now equal to £l3 2s. 2d. per cent, per annum
on the paid-up capital of £50,000, and if it were free of the
burden of these ‘ ‘ bad and doubtful debts ” this co. would
be a highly profitable institution at present. The balance-
sheet conveys no information as to the amount of said
bad and doubtful debts, so it is impossible to say how fast
they are being 1 reduced, and whether old doubtful debts are
turning good, or whether new bad debts are arising as
fast as, or faster than, the old ones are paid on.
The principal purpose of a balance-sheet, under the
ridiculous Australian company laws, is to conceal a com-
pany’s position. But at 30th June 1899, Pitt, Son and
Badgery’s bank overdraft was £65,437, and other creditors
£6680 (total, £72,117), while at 30th June, 1900, overdraft
was £55,741 and other creditors £3009 (total, £58,750),
which is a very large and promising reduction of liabilities,
and looks as if the concern was rapidly righting itself. A
notable item among the assets is, “ Goodwill, office furni-
ture, plant, &c-, £25,000. How much of this is the purely
paper item of goodwill doesn’t appear, and when this and
the absence of all information about the bad and doubtful
debts are added together, it is impossible to guess how
much of the shareholders’ nominal funds (paid-up capital
£50,000 and reserve fund £7500) are intact on present
values. It can only be said that the co. appears to be
making good progress at replacing the loss, whatever it
may be. When this country has a decent company law
it will be compulsory to show the paper item of goodwill
separately, in order to reveal how much of the assets are
only , , .

The Farmers’ Co-operative Fire and Marine In-
surance Association of Canterbury (Maoriland) has now
been 9i years in operation, with these good results :
The co. with the long name has accumulated £7250'
of reserves (general reserve fund, £6OOO, and rein-
surance reserve, £1250), which is by no means bad.
for the first 9£ years of a very small concern. It
has also £llOO undivided profit (subject to adjustment
when the amount of the policy-holders’ bonus for 1900 is
ascertained, the £3420 set aside for dividend purposes
being only an approximation). It has paid a dividend to
shareholders every year, and has given policy-holders a 10
per cent, bonus on their premiums every year except two.
And—again apropos of that mendacious and interested
expert who tries to scare the State off taking a hand in
fire insurance business by assuring it that such business
can’t pay if confined to one State, as Government insur-
ance would be, and that “ a wide diffusion of risks” is
absolutely required—the Farmers’ Co-operative has pro-
duced these results, not only by sticking to one province,
but mostly by sticking to one corner of it. The only
dubious feature about this concern is that its funds are
almost wholly invested in mortgages. Still, its invest-
ments yield over 6 per cent, all-round, which is a highly
respectable rate of interest.

At the Junction North meeting in Melbourne
a shareholder wanted to abolish the London office, estab-
lished in the few days of the co.’s fatness. Directors
admitted it was useless, but said it would be a breach of
faith to do what was asked. And yet expenses will have
to be pared somehow. In 12 months, to the end of July,
42,390 tons of ore were taken out of the mine (and 42,360
tons of ore make a big hole, especially in a mine like
the Junction N.), and the profit, according to the direc-
tors, was £4159 ; but, counting in all that was spent on
development, it was really less than that by a few hun-
dred pounds. Per ton of ore, in fact, it was Is. Bd.—just Id.
per cwt. And that notwithstanding that the metal mar-
kets have been Dooming this last year. No doubt develop-
ments at the deep level are good; the ore there is
better grade than the average crushed last year. But the
management will have to keep its nose to the grindstone.
A few months, or perhaps weeks, will give a better idea
whether it wouldn’t be wiser to seek amalgamation on
some terms with the North.

Compare the recovery of the metals contained
in the ore respectively in the North and the South groups
of B.H. mines South group: South, 67'39 per cent,
of the lead and 50 51 per cent, of the silver ; Block 10,
72 -31 per cent, of the lead and 35'35 per cent, of the
silver. North group: North, 54'1 per cent, of the lead
and 37'1 per cent, of the silver ; Junction North, 55 per
cent, of the lead and 31 per cent, of the silver ; Junction,
52 per cent, of the lead and 30'7 per cent, of the silver.
The difference is partly due to the fact that the Southern
mines (the Sulphide Corporation’s figures are about the
same as Block 10’s) have rather more appliances for re-
treating slimes, &c. ; but it is mainly due to the fact that
the northern ore is more refractory, though the North’s is
less so than its neighbors’. It will be seen, though, that
onlv one of them all saves half the silver.

Block 14 tested its mill capacity the other week
lt weighed all the ore it put through, and made the tally
2905 tons. This co. is putting through rather better ore
again. Its neighbor, the British, is making a “new
departure ” in prospecting its northern block with a dia-
mond drill. At the other B.H. mines the most interest-
ing features are the discovery that the new South Blocks
can mine payable mill-sulphides from the start (but it
hasn’t a mill) and the completion at last of the small
Odling magnetic zinc plant at Block 10.
* * * *
B.H. South has been obliged to adopt a resolu-
tion for dealing with divs. unclaimed after one year. This
co. was for 8 or 10 years regarded as hopeless ; it struggled
for 12 years before paying a div., passed through three or
four reconstructions, and got into touch with final
liquidation once. Hence a good many shares have
apparently been thrown aside and forgotten, so that at
the end of the last half-year there remained £4700 of divs.
uncalled-for.

The Federal Centrifugal Dredging Co., near
Braidwood, N.5.W.—2740z. ; result of 33 shifts of 8 hours
- outlay said to be £350, which should therefore mean
nearly £7OO profit. Shares selling at 7s. ; show run
chiefly by a Ballarat crowd. Turon No. 2 Dredge.—
28oz. for week ; shares 305., and every Sunday people are
advised to buy them, but no more is said about getting
“ round the corner at Golden Point.” People are weary
of most things and they are becoming rather tired of the
eternal over-boosting of the Turon. Araluen Pro-
prietary.— 260 z. gold for less than a week’s work. Sellers
20s. no buyers reported. Running a dredge under favor-
able’ circumstances is cheap biz., but these little yields are
not good enough under the best conditions. There
will be a few dredging successes in N.S.W. and many
hideous failures ; some of the latter will be on the Shoal-
haven River, the top part of which may be suitable for
dredging. So, too, that part of the river in Nowra dis-
trict'’but the 100 miles of river-bed and banks between are
quiteunfitted for the game, and anyattempt todredge them
will ignominiously fail. The Shoalhaven had a sluicmg-
boom some 8 or 10 years ago when many scores of thou-
sands sterling were lost in genuine efforts to establish the
hydraulic sluicing industry- Not a word is said of this in
any of the dredging prospectuses. Of course, hydraulic
sluicing was all wrong—the modern bucket-dredge is
going to do the trick. Attempts were then made to dam
back parts of the river with the object of getting the
« rich gold deposits ” at the bottom, and those who made
them have been “damning ” the Shoalhaven ever since.
* • * ♦
Associated Gold Mines: Wonders will never
cease with this concern. The very latest: “ This morning
the cages ceased working in Tetley’s shaft owing to the
pit having come together. This was caused through
stoping being carried on too close to the shaft.” It ap-
pears that this mine has been gutting out the last of
Tetlev’s shoot, so “ their troubles ” about the shaft coming
together. As a result of the gouging, this month’s return
is expected to be better than last. The newest sensation
in London is the appointment to the board of a gentleman
who reported on the mine last year and went one better
than even the manager. He then declared that there was
750,0000 z. gold in sight in the mine. Directors seized on
this, and after the chairman had made much of it as a
confirmation of their own manager’s report, shares
reached £l3—a modest valuation of 5& millions. When
the bottom fell right out of the mine and the market
dropped 4i millions, the optimist was asked what he meant
by it, and he still said the gold was there. When asked how
long he was on the mine, he replied, “Three whole days.’
The three experts who came out —Frecheville, Angovy,
and Prof. Liveing—took three months over their examina-
tion, and their report was the result of as exhaustive a
sampling as was ever given to a mine ; its correctness has
been confirmed by the mine itself. Nevertheless they are
still regarded bv many people as the wreckers of the.
show. It is admitted that the biggest gilded fools in
thG world are in London, so it can he assumed that there
are some shareholders in the Associateds living there
who think that the election to the Board of this sayer of
sweet things will be followed by his getting the 750,0000 z.
out of the mine. Here is briefly the present position.
The less than 40,000 tons of pay ore left in the mine
when the three experts completed their examination is
almost exhausted and the average return obtained
has confirmed their estimates of value. The nulls
have shut down for want of payable ore to crush,
the mine is gutting out the balance of Tetley s
shute for the purpose of shipping sulphides and
making as good a show as possible under the distressing
■circumstances. Thousands of feet of development-work
in the form of sinking, driving and boring in all direc-
tions with a view of finding some more ore have been
■done, so far without any result. A little more of this pro-
specting and the mine, unless something wonderful hap-
pens, must be regarded as a property of one payable ore-
shute like its neighbors, the Kalgurli, on one side, and
the Central and West Boulder, on the other. Fortunately
it has over £200,000 cash to go on with, and if it is the
intention to spend all this money in prospecting, then
the Associateds are even bigger fools than writer
imagines; much better buy another mine with some of
it. Shares still show a market valuation of £1,700,000
this for a mine that has never paid a div. for a year, has
no hope of one this year, and could only W
out of capital if it were used for that purpose. T e
are 500,000 shares in the Associated, and upwards m
5000 shareholders, consequently there is no P“ b h l 'P I'® 1 '®
pect of any bear-squeeze. It is for that reason that
a million shares have been oversold, and but for this over
selling the shares to-day would not be one-third their
market value. _ - ,
• * * *
“Moses Moss,” the mining man °f that mis-
named London paper, Troth, chuckles over the alleged
fact that some bears have been caught by Whittaker
Wright, who has been laying wait for them ever since he
himself was caught as a “bull ” about a year : •
sees nothing wrong in “bulling shares to thiee
their value by lying statements, etc., m newspapers, or
in bringing about a “corner,” ami making people pay
£l5 for shares that are not worth 15s. M.M. is pro-
bably so innocent that he saw nothing wrong in the
“Half-forgotten Frauds”—when London CliiTicexposed
the swindles perpetrated in London with the able assist-
ance of several most respectable newspapers. Stated in
Adelaide that that nerveful operator R. H. Hemung, has
been caught to the extent of £125,000 m the Le Roi No. 2
deal. This co. came out a few months ago as an offshoot
of the Le Roi, a copper mine in Bntish Columbia. It was
manipulated by Whittaker Wright for the London Globe
Finance Corporation. A small proportion of the shares
were widely distributed, and other traps were laid. The
moment it was found that some big parcels had been
sold “short” all the outside scrip was quietly bought
up, and when the mail left the shares were standing
at £l5, and the last cabled report is that they have
reached £3O ; so no doubt “ Moses Moss is again shriek-
ing with joy in Truth. Whittaker Wright’s crowd be it
remembered, are the people who control the Consols and
Ivanhoe mines, the shares of which rise and fall £1 per
day, and are usually far beyond the price warranted
either by yields, prospects, or divs. These two mines
fluctuate more than all the others of Hannan s. It is
also rumored that some “bears have recently been
caught in over-selling these shares, and the same Moses
Moss ” insinuates that some of them will soon have to quit
London for Montana and elsewhere. It is altogether a
lovely state of things, and indicates pretty plainly the
condition of the mining industry of Westraha, where
there are several mines so monstrously over-valued that,
if all the gold they produce was profit, the dividends
would not represent more than bank-interest on the in-
vestment —and this is in face of the fact that the cost of
mining there is the highest in the world.
* * * ♦
Mt. Lyell. —Writer was wrong last week in
assuming that there would be no div. this quarter on
account of the South Tharsis purchase and others in con-
templation. Allowing for these the mine has not earned
the usual div. this quarter, but has paid a little one of 2s.
No doubt had the money been held m reserve, other
people, who have mines for sale in the district but are
greedy with regard to price, would have at once jumped
to the conclusion that it was being kept for them and
would have regulated prices accordingly. The S. Tharsis
crowd showed their good sense in taking £45,000, but even
this good sense would not have been arrived at but for
the heavy purchases made on account of the Mt. Lyell Co.
itself. Next hardest thing to selling a mine is sometimes
to buy one at double its intrinsic value. The S. Tharsis,
as a concentrating copper proposition, was too low-grade
to pav and had it been 2 miles further away from Mt.
Lyell it would have been no use to that Co. and could
never have paid for working on its own.

Croydon notes to Aug. 17 : Golden Gate 3 and
4 South Tribute cleaned up finally, making yield 91650 z.
lldwts. for a month’s run on 1059 tons ore. Aggregate
value of gold, £23,701. Tribute Syndicate divided
£16,640, and the Proprietary Syndicate out of royalty
divided £4400, making total div. from crushing £21,040.
An illustration elsewhere shows the 20 gold bars got from
this record crushing. During two years, or less, the mine
has crushed 9473 tons ore for 36,2970 z. gold, gross value
£96 927, and paid divs. to its owners and tributors aggre-
gating £72,459. Golden Gate 4 and 5 South Block re-
cent crushing, 200 tons for 6230 z., valued at £1592.
Jubilee Consols (True Blue) recently discovered rich gold
at 400 ft. vertical; gold improving in value with depth.
This is the deepest-level gold worked on Croydon. G.
Gate mines and field generally looking well, and with
rain, which is badly wanted, would boom.

SOCIETY
The difficulty of fittingly rechristening an
organisation was proved last week by the Anglican
Synod sitting in Sydney to be as great as that of
rechristening an adult individual. A name is
something, The lame notion of calling the
Anglican Church in Australia the British church,
as one dignitary suggested, or the Empire, as
suggested by another, or the Kangaroo, as hinted
by a third, or the Anglo-Australian or the Anglo-
Catholic or any other of the absurd inventions,
was properly banned. As well rechristen George
Reid Harry, W. J. Lyne Edward, or John Norton
Joseph.
* * * m
At the Anglican Church Synod in Sydney
the other day the Dean of Perth (W.A.) perti-
nently referred to the absurdity of the Australian
Anglican clergy calling themselves “ priests and
deacons of the Church of England,” although
when they went to England they were not recog-
nised as such without passing an additional exami-
nation. Even the Australian churches are catch-
ing a faint reflection of national spirit.
**. * *
In the matter of reports of the Aus-
tralian delegates’ speeches, the British papers
apparently don’t yet think Australian Common-
wealth affairs interesting to their readers; they
invariably reserved their space for the Imperial
leaders who spoke on the same occasions. At one
function, held specially to give Barton an oppor-
tunity of putting forward certain views, the
speech of John Morley, the chairman, was purely
introductory to Barton’s, and he “apologised for
standing between them and the principal speaker
of the evening” (Barton). Though all the lime-
light was turned on Barton, who is said to have
made a fine speech, the press next morning gave
Morley a column and a half and Barton exactly
13 lines !! ! After all, though Australians are
overwhelmed with kindness and hospitality in

Rights.”

England, they are always put in the moral posi-
tion of country cousins. Hence Copeland’s tears
and letter to the Times.
* * * *
Premier Lyne’s defence in N.S.W. Assem-
bly to the attack re the appointment of the
“ assistant paymasters 55 to the China Contingent
was lamentably weak. He said there was a pre-
cedent : when the Imperial Bushmen were
despatched to S’Africa the ’Ome people requested
the Govt, to send a clerk to act as paymaster, &c.
Consequently an officer from N.S.W. Treasury
was selected to go. Well, the Bushmen numbered
2,000 and if one man could do the work of that
crowd it is nonsense to say that the appointment
of two assistant-paymasters for under 500 men is
justified by the case. And while the two persons
selected may be excellent journalists there is no
reason for Premier Lyne to suppose that they can
do the work for which on the first occasion he
selected a Treasury clerk as likely to possess the
necessary qualifications.
* * * *
Quaint answer of a London Missionary
Society representative to the query whether his
society would send out missionaries to replace
those dead in China : “ Will merchants send out
cotton goods again?” Shoddy religion and
shoddy clothes; the analogy is perfect.
* * * *
The dreaded examinations, introduced by
N.S.W. Public Service Board with such a flourish
of trumpets, are likely to be abandoned. In reply
to a question in the Leg. Assembly, Attorney-
General Wise announced that the Board did not
bind itself to make the examinations an
annual institution, but only to hold them “when
occasion demanded.” So that the majority of the
luckless clerks who failed in the solitary one held
in|lB99 will have to remain unpromoted until the
Board think fit to hold another. But here is an
instance of the weight the Board attaches to
its own examinations. The regulations pro-
vide that the clerks in the service shall
(provided that everything is well with them)
advance by yearly increments of £lO each. All
those, however, on £3OO, £250, £2OO, and £l5O
cannot get any further, but in the last three cases
must pass the examination for the next grade. In
one of the sub-departments under the Chief
Secretary four of the staff sat for the examination.
Three passed and were granted the regulation
increment of £lO. The fourth man failed, and,
appealing to the Board for further consideration,
was immediately granted an increase of £5O a
year ! He is thus better off than his three col-
leagues who were unlucky enough to pass the test
prescribed by the Board.
* * * *
The true story of the burlesque Bushmen,
who sailed by the Atlantean to S’Africa, is gradu-
ally leaking out. One “trooper,”
a baker by trade, passed no tests
—merely “gave his word that he
could ride and shoot.” . . . “An
officer shouted: ‘ Now then, you
bush-rats, get ready.’ A number
of men grouped together and re-
fused to get ready.” Then a war-
rant-officer ordered the men
“roughly” to wash decks, and
“they told the warrant-officer that
if he wasn’t careful, he’d go over-
board. And he would, too!” No
notice seems to have been taken of
such gross insubordination ; yet
when the baker aforesaid com-
plained about the food, he was
clapped into the guardroom. So
that it was safer to disobey orders
and threaten to murder a warrant-
officer than to make a complaint.
Good old discipline that!
* * •
T. M. Slattery in N.S.W.
Leg. Council and J. C. Neild in the
Assembly move to legitimatise off-
spring of parents subsequently
married, and despite a lot of hen-
opposition are likely to succeed.
N.S.W. follows England in the
brutal law of “once a bastard al-
ways a bastard,” though in Eng-
land the Queen is vested with
powers. which, when the illegiti-
macy is in “noble” circles, can
alleviate the condition materially.
Eor ages, canon and Roman laws
have legitimatised such offspring
when the father and mother entered
into wedlock ; France, Holland and
Germany passed special Acts legiti-
matising children on the after mar-
riage of their parents, and from
time immemorial Scotland has
acted on this principle of fair play
to the innocent. Most States of the
American Union have passed legiti-
matising laws. Despite all this, however, stiff
resistance is anticipated in N.S.W. Parliament,
and. friends of the proposed measure will need to
put forward their best strength.

ParisNesbit, Q.C., considers that whenever
a wretched abortionist kills a girl, the six best-
dressed and most respectable women in the
nearest fashionable church—Anglican preferred-

tralian judges who wear gold-rimmed spectacles.
Now that early closing is the vogue why
do not all Australian Parliaments
run through a measure ordaining
that law-courts, especially in the
trial of criminal cases, shall not
sit after sundown ?

*• * * *
“ Hawkesbury ” : M.L.A. Ferris’s pro-
posal that the proclamation of the Commonwealth
should be made at the old Govt. House, Parra-
matta Park, should be incontinently rejected.
Parramatta smells badly in Marsden-made history,
but that particular old house contracted such an
odor from Charles Augustus Fitzroy that the
succeeding Governor, Denison, refused point-
blank to live in it and no Governor since has slept
a night under its roof.
* * * *
A member of N.S.W. Assembly withheld,
under advice, an important question which he had
ready for the Notice Paper one night lately. The
question referred to the total moneys paid per
annum to N.S.W. Supreme Court Judges, as
salaries, travelling and other allowances while on
circuit, and otherwise. In view of a recent
demand made on the Treasury the question should
be asked.

It is strikingly typical of most of the
characters figuring in Australian democratic his-
tory of the past 30 years that the bitterest feud of
members Lesina and Glassey in Queensland should
pivot on the distribution of Gov. Lamington’s
complimentary banquet tickets. Lamington sent
Glassey 20 tickets for distribution among mem-
bers ; Glassey, alleging in excuse the way the
Laborites treated him, passed them by, and
Lesina, representing the Laborites, rends Glassey.
A political melee, with an ugly moral,

fully printed in Colac (Yic.) Reformer— Heaven

All I wish for is to have about a week or so with the

Noble warrior!
* * * *
The N.S.W. Public-School Teachers’ Jingo
fund closed with £4302 and the presentation of an
address signed by 500 teachers to someone who
took a prominent part in the affair. The Bulle-
tin could easily get 1500 teachers to privately
sign an address cursing the Minister who did not
interfere to prevent the teachers from being
politely but most effectually squeezed. “Why,”
asks a correspondent, “ were not we, the teachers,
allowed to contribute, or decline to contribute, to
the general fund raised by the public at large
(though many of them oughtn’t to be at large),
instead of being in reality forced to pay toll to the
self-importance of a greasy and obsequious bureau-
cracy ? ” The reasons which prompted signatures
to the address in question were no doubt exactly
the same as those which prompted subscriptions
to the teachers’ Jingo fund.
♦ * * *
On the Manning River (N.S.W.) the
Chinese threaten to become bosses of the vegetable
industry. They are adding to their holdings or
taking up fresh areas day by day and regularly
advertise for white laborers. Within a stone-
throw of one another ten white men may be
counted serving Chows for Bs. per week and
tucker.
* * * *
S.A. law met with a really tough problem
the other day. Seaman Charles White left
his ship, it was said with the inten-
tion of deserting, but ere he could
be long enough away to prove
that he didn’t intend going back,
he was arrested for stealing the
chief officer’s clothes, and sen-
tenced to a fine of £1 or 14
days. He took the 14 days,
chiefly, it appears, with a
view to the fact that his ship
would be away before he
came out, so that he would
get quit of it without any
unpleasant penalties for
desertion. But shortly
before the vessel sailed
the captain sent up the
£1 and demanded the
return of Ohas. White.
Next day, as Charles
hadn’t returned, he
laid information
against him as a de-
serter, and then he
ascertained to his
surprise that
White was still in
gaol, and flatly re-
fused to come out,
White’s plea was
that he, not the
captain, had been
fined. That he had
the legal option to
pay £1 personally or
do 14 days person-
ally, and that having
chosen, as a free Brit-
ish citizen, to do the 14
days, his right to do 14
days could not be torn
from him by the inter-
ference of any outsider.
Also, that he couldn’t be
a deserter when the law
had given him the option
of £1 fine or 14 days, and
he was absent from his
ship doing the 14 days as
the law ordered. The cap-
tain demanded that White
should be kicked out of gaol, and
have the blessings of freedom conferred
on him by force, so as to constitute him a
deserter if he didn’t return to his ship. The
law replied thatif the Governor remitted White’s
sentence then he could be liberated by main force,
but otherwise it really didn’t know whether drag-
ging White out of gaol when he refused to pay the
fine himself wouldn’t open the way for an action
for damages. While the law was in this para-
lysed state the ship sailed, so seaman Charles
White came out on top. And now the law really
doesn’t know for certain if White hasn’t a good
case against it for false imprisonment, because it
didn’t throw him out when the £1 was tendered.
Able-seaman White has reduced S.A. law to a
state of such imbecility that it doesn’t know
where it are.

Sydney Metropolitan Transit Commission
is now dead and buried. This fate was predicted
for it when John Norton became licensees’ repre-
sentative. Some 20 inspectors will have to be
provided for somewhere.
• * *
“Z.” : Apropos the huge lump of bad debts
in an undertaker’s estate which has just got into
Bankruptcy (8., August 25). Undertakers, like
doctors, habitually allow for a big proportion of
bad debts. Jabez Wright, the much-discussed
Labor Mayor of Broken Hill, and an undertaker
by trade, therefore advocates that undertaking
should be one of the first things “socialised.”
Bad debts, he says, mean that people who can’t
afford to pay have to get the work done (there is
no avoiding that), and so the charges are piled up
on the other clients, whereas the deficit ought to
be borne by the community. In Paris the muni-
cipality does all the burying.
* * * *
Melbourne’s Immigrants’ Aid Society,
which at least feeds them decently and allows
them their liberty, maintained last year in its two
homes nearly 800 inmates at a cost of 3s. Bd. per
head per week. Yet Victoria’s Govt, still persists
in the costly and infamous system of gaoling the
sick and aged poor. Of the 800, 31 are totally
blind, 72 of weak intellect, 71 suffering from
paralysis or epilepsy, and 337 from other chronic
ailments.

The Melbourne Bung brotherhood heh
jubilee t’other day, when a spacious and elegant
suite of offices was formally opened for its use in
the Block Arcade. There was a large assemblage
of people interested in the Fiend, and the atmo-
sphere had a decided smack of “ What’s yours ? ”
The speeches also were all highly laudatory of
Bung as a national and necessary institution. The
L.Y.A., it was stated, was growing in numbers
and in strength, and is soon to become a powerful
political organisation. And then Bung went
home to “break-down” the rum, and bedevil the
beer, and disgruntle the whisky !
Again “ drunks’-luck ! ” Early one recent
morning a very beery mariner fell out of a third-
floor window at the Melb. Sailors’ Home, banged
upon a verandah-roof 70 feet below, and bounced
off to the ground. It was apparently a case for
the morgue, but when they took the “shell” to
the hospital on the ofif-chance, it was found that,
except for a bruise or two, he was none the worse.
The gentleman himself, indeed, was inclined to
think that too much fuss was being made over a
“ bit of a tumble,” and departed from the hospital
upon his own legs.
. * * *
Not many weeks ago two Australian
judges—one of Supreme, t’other of the minor
Bench—settled a little difference of opinion, on a
question of honor, in the good old-fashioned way
with bare fists. Preliminaries were fixed up in a
few minutes at a fashionable club, and the legal
luminaries retired with their seconds to a well-
known private boxing-hall, where they vigorously
pounded one another for 15min. Both were ex-
Eerts, and some blows were beautifully delivered
y the minor judge, who eventually established
his claim to precedence—probably for the first
time in Australian history—by a knock-out stunner
under the Supreme Court jaw. Fact!
* • » •
Four cases of old misers who deprived
themselves of the necessaries of life while pos-
sessed of from £lOOO to £6OOO, have come to light
within the last few weeks—two in Melb. and two
in Adelaide. Novelists tell us that avarice is the
last of the passions to die. The truth is the miser
is the monomaniac whose disease is of gradual
growth. Three out of the four poor creatures
referred to were proved to be lunatics, and the
other one was known as eccentric.
* » * m
Sydney will by-and by have several train-
ways which are sorely needed for summer holiday
purposes: the line to South Head, now in course
of construction; a line right through to Manly,
now almost completed as far as the Spit; and one
from Botany to La Perouse. The through line to
Manly, which has so far been blocked by the
bridging of Middle Harbour, will no doubt be
facilitated by punting the passengers, or perhaps
even the cars. When there is easy land-commu-
nication with Manly the place (and the district
beyond it) will go ahead like a house on fire.

Here is one of the things which-
help to make this life the vale of woe
it is. According to a lengthy Mel-
bourne paragraph, Mr. John
Danks, who was a member of South
Melbourne Municipal Council
from 1871 to 1880, has modestly
presented his own full-length por-
trait in oils to the municipality.
The portrait was painted by the
daughter of the municipal engi-
neer. Cr. Cockbill presided, and
Cr. Mountain proposed the
health of Mr. John Danks in the
presence of Mr. John Danks’s
own oil - painting. Mr. John
Danks feelingly responded. Then
Mr, Aaron Danks proposed the
health of the lady who painted
Mr. John Danks in oils, and later
on somebody else (Mr. John Danks
probably) proposed the health of Cr.
Cockbill. So Mr. John Danks’s
portrait in oils was suspended, to
be a grief to future generations,
and all the Dankses went
home. In this beautiful world
there are seas and lakes with
the moonshine on their waters
waiting for an artist to do them jus-
tice ; there are mountains and val-
leys, and rivers, and cataracts and
forests, and haunts of fern and shadow,
and all the beauties and wonders of
earth and sky and ocean. And yet
there are people who paint ex-Councillor
John Danks in oils ! No doubt they do it
because there is a demand for Danks in oils.
But that isn’t an explanation. It only shifts the
question a step further back. Why, so long as
there is a moonrise to be painted on Dandenong
ranges should there be a demand for Danks in
oils ? Possibly there are people who prefer Danks
,in oils to the great silver moon climbing over
Dandenong. Or possibly these people encourage
Danks in oils so that he may lead to a demand for
themselves (Smith) in oils later on, and with that
view want to create a habit of Danks in oils. It
is all a mystery.
Miss Jane Sutherland, the level-headed
manageress of the Melb. Neglected Children s
Society, gives it as her opinion that “illegitimacy,
crime, prostitution, and misery have not
decreased since the advent of the Salvation
Army.” All those troubles come of weakness and
impecuniosity, and the commercial-minded
Harmy offers little or no “ means of salvation ” to
weak “sisters” who haven’t a penny to bless
themselves with. As for the various self-support-
ing Homes where wretched women (some of whom
have not even “fallen ” in the sexual sense of the
term) are required to wash and pray for 12 months
in return for bare tucker and a scanty supply of
clothing, Miss Sutherland doesn’t think much of
them. Her ideas, for that matter, are the ideas of
everyone who can speak from personal acquaint-
ance with the victims of this wash-tub philan-
thropy. To get a domestic servant from a Home
where religion spells soap-suds is to know that the
released prisoner—for a Home is a prison—would
rather cut her throat than return to the awful
discipline of the Tub.

The injunction granted restraining N.S.W.
Govt, from using Randwick rifle-ranges recalls
that rifle practice in populated neighbourhoods is
risky at best. A peripatetic rifle ball is a thing of
delightful vagaries. A tree, a stone, may change
its course to any angle from the direct line.
Writer has known a spent Martini-Henry bullet —
having struck a near rock —to rebound and fall
behind the shooter.
