* * * *
That old Melb. vagrant discovered cutting
firewood in Fitzroy Gardens while he had £2OO
concealed on his person was adjudged a lunatic
and sent to the asylum. Yet the ancient is the
very essence and embodiment of the great Fatman
virtue—Thrift. People have to be careful in Vic-
toria nowadays. If a person commits suicide
through want of cash he is pronounced insane ; if
he doesn’t die he is run in and gets six months’
gaol, and if he has money and doesn’t spend it he
is again reckoned insane. By the way, the old
miser wasn’t the only one to get his firewood from
the Fitzroy Gardens. Whoever has control of
that beautiful pleasure resort and play-ground for
children and lovers is cutting down his trees
wholesale, and turning the place into a collection
of railed-off bric-a-brac beds and peep-show plots.
Last autumn that magnificent southern avenue of
Moreton Bay figs was utterly spoilt, apparently —
as only the street side of the trees was ruthlessly
hacked away—to save the City Council the trouble
of sweeping the fallen leaves off the pavement.
* * * *
The Bulletin hears that, at Liverpool
(N.S.W.) Benevolent Asylum, where 200 more or
less phthisical patients are walled-in —permission to
allow them fresh air and exercise having been
officially refused (inmates of charitable asylums
have no votes)—the Govt, chief medical officer has
an official public notice dealing with consumption
and its beneficial treatment boldly placarded.
This proclamation insists on fresh air and sunshine
as the best-known possible remedies for the pre-
vention and cure of phthisis. “ Closed gates,
however,” remarks a justly-embittered patient,
‘ ‘ reduce advice so truly excellent to a mocking
burlesque—ever tauntingly proclaiming the vicious
effect of incomprehensible paradoxes. Verily, a
patriotic Govt., which spends scores of thousands
m unwarrantably poking its nose into other
people’s affairs, might spare a few thousands in
endeavoring to prevent, if not to cure, consump-
tion. Phthisis, being so treacherously infectious,
is, after all, more a question for the unafflicted than
the afflicted.”
* * * *
The s.s.. Moana last week took from Syd-
ney, for ’Frisco, £550,000 in gold. Over half-a-
million of money in one bottom ! —and, Sydney to
Maoriland, 1000 miles; M.L. to Samoa, 1200;
Samoa to Honolulu, 2000; Honolulu to ’Frisco,
2500. Nearly 7000 miles of ocean, on an almost-
uniquely solitary track, to tote the huge treasure
over! The chances are enough to evolve a
piratical coup even out of these prosaic and unad-
venturous days ; and, if the Moana should mys-
teriously disappear, many people wouldn’t feel at
all certain that her fate had been either fire or
wreck. Anyhow, the bottom of the sea by this
time holds more gold than the dry land, and it is
surely tempting the Fates to send so much
treasure in one ship.
• . * •
N.S.W. National Council of Women, at its
recent biennial meeting, decided to present as a
petition to Parliament the following resolution :
That this meeting is of opinion that the law as to hours
of closing public-houses and wine shops should be brought
into harmony with that for ordinary shops, especially in
view of the manifold evils connected with the sale of in-
toxicants.
In a word, when unlovely woman (most of the
“National Council” are unlovely) has her way,
poor Man won’t be able to get a drink after
6 p.m. ; he will have to go home to his wife.
Woman always was jealous of Wine.
* * * *
Report by a Board of Health Inspector re
a visit to a Kyneton (Vic.) dairy is summed up in
this picturesque paragraph :
His kitchen was crowded with all sorts of things i.e.,
the general safe, containing meat, bread, butter, cold
vegetables, jam, and other edibles. On a table in one
corner were the remnants of a meal, and dirty crockery ;
on another table was a can of milk, said to be intended
for sale. Beneath this table were two parrots in cages.
A perambulator with a baby in it, and another small
child tending it, were placed between the fireplace and
the milk-table. A bag of potatoes and a lot of stale
cabbage-leaves were lying in another corner. Articles or
old and dirty wearing apparel were hanging from nails all
round the walls.”

Breelong nigger - item from Bathurst
(N.S.W.) National Advocate :
It was shortly after the murderers had commenced
their reign of terror. A number of persons were
in the bar of an hotel, discussing, of course, the topic of
the moment. The police and black trackers were being
severely dealt with, and just about the moment when the
debate was at its highest pitch one of the black-trackeis
entered. He was picked upon immediately, and beca-'a •
the object of many taunts. They fell fast and furio,.:j,
and at length he could stand it no longer. “ Why,” he
said, “could have caught them last night. Tracked
them to a scrub ten minutes after they went into it. Me
and a sergeant. Asked sergeant for rifle, but he said
‘ No.’ Told him to go in himself, and he said ‘ No.’
Asked him again for rifle, and he said 1 No.’ Said he was
sure to get them, and he said ‘ No.’ Asked him to go in
w 7 ith me—him first —and he said ‘ No.’ He asked me to
go in first—l said ‘ No.’ Pulled out his watch then, said
‘ Time to get back to head-quarters now ; let’s go.’ We
went; so did the blacks.”

A Vic. Jewish Rabbi preached recently
in the Synagogue on the subject of the recent
deaths of the King of Italy, the Duke of Saxe-
Coburg, and Mrs. ,of Fitzroy ! With fine,
unconscious humour the rev. gentleman said,
“ The loss in each case is irreparable.”

At a “veteran” social, held recently in
Melb., two whilom warriors were. mentioned as
having won the V.C. in Maoriland in 1863. This
was another case of confounding the Y.C. with
the quite distinct cross locally issued at the time
referred to.
BEING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I sit on the pack at the foot of a tree,
And a bird in a bush is looking at me.
His mate in the tree has a grub in his hill,
And the young in the nest are singing out still.
And all the world over the lesson’s the same.
Their lives are the stakes and they’re “playing the game. 1 *
I sit on the pack at the foot of a tree,
And the bird in the bush is sorry for me.

"LEONIDAS."
A FAITHFUL WOMAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Wonder not that I am moved
Thus to leave you lonely;
I an epicure in Love
Claim its freshness only.
To my high ideal firm,
Not to any wooer.
Seek I now a Love as true
As yours is,—and newer.
Let the world misunderstand,
Call me trifler,—natheless
Who would faithful be to Love
Must to Man be faithless.
CONSTANUE OLYDE.
Withdrawal of “The Times” Offer.
In accordance with arrangements made by The
Times and by Messrs. A. and 0. Black, original
publishers of the “ Encyclopaedia Britannica,”
The Times Reprint of the Ninth Edition has, for
some time, been sold by The Times at a reduction
of 55 per cent, from the price originally named by
the publishers.
These arrangements will, on September 15
(eight weeks from the day on which The Times
Reprint was first offered to the public), cease to be
operative ; and the sale in New South Wales will
be discontinued on that day. This early intima-
tion is given so that readers who desire to procure
a copy of the “Encyclopaedia” on the present
terms may send in their, orders promptly. It will
be impossible to accept belated applications, and
if more orders are received than can be filled,
those which first reach The Times office will have
priority.
The results of the plan of sale adopted by The
Times have been gratifying in the highest degree.
Apart from the advantages presented by the
great reduction in price and by the system of
monthly payments, a larger usefulness was given
to the “Encyclopaedia Britannica” by the mere
fact of the broad publicity which has been given
to the issue of The Times Reprint. The “En-
cyclopaedia ” was, of course, already known every-
where the English language is spoken, and every-
where recognised as an authority.
This constant usefulness of the “ Encyclopaedia
Britannica ” has been brought home to the public
in the course of the last seven weeks. The subject
has been in the air; everyone who bought a copy
of the “Encyclopaedia ” has talked about it to his
friends; and the volumes have been used and
quoted more than ever before. It may even be
contended that this increased appreciation of the
work has done more to promote its sale than the
reduction of the price and the new facilities
offered to those who desire to procure it with the
least possible effort.
Those who would avail themselves of The Times
offer before it is too late should do so at once, as
we understand that while the sale is to remain
open another week, there is a probability that the
popular half-Morocco binding will be sold out
before that date. In this case late subscribers
will be compelled to take the higher-priced full-
Morocco or the cheaper Cloth binding.

to the revel of female contour, may be said to

A WOMANS LETTER.
Sydney, September 4, 1900.
Mr Dear Mtee,—
The atmosphere of laity, usual to September, is
being so thickened with black clerical vests and
low hats that Sydney looks like redeeming itself
without the ticket, as it were. It is the great
Mission Meeting that rains bishops into Coffee
Palaces in this lavish fashion. But why Coffee
Palaces? I merely ask because an incident has
floated my way that tells how a wild man from the
west got into a lift with a long-lipped clergyman,
the other night, and kept his head turned the
other way, considerately endeavoring to keep the
odor of—er —tobacco from the clergyman’s delicate
nostrils ; and then, at the third floor, the reverend
gentleman gave a whispered order to the lift-boy
—“ Three brandies and sodas, up to my room, / ”
The lift-boy’s explanation was : “Three b’s and
hesses! ’E (as one when ’e gets ’em, and one
when ’e gets into bed, and ’e keeps t’other standin’
on the washstand to look at till the momin’.”
But the beery man from the west, who hadn’t
been to church since his beard began to stiffen,
and still reverenced things, amended, solemnly—
“ Oh no, he’s sick, and his wife’s sick, and he’s got
a sick friend ! ”
Speaking of what Cloth can stand, there was a
procession of Bishops last week at the Town Hall,
each one carrying, in a little black satin bag, the
collection from nis diocese. One lordship stag-
gered under the awful load of £l2OO subscribed by
children, but it wasn’t all in pennies ! But what
do you think of that offertory at St. Andrew’s
last Friday that amounted to £B4OO os. lid. ? I
wouldn’t be surprised in the least if you replied
that you are almost persuaded to be a Christian.
Sydney is certainly raising the wind in a royal
way just now. Look at the Children’s Hospital
subscriptions ! Miss Eadith Walker, Mrs. John
Fraser, and Mr. W. P. Faithfull are giving a thou-
sand pounds each, and money is pouring in from
our business houses.
At the Advertisement Ball, this Thursday, Miss
Walker is to represent Hardy Bros., and on dit
that our heiress will be shadowed by two
detectives —to see that nobody else makes off with
the trimmings. The A.B.C. has donated all the
supper to this same grand ball. But it has its
reward by being represented by Mrs. Forbes
Angus, whose gown, I hear, is going to be some-
thing too wildly beautiful for words. One
Society dame has been inspired into a sedan chair
with four men to carry her round all the evening.
Mrs. “Monty” Severn scores off everybody by
getting Tattersall’s at £lOO. Up till then the
highest price for an advertisement was £5O.
What a lively time business people have had
lately ! One music warehouse had 27 females of
all ages calling for permission to be “your repre-
sentative.” To decide between them all was too
much for the music-shop. It sent a donation,
but declined to be personated. Pretty Miss
Enid Whiting is going as David Jones, Mrs.
Fred. Aronson as Sunlight Soap, Mrs. Harry
Levy as the Sydney Book Club. Big sums are
being paid for all these, so you may imagine
the feelings of a society matron who sailed into
a draperess’s shop the other day, laid down her
proposal, and was calmly told “Well, XXX,
I’ll give you 10 shillings.” This is the lowest offer
on record, and I needn’t add that it was rejected.
“A Night in Bohemia” brought thousands of
people to the Town Hall last Wednesday. His
Ex. came along, with A.D.C. Harris. They sat
at a big table and fondled their cigars, but neither
of them smoked, and the suggestion of an eminent
counsel that they should take the Havanas be-
tween their teeth, and deceive the large and
breathless audience, was not acted on. For my
part, I thought there was something cruel in ask-
ing “ Little Billee” to smoke his first cigar before
three thousand people. Financially, Bohemia was
all there.
“ Ars et Fraternitas” will profit Mr. Nelson
Illingworth to the tune of some £3OO. But, as
always happens, the affair resolved itself into an
ordinary smoke concert, with a few ferocious
items, and some good ones, and one quite top-
lofty—Rignold’s magnificent recitation of the
Forum scene. Leist and Souter lightning-
sketched. The latter threw off a hideous black,
alongside the words “ The Governor,” not adding
“Blacks” till finished. There was a shocked
pause, and in the midst of it His Ex’s clear voice
rang out, “ Charlie, go and ask Mr. Souter if he
will give me that?” And “Charlie” carried it
home under his arm tenderly, and everyone
breathed again.
Trust the Italians to make an artistic and im-
pressive affair of anything they undertake. At
King Humbert’s Funeral Service last Saturday
our Town Hall was turned into a vast black cham-
ber, silver touching the black up lightly, lovingly,
until it became a fitting place for beautiful music
to wander in. Away at the end, a huge painting
of Umberto displayed the fierce white moustaches
of the benevolent king; colored lights burned
dimly all around the portrait. And, presently,
there came stealing out from behind the organ
little fair-haired Italian girls, dressed all in
white, who crept up to the feet of the por-
trait, laid white wreaths there, bent low, and
stole away again. The Englishman, fearing to be
theatrical, never does things that way. He brings
in hordes of public-school boys and girls, who
shriek cantatas out of tune, move through stiff
drill, and are robbed of all childhood’s
grace by neurotic teaohers frowning nervously
from every possible disadvantage - ground.
The music, too, was Italian ; dramatic, mourn-
ful. Hazon led his Amateur Orchestra through
funeral music of Verdi’s, of Faccio’s, and Pon-
chielli’s “Prelude' in the Death of Donizetti.”
Our leading pianist, Signor de Beaupuis, wrote a
special Funeral March, and played it tenderly on
his beloved piano. But when the Italian element
retired a little, and Sydney politicians tookthefloor,
and Harris, Barton, Lyne, and Reid began long
speeches of sympathy and consolation, and eulogy,
and heartfelt feelings, and the crowds clapped, or
didn’t clap, Art broke up suddenly, and the cere-
mony came near attaining the “ pre-eminent in
grief” in the usual crude, colorless “Anglo-
Saxon” way.
Our Free Kindergartens Union had an evening
party at the Australia last week. Singing, music,
refreshments, and plenty of cult. Mrs. Dane, the
secretary, received people in a pink silk gown; and
Miss Buckley, president, wore a beautiful frock of
a flowered silk polonaise over flounces of crepe de
chine. _ These two ladies have worked like Trojans
for their cause, and you will be glad to hear the
Kindergarten is getting a large, firm foothold
wherever there are children in Sydney.
Miss Thea Cowan’s Afternoon Tea on Monday
had for cause Mr. Barton’s bust, which seems to
give general satisfaction. The aesthetic sculptress,
gowned in ultra-clinging pink, had her uneasy
moment when a too-ardent admirer lifted his child
up and said, “ Stroke Mr. Barton, dear.” The
little black kid hand was just descending on the
marble, and long black streaks were already there
in imagination, when someone averted the acci-
dent, and the bust came off with a clean face. It
is being presented to Mrs. Barton by a hundred
friends.
In new music I have only Annie Summerbelle’s
“ Mafeking Waltz,” published by the composer,
who played it at the Society of Artists’ Ball the
other night. Like all this clever young composer’s
work, this has something in it.
The Society of Artists’ has prepared a regular
bomb by way of the next exhibition. Instead of
the usual September show, the young society will
keep its inspirations dark till January, when it
proposes to collaborate with Melbourne, Brisbane,
and Adelaide artists in a great Federal Art Show
that will rank among the festivities of Common-
wealth week. With Lord Hopetoun to open it,
this looks like a brilliant idea.
Not apropos of the above, it makes one sad to
think that the Governor-General’s name begins
with “ H.” Anybody that goes to many meet-
ings will realise the immense debt of gratitude we
owe to Lord Beauchamp for beginning with a

and the “h” that gets into “hexcellency ” but

Apropos the succession of Mr. Ted Samuel to
his deceased father’s baronetcy, the new Lady
Samuel is a plump little lady, fairly well
gilded on her own behalf by her father, Mr.
Abraham Cohen, but otherwise the baronetcy
doesn’t carry much with it.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The Poster Ball (August 28) was big, vulgar,
and terribly overcrowded, so that even from
the gallery of the Town Hall, looking into
the throng below, one could see very little
more than a thickly-jumbled-pack of papier-
mache bottles, cigars, mugs, jugs, and scare
crows mixed up with milkmaids, monks, and
Pear’s Soap inebriates. This last ad. was a
living replica of Harry Furniss’s familiar drawing:
‘Since using your soap I have used no other.’
“ The ball packed the Town Hall and was such
a success that its glorified ghost, in the form
of a ‘Grand Advertisement March,’ was in-
vited to walk the Exhibition Building (Chil-
dren’s Hospital Bazaar), and will have done
so ere this is printed. The Naicest, with
scarcely an exception, and all the nobodies who
got the chance, poster-ed for sweet Charity’s sake
—the advertisers in some cases finding the gorgeous
dresses, cab-fares, ball-tickets, etc., while the
human advts. basked in the admiration of a record
crowd ‘ on the cheap.’
“The most spirited ad. was Yankee Doodle
tobacco. You know those three Bunker Hill
braves, squeaking out ‘ Yankee Doodle’ on drum
and fifes, while quenchless grit and Puritan dog-
gedness shine through the blood and bruises on
their haggard faces.
“S’Rupert Clarke, as a Kandyan chief in a
costly Eastern costume, represented a brand of
tea. His facial make-up had cost him much
anxious thought, and theatrical experts had been
called in to prescribe the exact proportion of
coffee-color and grease-paint. His fascinating
step-mother, in her Argus role, expressed the
‘hundred eyes’ idea by a train of peacocks’
feathers hanging from the shoulders of her white
satin gown. Crests of the same birds perked on
her head and shoulders, while diamonds and
crystals added to the peek-a-boo effect. Supersti-
tious guests who felt shivery at the sight of her ill-
omened plumage wonder if the popular wearer has
always disregarded traditionand harbored the ‘bird
of ill-luck’ within her gates. ‘lf so,’say these
latter-day augurs, ‘it accounts for the many
troubles of the lady’s life.’ This gloomy view of
her career would probably sur; rise Janet Lady
Clarke, who, despite cloudy days, has always
regarded herself as one of Fortune’s favorites. At
small domestic card-parties in bygone years family
friends often heard her say, laughingly, when her
successful play was discussed : ‘ Of course I’m
lucky, or 1 wouldn’t have got such a good hus-
band !’
“The Lieut.-Governor’s prettiest daughter
would soon lose her reputation for beauty if she
often appeared as that tea-poster in Jack Tar
frock, big, red-lined sailor-hat worn on the back
of her head, and scantyish tress-supply hanging
down her back. ’Tis said that the poster-girl m
question, now smirking from every hoarding, was
drawn from Miss Ruby Madden. And there is
a large, hard, shrewd resemblance. An unsus-
pected likeness to the supposed original’s Pa is
disclosed in the picture’s smile —you know his Ex’s
pawky, canny look when, with a neat bit of
blarney to bestow—he surveys the recipient-to-be
with an artful ‘ little-bit-of-sugar-for-the bird ’ air.
“ Miss Leslie Madden advertised a fashionable
drapery-firm, as a chic, little stage modiste with
short skirts, auburn curls and cute little band-
boxes inscribed with her Principal’s name. Mrs.
George Chirnside, in pale-blue satin regal robes,
jewelled crown &c., distributed tea-ads. Col.
Campbell lived and moved and had his being in a
mammoth ale-bottle, only his face and feet show-
ing. He did his duty as an advt., and even danced
the Lancers with Lady Clarke, though she had to
steer and tote him round, as, of course, he could
only see straight ahead. These evidences of con-
scientiousness in the chief Madden aide are here
recorded as so much in his favor. There’s no love
lost on him when his Chief takes him to suburban
bazaars, etc,, where he fusses and hurries stall-
holders up with theory, ‘The Guv’nor’s goin’.’
Meantime his Lord and Master, all sweetness and
light, returns paid-for sweets with the request
that they may be distributed amongst chance
children, etc. Keeping a dog he naturally leaves
it to do the biting.
“A monstrosity at the Poster Show was Some-
body’s food, enacted by a ’Varsity’s prof.’s fatted
son, in short sleeves and baby socks, etc.
“ Widow Clarke’s dress recalled the one in
which Ellen Terry played Lady Macbeth, and in
which Sargeant, R.A., painted a fine portrait of
her. The costume, with its countless greeny-blue
eyes, was certainly very beautiful, and an ex-
cellent symbol of the Argus— all glitter and no
eyesight. The Age missed a chance in not being
represented. The representation would have been
quite easy—just an elderly dyspeptic Scotchman,
sipping hot water and saying ‘ I canna afford it! ’
“A commotion arose when one of the poster
sets was being arranged. One cold, proud
and over-wise virgin refused to take the
partner! allotted to her —the very beau
who is regarded as her probable fiance.
She was reminded she had been told whom to
expect for her partner, but making the sweetly
feminine excuse that she had forgotten, she in-
sisted on being apportioned to another fellow,
much to the chagrin of the beau.
The prayer of the advertiser for a mention of
his Poster Ball contribution is irresistible in this
case. It is so beautifully worded (meaning the
prayer) that I feel bound to remember the affect-
ing spectacle of four ‘ Beef-Eaters ’ who bore the
strange device, ‘Smoke Derby Tobacco.’
“ Quite a relief from the prevailing common-
placeness of theatre play-bills is the dainty little
Booklet programme of the Brighton Yacht Club
‘Smoke Night and Distribution of Prizes.’ A
copy of the B. Y.C. production came my way last
week and set me thinking that the amateur
mariners who edited it must have inhaled a sense
of art along with the odour of brine from the
ocean. One wishes that theatre and concert
managers would go out on the deep and get ideas
for something tasteful in their programmes, which
nowadays seem to be printed for the purpose of
being left in the theatre by departing audiences
and flattened out for re-distribution next night.
By the way, talking of the respectable ‘ get-up ’
of the two books published for the Children’s
Hospital Fund, I said that the pictures of ‘ Bud
and Blossom ’ were to be ‘ worthy of their cesthetic
selector,’ Mr, Josh Lake. The humorous comps,
put ‘ athletic ’ instead of ‘aesthetic,’ and now
people are calling on Mr. Josh—a fragile-looking
person who wears white spats and a thoughtful
stoop—to pull himself together and show them
some feats of strength.
“The Wilkie divorce-case witness from whom
the petitioner has been taking lessons in “social
etiquette,” at 3s. an hour, is~ the same lady who
figured as guide, philosopher and friend to Mrs.
Silas Harding some years ago. She was one of the
victims of the carriage accident which cut short
poor Mrs. Harding’s career just at the time when
that joyful relict of stingy old Silas was bidding
fair to be the most conspicuous entertainer in
Melbourne.
“An interesting social-literary event that is
likely to come off in October, in Melbourne, may
be a Canterbury Pilgrimage Show, in celebration
of the fifth centenary of the death of Chaucer.
There is plenty of room for picturesqueness in such
an affair. If the organisers want a natural
Chaucer-looking man they have only to dig out
Mr. Justice Holroyd, who could look the part
without trying.
“ Over 5000 people in Melb. signed the petition
to Prof. Marshall-Hall asking him to apply for
the Chair of Music and not leave Victoria. The
5000 included the l .ainiest folks in the Southern
metropolis: doctors, barristers, professors, bankers,
merchants, social women, and almost everybody
who counts. The petition is a great black eye for
the bigots and their press backers. People all
recognise that though it is possible to get another
Ormonde professor, it is extremely unlikely that
any money can buy the enthusiasm, public spirit,
and artistic thoroughness which Marshall-Hall
has poured into his work, and which have made
his Conservatorium a really fine National institu-
tion. You can buy talent in the market, but the
something which is more than talent, and which
Marshall-Hall has, is rarely met with.
“At the opening of the Children’s Hospital
Bazaar (Sept. 1), the daintiest pair on view were
Janet Lady Clarke and Miss Ruby Madden—the
former in white silk, overlaid with exquisite white
lace, and lilac tulle toque ; the latter in creamy
silk-voile over white satin, skirt befrilled, bodice
and sleeves of white satin partly veiled by ivory
guipure, and a tiny relief-line of black inserted
down the front. White hat and feathers went
well with the wearer’s blush-rose complexion,
while two pink-white camelias tucked into the
belt matched exactly with the faint-rose lining of
the belle’s white satin reticule.
“ The Lieut.-Governor, as show-opener, poured
forth his usual floods of fluency, picturing Toorak
husbands kept awake o’ nights by their wives’
charitable schemes. Later, his Ex. referred to
said husbands as ‘these interesting men.’ Can
nothing uninteresting come out of Toorakia?
“The bazaar itself is prettier than most, its
stalls not built against the walls but scattered
mushroomlike, with red and white tent-tops. To
the wanderer in boothland, it seemed on Saturday
that the white-aproned busy-bees of the Trades-
Hall, driving their needles to the very last minute,
deserved gubernatorial gush quite as much as the
naice butterflies of the Cafe Chantant.
“From a would-be customer point of view the
tea-room arrargements on opening day led to
nothing but anarchy and vexation of spirit. The
department, accessible only from one side, was
barricaded in front by foliage of the porcupine or
debbil-debbil order. When thirsty invaders
effected a breach, a flushed dowager drove them
back, hissing ‘No thoroughfare ! His Excellency
will pass this way.’ The Governor always sends
two huge policemen and an aide to clear his path,
and, considering his size and the strength of his
body-guard, it seems to matter little if a mob of
thirsty women did get between the wind and his
nobility.
“ Cliveden, the Clarke mansion in E. Mel-
bourne, was gayer last week than it has been
at any time since the Baronet’s death. It was
thrown open for amateur theatrical kickings of
the swagger kind, with bunches of Maddens,
Clarkes, and military fuzzywuzzies in the bill.
They played Pinero’s ‘Schoolmistress.’ The
acting was lamentable, but no more than might
have been expected; and as the object was a
charity, and the people in the audience had mostly
paid an extra twopence a week at school to learn
manners, nobody threw bricks. Mrs. Col. Bing-
ham, who is tall and willowy and graceful, and the
sort of woman men rave about, played Mrs.
Rankling rather well; and Miss Ruby Madden—
growing plump in the family fashion—looked
amazingly pretty in one of the ingenue parts.
Then two colonels, a major, a captain, and a
lieutenant played parts as badly as they knew
how, and everybody said how naice it all was. The
big Cliveden ballroom was full, and looked bril-
liant./ All the folks had their best clothes on, and
Govt. House was there to give it tone.
‘ ‘ It seems pretty well known that a memorable
cablegram addressed to a certain pompous official
and signed ‘Victoria’ was not despatched by Her
Gracious or anyone belonging to the hupper circles,
as recipient fondly supposed, but by one of the
young ladies at Spiers and Pond’s Criterion.
“People who look well in mourning seem
slightly piqued that distant relatives in Pekin are
still absurdly well. Of course they’re not sorry
that the massacre was only imaginary, but they’re
very angry with the newspapers. Missionary
Ethel Halley, like some other supposed martyrs,
is as jolly as ever, and volunteered t’other day to
go to the front with the allied troops, as nurse.
“The crowd that poured into ‘Cliveden’
was met at the door by the youngest
daughter of the house with a monotonous
‘Do you care for a programme? Thruppence,
please ! ’ Men dived into their pockets, whilst
the women searched their gloves for anything
over their tram-fare, and not having provided for
the threepenny emergency many passed pro-
grammeless into the ball-room taking their cloaks
and goloshes with them.
“ When the set of .Lancers started on the stage
an elderly beau sitting in the front row quietly
but heroically shifted his place to the side where
he would not see so much. This to the admira-
tion of all the ‘right-thinking’ women in the
vicinity, for the frilly skirt of one of the three
thoughtless spinsters stuck out like a lamp-shade,
and when she swung—well, the front row was no
place for old gentlemen.
“Lieut. Crespin, aninfantile-visagedGussie, with
short curls and an ‘air,’ was the lucky individual
who had to make love to glowing Ruby. The pair
fell asleep with heads nestling close together, and
woke up to clasp hands and dote on one another.
The beauteous one took kindly to the nestling
business, but otherwise she was an unhappy
amateur, given to pulling her frock down and
running after the more lively Leslie, whose streak
of grey hair in her brown tresses showed out with
quaint effect.
“Janet Lady Clarke enjoys playing fairy god-
mother to her kinsfolk, and the little cousin, now
her daughter-in-law-elect, has from her youth up
paid periodic visits to Cliveden, whose mistress is
a Cotton on her mother’s side. The original
Cotton, an English gentleman in whose life there
was much romance, is remembered hereabouts as
the owner of a house of tame birds and pet animals.
“Since Melba’s warning blast, the Cliveden
Dowager is bound to draw in her horns and lend
a less flattering ear to the aspirants who daily
flock to the gun-girt portals for sympathetic aid
and recognition. Lady Clarke has helped many
m their musical careers, cheered and encouraged a
legion of others, and still they come. But the
widow is alive to her ‘responsibilities,’ and
may now dissuade a few from going ’Ome to swell
the army of impecunious Australian nightingales.
ours affectionately,

The old difficulty of insuring valuables against
fire and burglars has come up again. It is, however,
becoming an acknowledged fact that it is impossible to
arrange a satisfactory insurance. The Sydney Safe De-
posit has overcome the difficulty by offering to secure to
insurers the articles themselves in their fire and burglar
proof vaults, and thus, by the payment of a comparatively
small premium in the way of rent, avoiding all possibility
of loss. The system is most interesting, and the manage-
ment invite a' call for inspection. The Safe Deposit is-
situated at the hack of Paling’s, off George-street.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The Historic Record Society of Ballarat
(Vic.) has secured the wig and gown of Peter
Lalor, worn when Speaker of Vic. Leg. Assembly
from July, 1880 to Sept., 1887.
Recently “ passed-in ” at Kurrajong,
N.S.W., William John, native of Pennant Hills
in same province—aged a trifle over the century,
llis youngest offspring is over 60.
Noteworthy that Vabien Solomon, in
giving notice of his no-confidence motion in S.A.
Assembly said, as reported, that “he not
counted noses before giving notice of motion.
About the most isolated Australian is Mr.
Jardine, who, with his Samoan wife, dwells on
the tip of Australia, at Cape York, with a snug
homestead, four hours’ sail from any other place.
Three Q.C.’s talked of as affording a
probable successor to Chief-Justice Harley on the
Supreme Court Bench read with theC.J. when
studying for the Bar—Pilcher, O’Connor and
W ant.
Bishop Green, now bound for Ballarat, is
the first C. of E. man ordained deacon, priest and
Bishop in Australia to take charge of a diocese in
Victoria. Dr. Green is an Englishman, but an
LL.D. of Sydney University.
Prof. Baldwin Spencer, whose book
(written with the aid of Gillen, of S.A.) has been
so successful, is likely to go for a sojourn amongst
the blacks of the Northern Territory, if he can
get leave from Melb. University.
N.S.W. Leg. Council has knocked out of
the Sydney Corporation Amendment Bill the
clause empowering the ratepayers to elect the
Mayor. If it hadn’t, John Norton would almost
to a certainty have been elected Mayor.
Died, the other day, at Singleton, N.S.W.,
Richard Hooker, said to be born at Canterbury,
Eng., in 1800. Hooker brought to Australia, in
’39, a wife and four children, and leaves 200
descendants. That’s the kind of man to give a
statue to.
Saul Samuel was the last surviving mem-
ber of the first Forster Ministry (1859) and of the
Cowper Ministry of ’65. With J. E. Salomons he
shared the honor of being the last survivor of the
Robertson Govt, of 1868 and of the final Cowper
Ministry, 1870.
Press censorship was mooted in connection
with the army put out to circumvent the Breelong
blacks. It was insinuated that Jimmy and Joe
Governor were regular subscribers to the Sydney
dailies, and had even lodged complaints against
defective postal delivery.
Quite recently N.S.W. Chief Secretary
John See was initiated an hon. member of an
Odd Fellows’ Lodge, and last week, as a sort of
counter make-weight, N.S.W. Opposition leader
G. H. Reid was initiated as a member of the
Strangers’ Refuge Lodge.
Died recently at Prospect (N.S.W.), John
Booth, the noted cattle-breeder. Durhams were
his hobby ; his stock originating in animals con-
signed to his father on behalf of George IV. His
attention to his herds had its outcome in the
numerous prizes he obtained.
Svdney Debating Club has decided “That
the Right Hon. G. H. R,eid, P.C., M.L. A., should
be the first Prime Minister of the Common-
wealth.” The fact that Mr. Reid is a graduate
and was for long a bright star of Sydney Debating
Society aforesaid possibly accounts for the milk in
the cocoanut.
The resignation of his seat in N.S.W.
Assembly by W. W. (“Baldy”) Davis, member
for Bourke, crushes the spirits of a small bunch of
members who took the bushman’s yarns as the
essence of original humor. The garrulous back-
blocker did little else to establish a memory in
Macquarie-street.
The latest rumor with regard to the
aydney Harbour Trust presidentship is that the
Lyne Govt, is not “game” to go on with its
alleged promise to Varney Parkes, and intends to
throw him over. The favorite tip for the position
is Commissioner-for-Works R. P- Hickson, one of
the best of N.S.W. public officials.
“To be Captain (of the N.S.W. Lancers)
on probation: Philip Charley.” How - many
remember that Capt. Charley, now a N.b. W.
squire, was the knockabout lad on Mt.
Gipps station who got hold of a full fourteenth
share in the original Broken Hill (the mine forms
part of Mt. Gipps) and hung on to it all'<
Edward Levien Samuel, son of the late
Saul, entered into his baronetcy unawares. He
was a juror in a Sydney Court case when news of
his father’s death came through, but the Judge
suppressed it, the legal theory being that a juror’s
mind must not be disturbed. After being locked
up two nights the juror emerged Sir Edward, Bart.
Paris Nesbit Q.C., the eccentric lawyer-
poet who is so prominent in Adelaide just now, is
suing Dr. Ramsay Smith for heavy damages for
signing the medical certificate which consigned
him to the Asylum two years ago. The action
will furnish much excitement, as nearly all the
prominent citizens, including the three Judges of
the Supreme Court, have been subpoenaed to give
evidence.
Thus Paris Nesbit, Q.C., in his new
paper: —
Has any one noticed, what has been apparent to me for
years, how Kingston would pass for a reincarnation of the
Apostle Peter? All his virtues and all his faults —violent,
passionate, choleric, full of strange oaths, and bearded
like the pard—look at his jowl and those enormous
shoulders. No weary Titan this, but a demi-god, capable
of carrying on his bowed Atlantean back not only the
Church, but the World to boot.
Lord Stanmore, a younger son of the Earl
of Aberdeen, and the last peer created by Glad-
stone (whom he accompanied to theTonian Islands
in ’5B), took a lively interest in the passage of the
Commonwealth Bill in the House of Lords. As
Governor of Fiji, Maoriland, and Ceylon (when
Sir Arthur Gordon), he was a strong Exeter Hall
man, and a great stickler for the rights of native
races. Being cold and secretive also, he was not
fipular, though he considered he ought to be.
he mention of his name still brings swear-words
to the lips of the old Fiji planters ; and for a long
period he was not on speaking terms with his
constitutional advisers in M.L., whose method of
exploiting the subject race did not meet with his
approval.
Murphy, the Ballarat
M.L.A., who passed into the
“ Upper House,” t’other day,
was the first man sent into
Vic. Assembly by female in-
fluence. His wife was given
credit for hustling him into Par-
liament, but Murphy was prac-
tically returned on the day
when a great picnic from Bal-
larat was stuck up in Geelong
station, and the genial Irishman
chased himself all over the place
with liquid refreshments for the
women and childer. It was a
terrible brickfielder of a day,
and the youngsters and their
mothers were utterly exhausted.
The sitting member hustled for
himself, Murphy hustled for the
ladies, and literally irrigated
the train with tea and lemon-
ade, with the result that he
awoke next day to find himself
famous in Ballarat as a true gal-
lant and a philanthropist. After
that, election was easy.
The biggest man in Q.
Leg. Assembly is “ Bill ” Hamil-
ton, 6ft. 3in., and stout at that. Fittingly, he
represents the Gregory, biggest electorate in the
North, stretching from Winton to the other side
of Sundown.
Apropos the death of the Duke of Edin-
burgh, there was for five years in the window of a
humorless Melb. tailor a frame with the device,
“England’s greatest admirals.” Drake, Blake,
Nelson and Hawke were in the frame surrounding
a portrait of no less eminent a seaman than Prince
Alfred himself—the intrepid salt whose only
memorable exploit on the water was to wreck a
warship worth perhaps half-a-million.
Sydney D.T.'s London correspondent tells
this:—
Of Lord Hopetoun there are few stories in circulation,
but, maybe, the following concerning his father will be
new to you. The late lord was one of the shyest of men,
and when the future Governor-General made his debut:
on earth his pater was conducted by the nurse to see the
son and heir. On Lady Hopetoun inquiring of the nurse
whether her husband had kissed the baby, she received
the following reply, “ ’ls lordship, mum, shook ’ands with
Lord ’Ope.”
JOHN ROBERTS.
The veteran Miss Spence delivered a lec-
ture on her voting system to about 20 S. A. M. sP.
recently. Brooker, Govt, whip, who opposes
Hare-Spence ideas, remarked that present condi-
tions had always been good enough for him. “ But
the final results are so unsatisfactory,” said Miss
Spence. “Not to me,” said Brooker, “Ive
always been satisfied.” “Perhaps so, was the
quiet rejoinder ; “I was thinking of your consti-
tuents.” Collapse of Brooker 1
Alex. Hutchinson, when head of N.S W.
Cold-tea party, was invited to Bega to open an
1.0.G.T. lodge. He did, and the lodge was very
numerously joined. But when the Grand Chief
Templar was again invited to pay a visit he was
staggered as one grizzled farmer arose ana
asked if it was a breaking of the pledge to drink
rum-and-milk in the morning. “Of course, it is,
said Hutchison. “Then, was the reply, we
have all brokeu it ! ” And thereafter that lodge
rapidly petered-out.
ON HIS DAILY PARADE IN TOPHET.
OLDMAIDUS : “ Now, girls, no smiles, he’s a woman-hater.”
One of the oldest of Australian mission-
aries among the aborigines, excepting the R.C.
community in the West, died lately in S.A. —
pastor Rechner. He worked first somewhere near
Kopperamanna, in Centralia, and afterwards pro-
moted a mission in S.W. Queensland. For some
years past his chief work has been the translation
of bits of the Bible into aboriginal dialect—a
tough job to which the Lutherans have given
more attention than any other sect.
Centralian papers lately announced the
death of Donald Coutts Gordon, original owner of
what is now the Mt. Morgan Co.’s freehold pro-
perty at Mt. Morgan. Gordon was G 9 when he
died, and had lived 50 years of that time in
Queensland. He seems to have died very poor.
A bachelor—hence no anxious youngsters en-
quiring eternally about the dividends poor Donald
Coutts never had. One wonders did he repine
very much when he considered all things.
The death of the Duke of Edinburgh
recalls an incident of his first visit. At Melbourne
public library, an address signed by old colonists
who had arrived prior to 1846, was presented by
one of the Hentys. The pure merinoes en-
deavored to ignore John Pascoe Fawkner because
his father was a V.D. Land first-fleeter, but the
little old man with the skull-cap pushed through
the crowd and introduced himself to the Duke
who warmly shook his hand.
R. Barr-Smith, who has lately followed up
his big benefactions with a £lO,OOO gift to Adelaide
C. of E. Cathedral, is generally bracketed with
J. H. Angas as S. Australia’s rich man. Both of
them, as well as half-a-dozen of Adelaide’s best-
known merchants —the Goodes, Fowlers, and
others —are large givers and philanthropic people ;
and this has had more than anything else to do
with the fact that there has never been any really
fiery anti-capitalist party in the-province.
Historic St. Philip's, Sydney, solemnly
chimed the “Old Hundredth,” a few days ago, to
celebrate the anniversary of the birth of the late
Charles Moore, M.L.C. Who was Charles
Moore ? people asked. It seems that that medi-
ocre politican bequeathed a sum of money to have
his memory chimed annually down the future, and
the contract must be fulfilled. Moore never said
or did anything that the world wants to know
about, and now when he’s dead he makes a hideous
noise. ir
Sir Robert Hart, Commissioner of Chinese
Imperial Customs, who was among the slain in
that hideous massacre at Pekin which never came
off, when the infuriated Chinese stripped and
hacked to pieces the European women who are
still alive, is a quiet, middle-aged and harmless
gentleman who uses a big blue-and-white tea-cup
which holds a quart. Also, he potters about in
slippers among his flowers in the mornings, and
smokes a long pipe with a china bowl, and is fond
of children and dogs.
Paris Nesbit, Q.C., launched his new
paper (Morning) at Adelaide on Aug. 29, and
such was the general curiosity in respect to it that
the whole issue of 5000 was bought up the same day,
and at night as much as a shilling was paid for
copies published at a, penny. The paper is filled
with Nesbit from beginning to end, for the anony-
mous poem which has attracted so much attention
was written by Paris Nesbit’s cousin, E. Nesbit
(Mrs. Hubert Bland) —authoress, by the way, of
“ The Ballad of Splendid Silence.”
When Editor Bonython (S.A.) was visiting
Cornwall, in July, he met one James Carne, the
parish clerk of St. Columb Minor, who is 94 years
old, and has held the position for 54 years. His
father filled the same office for 50 years and his
grandfather did the same before him for more
than 50 years. So that the three James Carnes
carried the record back in uninterrupted succession
to 1740. This record can rarely have been
paralleled. The Queen was born a year before
George 111. died, and their two lives also stretch
back to 1740, but that is not quite the same thing.
Samuel Toinkiuson, M.L.C., who died last
week, was the most unbending Tory S.A. politics
have seen. Although 84 years old, he was in active
political harness up to within a month of his
death. “Sammy,” 50 years ago, worked with
Cobden and Bright in the anti-Corn Law move-
ment. He had at different times opposed almost
every progressive idea, and yet was so transpar-
ently honest that he died without an enemy.
Three years ago, when 81, he contested and won
the big southern constituency of S. A. Leg. Coun-
cil, and addressed meetings at every polling place.
Ex-Tasmanian Treasurer, JohnF. Packer,
recently deceased at Sydney, used, in the early
50’s, to ride the skinniest horse that ever pre-
tended to be alive. Street urchins referred to the
noble animal as “Ribs!” and used to ask its
owner if he fed it on sharps. Packer came of a
musical family, but was wedged into the Civil
Service by Gov. Sir Win. Denison, and stuck like
wax to his billet. A quiet man, of domestic tastes
and small ambitions, he was just cut out- for the
life he led. His uncle was Packer, the comuoser,
who also died in Sydney years ago —half a degree
from being a genius, and badly afflicted with
elephantiasis.

You couldn’t count them on all your fingers and

there’s G.H.R. the imposer of Protectionist
duties. Likewise, we have G.H.R. the Federalist,
the anti-Federalist, the mob-orator, the Union -

servative, the man of surpluses, the originator of
deficits, the financial reformer, the spend-as-you-
pleaser, the sweet-tempered, the irritable, the
joker, the tragedian, the vigilant, the stiorer, the
simple, the cunning, the Jingo, the anti-lin-
perialist. And so on ad infinitum.
Geo. Hutchison, M. H R., of M.L., has
great expectations of the future for British
lawyers in Johannesburg, and has shown his
faith to the extent of giving up his M.L. practice
and practically selling out of all his interests
there including, no doubt, _ the historical

resigned. Among the Govt, candidates,! the

life’s little ironies were the brother of the late
editor Gillon, of Wellington Evening Post, to sit
in Parliament as a supporter of Premier Seddon r
against whom deceased levelled some of his
bitterest shafts.

Dr. Nicholson, Sydney’s old medico-
politician, is ranked by the English press as the
oldest doctor-statesman now in Britain. His
M.D. degree goes back to the early days of
William IV., and he was Speaker of N.S.W. Par-
liament nearly 60 years ago ! Dividing his life
into three parts, it is found that the first was
spent in England, the second in Australia, and
the third (which he still enjoys) in England again.

Aboriginalities.
The other day some wag, signing “E. H.
Thompson, F.R.C.5.,” worked off the following
lovely piece of information upon the guileless
new-chum who runs the kennel-column of S.M.
Herald
Perhaps you have not heard of the wild dogs of Central
Australia. In the course of my wanderings in the cause
of geographical knowledge I frequently heard of them
from the blacks, and on one occasion had the good for-
tune to locate their “ village.” They are a social animal,
living in burrows like the prairie dogs of America, and
associating in communities of several hundreds. . . .
They are uniform in colour, the males being black with
white ticks, and the females of a grey cast with similar
marking to the males. These colours, it is interesting to
note, are identical with those of our native cats. . . .
Owing to the remoteness of the place and the fierceness
of the natives, our party did not stay long enough to
enable me to learn much of the habits of these curious
and little-known animals ; but from the few specimens
which fell to our rifles I found the females to be endowed
with the pouch which is so conspicuously absent from the
dingo. Here, then, I submit, is the real Australian dog,
the indigenous product of the country. From my note-
book I am enabled to state, for the information of the
curious, that these burrowing dogs are about the size of a
spaniel, pricked-eared, and their coat resembles fur rather
than hair, and, as already remarked, is identical with that
of the native cat in colour and texture.
And nobody on Granny’s staff seems to have
“ tumbled.”

“N. 1.” (Tas.): A few of “Old Con.
Dineen’s ” experiences in mining will take some
beating. On aW. Coast (Tas.) mining township
Con. was working in a shaft. A lath fell from the
brace and killed his mate. In another shaft a
cleat dropped and broke his mate’s arm just before
the relieving-shift came on, one of whom was
killed a few minutes after starting work. Con.
was repairing a tramway one windy day, and a
tree fell where he was working, but a friendly
fork just fitted over him, and he escaped unhurt.
Again, Con. and his mate were being lowered into
a shaft on the bucket, which was full of drills, and
about 10ft. from bottom they struck foul air.
Mate climbed the rope to the next level to get at
the signal-line, which was in another compart-
ment. And about lOmin. later they landed at
the surface, Con. with his legs jammed in amongst
the drills and his body hanging down the shaft
outside the bucket, while the mate stood on his
legs to make sure of him. He had been overcome
and had thus travelled to the top, 300 ft., escaping
with a few scratches and no ill-effects from the
foul air. He couldn’t get a job in that township
at last, because he was considered the very genius
of bad luck. He was in W. A. when last heard of

“ Vril”: Just returned from a trip through
C.Q. The stock-losses are indeed an eye-opener.
“Will you lose 33 per cent, right through the dis-
trict ? ” I asked a wool-king, who has a property
close to Win ton. “Shall we save 33 per cent.,
even if rain falls now?” was his reply. Even
east of the Dividing Range, close to the coast,
where grass is fairly abundant, the fatal spear-
grass is at work decimating the exiled western
flocks. Noticed in a slaughtered sheep the seed,
not only up into the fleshy parts, but actually
penetrating the liver.
“ S.M.” : Be “A.H.J.’s” inquiry
(B. 22/7/’00) as to snakes’ power of remain-
ing under water, I am going to make some
tall but probable statements. A snake can
live entirely without air from the outside.
You can lug one up in a short length of
gas-pipe, and at the end of two weeks, if
you warm the pipe, you will find the snake
lively enougli when let out. Also, a snake
will not choke, although it may take hours
to swallow its prey ; the reason being that
when the snake is in the act of swallowing
a slit in the windpipe opens, so as to allow
the lungs to draw air from the stomach.
The edges of this slit are serrated, and so
closely fitted as to escape any but the
minutest observation. I have seen snakes
so flattened out that I have considered
them dead, but as soon as they inflated a
little they were very much alive. The hiss-
ing of a snake is caused by the escape of
air from this slit under excitement. I
have ploughed up snakes in winter with
their mouths so glued up with mucus that
they could not open them for some time ;
so, to my mind, they lay in an air-supply
before hibernating—and are, through tha
same means, able to exist a long time under
water. I have also a notion that it is while
the snake is in winter quarters the poison is
secreted from the foul air within the reptile.
Snake-poisoned persons and animals, at all
events, present the appearances of having
been killed by foul air.

“ Creeve Roe ” : Re “ Animal
Chivalry.” I met some time ago this
curious statement —made by Prof. Raco-
vitza, an eminent Roman naturalist: “ The
octopus does not behave brutally in its love
affairs. A certain delicacy is shown by the
male towards his companion in the course
of their courteous flirtation.” Fancy the
courteous and comely octopus going
a-wooing ! What a subject for a humorist
artist ! And what a theme for a “Lewis Carroll”
if he were alive ! Also what a lesson in love-
making to the rude human Romeos of the present
degenerate days 1

“ Yril ” : On Glengallan, Q., once saw a
pair of eagle-hawks coursing a hare ; one hawk
flying a bit behind and the other wide. Presently
No. 1 hawk—the nearest to the hare —made a dash
and passed the quarry ; and, while No. 2 pressed
the hare from behind, No. 1 suddenly flashed back
and nailed bewildered puss before she knew what
was happening.

Johnnie was 16 years old—the detestation
of the station and the pride of his mother. Since
his return from Sydney he had been fired with
the ambition to make his mark as aped. He was
convinced that he possessed more than ordinary
“toe,” and the flattery of the station hands, who
wanted to be “in” with the boss’s son, made his
assurance doubly sure. Sports were coming off at
the township—a big prize was offered for the
100yds., and money was freely wagered on the
event. Johnnie began training—on the quiet,
because he meant to make much money on him-
self, and did not wish anyone to share his good
thing. But he found he needed a confidant; he
Avan ted assistance in training, his time taken, and
other things. Who so suitable as his mother ?
The lady entered heart and soul into the game,
and in a week’s time was as well up in the mys-
teries of training, times, records, and all the jar-
gon of the pro.-ped. as any store-boy in the
township. J ohnnie had fixed a good track, and
worked regularly under his mother’s fond eye.
When it came to taking his time, however, a
difficulty arose. In her excitement the old lady
dropped Johnnie’s watch and broke it, and there
was not another timekeeper on the station which
would register seconds. J ohnnie was in despair,
but his ma found a way out of the difficulty.
From her youth up, she said, she had been noted
for her ability to mark seconds, tapping one hand
against the other and counting aloud. She had
often beep tested against a watch, and had always
been successful. So she said, at all events.
THE BOXERS.—OVERHEARD IN SYDNEY BOTANIC GARDENS.
HE: “ Look , Mary, these must be the chaps that’s kicking up the row in China.”
Johnnie, dubious at first, allowed himself to be
convinced. Daybreak saw them on the track ; the
mater in command. She placed Johnnie on the
mark, and went to the winning-post. “ Only one
word, Johnnie, remember. Are you quite
ready?” “Yes.” “Off! One—two—the dear
fellow, how beautifully he runs—three —just like
his dear father —four—five—keep it up, Johnnie
—six —seven —Oh, Johnnie ! —O-o-o-h, Johnnie !
Well done ! We-e-e-11 done ! —eight, nine—Oh,
Johnnie, you’ve broken the record ! ” Johnnie
thought he had, too, and plunged. His mother
plunged with him. He is now sadder and wiser.
His mother cannot understand it. Neither can
J ohnnie.

“ H.S.” : The South African eland (the
largest of the antelopes), which it is proposed to
acclimatise in Australia, has a wither-height, in
the male, of 16, 17, and even 18 hands; and a
well-grown and good-conditioned beast weighs
about a ton. The bovine affinities of the eland
are very obvious, and its flesh has the reputation
of being superior to that of the best Highland
cattle. Attempts made to domesticate the eland
in Europe have not been commercially successful,
the cost of the meat being too high, but the ani-
mal should do well in Australia, or, failing this, it
would be an ornament to any park. The eland’s
color ranges from grey-blue to dun, and both sexes
have horns, those of the cow being longer and
thinner than those of the bull, whicli has a short
mane. If it is true, as asserted, that the eland
breeds only once in two years, the fact would be
greatly against its commercial value as a beef-pro-
ducer.

“ Via ” : Be ghosts (B. 21/7/00). I “laid ”
one in N.Q. a while back. Years previously an
aged dame had hanged herself in a house which
thenceforth gave out weird sounds at dead of
night. The place, a lonely one, got therefore an
evil reputation, and the local blacks couldn’t be
coaxed within cooee of it. I, having made a pot-
valiant wager to clear the thing up, loaded my
revolver, let it be known I would pot anyone tom-
fooling around, and waited (in the dark, as per
wager) for the moans. They came, right enough,
about 1 a.m. as near as I could'judge, and I got a
nasty feeling down the spine. Three or four low
moans or groans and then complete silence for
the night. I had a sickly time until daylight.
Next night I had to go again—to win—and would
have “ chucked it ” but for the look of the thing.
I got through second night (same noises) and then
explored in daylight. Found that the ridge-
capping of gal. iron and eaves had “sprung ” from
the wooden-supports so that when the iron cooled
and contracted in the night it slipped back and
both timber and iron complained bitterly. I fixed
that spectre with a few 3in. nails, but the grateful
owner |tried to get me run-in for trespass and it
took all the wager and a bit over to keep him quiet.

“The Mussel” writes on the stupefying
effects of Melbourne whisky :
I met Jones on Spencer-street railway-station, wander-
ing around in an aimless manner. His brain was evi-
dently disentangling some knotty problem ; his forehead
was puckered with wrinkles and his eyes glared stead-
fastly at an advt. about somebody’s unshrinkable pickles
that wouldn’t wash clothes. This was what he told me :
“ I’ve had a fearful blow. It is 3.25 by that clock, isn’t
it?” (I said it was.) “To tell you the truth, Wilkins,”
he went On, “ I can’t trust my own eyes. I don’t feel
sure of anything. My train goes in half-an-hour. Let us
have a drink.” (We had a drink and I ventured to in-
quire the cause of his trouble.) “Trouble! Why the
trouble has hardly commenced yet. The trouble start 3
when I meet my wife this evening and try to explain how
this lunatic business came about.” (We had another
drink.) “ Listen, Wilkins! This is what happened :
The wife and I arranged to go up country for a holiday.
Just then I got a summons for not having Tommy vac-
cinated. Now, you know my ideas about vaccination.
You have heard me say many a time that no child of
piine would ever be vaccinated—that, with my consent,
no filthy disease would be injected into the blood of any
of my offspring to stave off another disease, an attack of
which is much less probable than being struck by light-
ning on the fourth Wednesday in the month. However,
we’ll let that go. Well, my wife was very anxious for me
to come with her and pay the fine —whatever it was.
But I was determined to stay behind, and have a run for
my money. It was hardly likely that I was going to
allow a parcel of wooden-headed J’s.P. fine me without a
protest and a very strong expression of my opinion. Day
before the case came on I saw the family off. I particularly
requested my wife to look up the paper and see how I
held forth to the Bench, and peruse my withering scorn.
After the train left I walked round town and met a few
friends, and I had a few drinks. I had my dinner in town,
and I believe I must have had another drink or two, or
probably more. We went to Rickards—that is, Johnson,
M‘Phee, and I. I think the drink must have gone to my
head, because I remember having to close up one eye with
my hand in order to see the stage. I don’t remember much
after this. I have a dim recollection of assaulting some-
one or breaking something ; but probably I only dreamt
it. I awoke with a dreadful head. The gas was alight
and the blinds down. Someone must have brought me
home and lit the gas—probably M’Phee ; he’s unmarried,
and, of course, has no awkward questions to answer.
There was about half a bottle of whisky on the mantel-
piece. Where it came from—the Lord ,knows. I had
some—perhaps more, and felt much better. I went to
sleep again, and when I woke it was still dark. It seemed
a terribly long night. I had a look at the clock. It was
about 20 past 3 ; but, on closer inspection, I found that it
had stopped. I had another drink. I didn’t sleep any
more thataiight. I wandered round the house rehearsing
my anti-vaccination speech, and waiting for daylight.
Daylight wouldn’t come. I got irritated. At last there
were signs of daybreak, and I went out on the verandah
to await the morning paper. It came, and I read this
paragraph.” (Here Jones drew a newspaper from his
pocket) :
“At the W Police Court yesterday W. M‘Wil-
liams and J. T. Thomas were each fined £l, with ss.
costs for neglecting to have their children'vaccinated.
W. H. Jones, who did not appear, was for a similar
offence fined £2, with ss. costs.
“ Well,” said Jones, “ I told you it seemed a very long
night. And now the only thing I can tell my wife is that
I got stage-fright, and ran out of the court when my case
was called.” (Here we had another drink.)

“Jaw”: They were telling damper-yarns
round the fire, and some of’em were tough ; but
they all shut up after one little chap had spun his.
In the early Coolgardie days he and a mate were
one night compelled to camp where the sand was
worse than ordinary, and a damper had to be
made somehow. There was no shelter from the
sand, and they had no tent. So this man induced
his mate to lie face-down wards and head to wind,
draw up his shirt, and allow him (the other) under
its protection to knead the damper on his back !

Dear Bulletin.— “Vril” (B. 12/7/’00)
was lucky in getting the duck, and not the “ egg”
only ; as a wire-cartridge fired from a “ choke-
bore ” almost invariably bursts before leaving the
muzzle, and scatters the shot in all directions.
Know of one instance when the case dropped not
20ft. from the gun, and at 40yds. the shot scattered
nearly across the creek. Anyhow a “choke-bore”
loaded in the ordinary way is often good for over
100yds., but to a “choke” I prefer the “all
cylinder” barrel, loaded with a cartridge cut
nearly all round between powder and shot. Thus
frovided, I have potted rabbits at 140yds.—Bo-
EEP.

“ W.V.D.” : Re “ Vril’s ” long shot (B.
21/7/’00). I was out, recently, after plain-turlcey,
with ordinary b.l. gun and common cartridges—
No. 1 shot. The birds were so wild that at last,
in desperation, I banged at one which rose, ap-
parently far out of shot, and was astounded to see
it fall. Found it quite dead, though subsequent
post-mortem showed that it had stopped only one
grain of shot, which had broken its wing. On
measuring the distance, I found it 130yds.

Dear Bulletin.—There’s a substance that
falls from the blossomed red-gum (the children
call it “manna.”) Looks like “frosted” sugar,
in pieces from pea to bean size, and has a most
delicious taste. Where ants are not numerous
sometimes quarts of it may be gathered. What a
country for bees this should be !—Ben Sun.

“Eller”: Re black sheep. R. Whitticker,
of Adelong Crossing, N.S.W., has a flock of 3000,
and J. Dennis, of Tarcutta, Wagga, N.S.W., can
muster 2300 blacks and greys. Black sheep are
much hardier than white, but their wool does not

“Xerxes”: Black cockatoos will bite off
limbs 4in. and sin. in dia. in search of wood-grubs,
and I think those gang-gangs and galahs must
have some such object in view. The black cocky
is never mistaken as to the spot where the grub is,
and does some honest, hard graft for his food.

“ Booboo ” : Old-timer used to tell how,
whilst telegraph-line-making near Cootamundra,
the party used often to find that the insulators
placed on the poles during the day were on the
ground in the morning. Kept watch, and caught
a native bear at the wild-monkey cocoanut trick.

A Goulburn Resident Nearly Suffocated.
Mrs. Thomas Austin, late of Taralga, now re-
siding at John-street, Goulburn, has passed
through an experience which might well try the
nerves of any woman.
Indeed, the rumors concerning the occurrence
were so extraordinary that a local i eporter called
upon the lady.
Mrs. Austin, a pleasant faced lady, when called
upon told her experiences in the following words.
“One morning, whilst engaged in household
duties, I was alarmed by a peculiar tightening at
the chest, and a little later found myself almost
suffocated, being scarcely able to draw breath.
This, I was told, was due to flatulence; hence-
forth I became a chronic sufferer from indigestion,
biliousness, frightful headaches, and dizziness. I
lost my appetite, grew thin and pale-faced, and
what little food I ate did not digest till late at
night; many a night I have been forced to walk
the room instead of going to bed. At times I was
unable to perform domestic duties, so acute were
the fits of suffocation. After suffering for over six
years I consulted a doctor and took many bottles
of medicines without permanent benefit; then my
skin became quite yellow. Three years ago I
commenced Dr. Williams’ pink pills, and after
taking two boxes found myself quite free from my
complaints. I now eat my meals heartily with-
out fear of indigestion, sleep well, and wake
vigorous and refreshed in the morning, ready for
the day’s work. Had I tried Dr. Williams’ pink
pills earlier I should have been saved years of
suffering.”
■ Any excessive strain on the system will cause
an entire derangement of the digestive powers
Dr. Williams’ pink pills restored Mrs. Austin to
robust health because they made rich, red blood,
thus enabling the digestive organs to extract
nourishment from.the food taken. By acting as a
nerve tonic and blood-builder, Dr. Williams’ pink
pills cure such diverse ailments as paralysis, loco-
motor ataxia, rheumatism, sciatica, ansemia,
scrofula, eczema, rickets, consumption, bronchitis',
hysteria, &c. Sold by chemists and storekeepers’
and by the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s
Place, Sydney, two and nine per box, six boxes
fifteen and three, post free—But mind you ask for
Dr. Williams’. Substitutes are valueless.


LAYING THE CORNER-STONE OF THE AUSTRALIAN COMMONWEALTH. — BY HOP’S UNDERSTUDY.
In t he picture above are depicted some of the features of the ceremony as now proposed. In No. 1 we recognise Mr. Earton, of course. The bottle of champagne in his hand was intended to be broken over the stone. It seemed a pity to waste it. 2. Prayer-competition for all denomi-
nations. 3. Commonwealth ode competition for poets, Australian and otherwise. The leader will have little difficulty in iecognis in ' (a) Mr. Kipling, (b) Australia's warrior-poet, (c) probably meant for Mr. Alfred Austin (or Allen?). 4. Musical competition, all-comers. 5. Terpsichorean
features. 6. Oratorical or talking competition, open to Australian Statesmen. 7. Fine-art features in the form of “lightning sketches.” 8. The masses —voters, rate-payers, etc.

“A DREAM OF MUSICAL UNION.”
“About a hundred musicians met at the Trades-hall yesterday afternoon for the purpose of forming a union. Mr. Thomas Smith, M.L.A, moved: ‘That, in order to unite the orchestral
and professional musician of Australasia for their mutual benefit and protection, and to improve the status of the profession in general, it is, in the opinion of tins meeting, absolutely necessary
to form a union, having its headouarters in Melbourne.’ He said it had come to the knowledge of the committee that civil servants were taking up musical engagements after their regular hours,
and so unfairly with the professional men. That was one thing the union would have to stop. Mr. Stephen Barker seconded the motion, which was carried. -MELBOURNE ARGUS.
