“Where the Dead Men Lie!”
In far-west N.S.W. for six or eight years there
has been only an occasional shower, and because,
in the seven months of this present year, six
whole inches have been registered the settlers talk
of a “grand season” and a “revival of pros-
perity.” In the bad seven years sheep died by
the million; one after another, runs and selections
got into the hands of mortgage companies; station
improvement was suspended ; £BOO-a-year man-
agers were replaced by £l5O-a-year jackaroos,
shearing-sheds on out-stations were nailed up; the
slieds at the home-station were not a quarter full;
immense areas of country were abandoned ; and
the permanent native growths —saltbush and the
like —were wiped out of whole districts by drought,
over-stocking, and rabbits combined. Before this
present wetness (6in. in 7 months) set in, Govt.,
as the easiest way out of a maze, appointed a Com-
mission to take evidence and suggest measures for
the relief of present holders and for the improve-
ment of the conditions of future settlement; and
the interested parties are now wisely trying to
agree upon the things to tell the Commission.
The particular group that wants The Bulletin’s
opinion suggests: (1) That no resumed areas
should be attached; (2) that a homestead lessee
should be entitled to take up country to run 6000
sheep; (3) that appraisement should be by the land-
boards as at present ; (4) that “ the blocks be taken
in rotation starting from two given points on each
resumed area” ; (5) that present holders be en-
titled to add to their holdings enough to enable
them to carry 6000 sheep in all. This is the
“ cocky’s ” budget. The squatter, of course, puts
in his little claim, too. He wants wholesale
attachment of resumed areas and a 42 years’ lease
(he has 28 now). Some of him also asks for a
fixed rent for the whole term (it is _ re-appraised
every seven years, now); and a third lot wants
rent to be paid according to the stock actually
carried. Also, there is a fourth lot that wants
rents to move up and down with the rainfall—
irrespective of the small fact that all depends on
how the rain falls.
* * * *
All the men who hold land out back are either
squattera or selectors, otherwise “cockies” ; and
between the two there is a deep and abiding love.
When legislation “ to assist the pastoral interest ”
is talked of the average squatter can think of no
other plan than to take something from the
selector; and to the cocky, legislation to assist
the selector can only mean giving the squatter an-
other nasty jar. So the squatter now wants to
shut out the selector for some years more; and
the selector now wants to get a freer grab at the
squatter’s open country. It never seems to occur
to either of them that, as both of them have had
cruel, hard times, both of them deserve a lift, and
that it isn’t a fair thing to help one cripple on by
robbing another cripple—crippled in the same
accident, too —of his crutches. If there are not
enough crutches to go round, and if one of these
two cripples must suffer, it’s better to let the
squatter suffer, for when land is selected about a
dozen people are generally maintained where the
squatter maintains one. But why take any-
body’s crutches? The Bulletin would like to
see the Commission try to show how both squatter
and cocky can be given a lift, seeing that the
same drought and the same rabbits have afflicted
squatter and cocky alike.
m • * *
Yet it is far simpler to give to one by taking
from the other. And in the end some of that will
have to be done, no doubt. The squatter, for
instance, will have to give up more of his country.
The selector on whose petition these remarks are
penned suggests that a selector should be able to
take up land enough to carry 6000 sheep. In the
Western division that would mean, on an average,
say, 45,000 acres. Far West, it would generally
mean, in the existing impoverished state of the
country, 60,000 acres. Still, 6000 sheep are much
more than enough to keep a family comfortably.
The present maximum (10,240 acres) is not enough
to carry, on an average, 1500 sheep; and a man can’t
put much by for a droughty day of seven years
out of a mob that size. When he comes to realise
that—if he doesn’t mind being a bit of a rogue—
he becomes a dummy, or employs a dummy.
Otherwise, he is a poor drudge, knowing no com-
fort, whose children grow up in semi-savagery.
Lately there was sent down a list of eight selec-
tions of about 10,240 acres each, on two neighboring
runs, which had not been able to keep any stock
whatever for 20 months ! A big station may spell
paddocks, and by putting stock on to those re-
served, when the worst conies, a fair proportion
may be saved. But this is a resource outside the
I lower of the Western cocky. He has all he can
do to ring-fence his unwieldy 10-acre-to-the-sheep
holding without sub-dividing it. If selection is
to continue out there, the area which he may take
must be enlarged.
• • • •
It is arguable, though, whether it would not be
better to discourage selection way-back, and cor-
respondingly encourage it closer in. South Aus-
tralia sees now that it would have been better if
she had encouraged farming in the south instead
of sending cockies to a hopeless battle in the far-
north • and the fact that cockies themselves
reckon as much as 50,000 and 60,000 acres a fair
thing for a selector seems pretty strong proof that
the country isn’t ideal for what the Lands Depart-
ment facetiously calls “close settlement.” On
the other hand, 50,000 acres isn’t an acre more in
proportion out of a two-million-acre holding than
2500 acres is out of a 100,000-acre run closer in.
And besides, the Western station - hand and
shearer reasonably want to know why they should
be driven out of a country which they have learned
how to work, and into a country which they know
nothing about. Also the old man wants to know
why his son should have to go hundreds of miles
away to get a bit of country when there are so
many million acres around him—to go hundreds of
miles away where the old man’s advice would not
be on tap. All things considered, there does not
seem any good reason why he (the son) should be
driven away. Yet, if he is allowed to select, he
ought to be allowed to select enough to live on
decently, rearing his children like civilised beings,
clothing them well, feeding them well, and edu-
cating them. And he must be able to run
3000 sheep to do it. In favor of fixing the
maximum in this way, rather than by a
stated acreage, there is a lot to be said. It is
fairest to the selector, and it is fairest to the
squatter. Especially if joined with the other sug-
gestion that the selections should tail on one after
the other—it will save the squatter from paying
rent for a lot of country which is no good to him.
At present his run may be riddled; strips and
patches may be almost, if not entirely, discon-
nected from the main area. Not being worked,
these fragments become the nurseries of vermin.
The tailing-on plan would end that. But the
squatter’s worst trouble, apart from droughts, is
that his choice patches, which naturally are, as a
rule, richest in improvements, are liable to be
selected if they are on the resumed half of the
run. If there is any movement towards selection 1
in the district (and on some runs boundary-riders
have to report all new tracks seen on selectable
country) stock are rushed on and the paddock is
eaten-out. When the Western runs were cut in
halves, the leased half and the selectable half
(called the “ resumed area”) were supposed to be
of equal value ; yet, on probably two-thirds of the
runs, the resumed areas are now of far less value
than the leased area—which is due largely to this
eating-out in order to repress selection. If, how-
ever, a selector could take up more country the
fewer sheep it would carry per square mile, the
squatter would not have this inducement to abuse
any particular part of his holding. Further, if a
selector could get more country, the poorer it was,
there would be no reason for keeping open large
tracts of country far away from rivers or railways
for the sake of the one or two likely-looking
patches, because the selector would take the
country nearer cheap carriage. All the same,
scores of resumed areas which might very well be
attached to the leasehold, so long as 10,240 acres
is the maximum area of a selection, ought not to
be attached at all if that maximum area is ex-
tended.
* • • *
As for the squatter, he would lose if the selector
could take more country; but he would gain if
selections had to follow on. Also, with larger
selections he would gain by getting better value
for his improvements. At present he dare not
Eut expensive improvements on his resumed area,
ecause, though they may be worth £lOOO to him
on his own area, they might be worth only £2OO to
the selector on his small area, and £2OO is, there-
fore, all the squatter can claim. These gains,
however, would hardly counterbalance his loss
through the larger area given to the selector;
hence, to his own gains might be added an exten-
sion of lease. South Australia makes it 42 years
for similar land ; and, for country subject to such
long droughts, during which it is a burden and
not a profit-giving asset—for country, moreover,
which is utterly useless unless it is improved at
great expense —a 42 years’ lease is not a long one.
The Military Gussie.
The first public utterance of a truth that will
presently take a lot of conceit out of Military
Spirit in Great Britain and her Jingo colonies
recently came along per cable. It was to the
effect that the various contingents are not getting
decent treatment from the “Imperial staff,” 1
which means, probably, not that the few brainy
officers who have retained from the first, or lately
arrived at, high places in the S’African army, are>
to blame for the trouble, but that a multitude of
minor johnnie snobs in buckram have made them-
selves hateful (by their mannahs, ordahs, and',
general incompetence) to the “meah outsidahs
who do such a mighty deal of the fighting work.
The British Army is an institution where the>
languid young “toff” does his very best to des-
troy Jingoism and Imperialism. He enters it for'
the purpose of putting on more frill, and.
he leaves it with the muttered curses of
Tommy Atkins on his empty head. And
he is the rule rather, than the exception.
His arrogance is everywhere. Plain Tommy
speaks of him in language unfit for publication.
Travellers who meet him in India wonder how it
is that the slight counteracting influence of the
more popular and diplomatic officers can prevent
another great black revolt against the military
rule of these abominable fools, whose life is one
long insult to their “ inferiahs.” The bitterest of
Boer-haters from the Cape—there are many of
them in Australia now—have nothing kind to say
about the Gussies and Algernons they met dur-
ing their recent brief campaigning with the
“regulars.” ' Their predictions of a popular out-
cry against Gussie seem to be already fulfilled.
The colonial troops all round are going to expose
the rottenness of the British army system, which
places the management of men in the hands of
insolent nincompoops. Tommy Atkins is pre-
pared to voice his disciplined, down-trodden dis-
content as he never has voiced it before, and
“military spirit” among the non-fighting masses
will suffer a heavy discount unless the Lord Tom-
noddies of the War Office admit the sinful failure
of Gussie, and take immediate measures to clear
him out of the business.
The Australian Fat Man is much troubled just
now about the excessive cheapness of money in
Australia. He can’t get large interest on his
investments. His banks can’t earn anything like
the dividends they used to do, for they have to
lend money at low rates or else they can t lend it
at all. In a country like Australia, which owes
an immense amount of money abroad, and which
is consequently being drained of gold to pay
interest, money should always be scarcer and,
consequently, dearer, than in a country like
England, which has immense investments abroad,
and has gold always pouring in by way of
interest. And just now, to the Fat Man’s grief
and discomfiture, Australian and English interest
rates are almost on a level. It is consoling, after
a fashion, to reflect that the Australian Fat Man
is only reaping the natural consequence of his own
cowardice and lack of brain. Australia keeps on
borrowing largely abroad because her Fat JVlen
think it is easier to borrow and let posterity take
the consequences than to tax themselves a little
more and pay their way. And the influx of bor-
rowed money makes a temporary artificial super-
abundance of money, and the artificial super-
abundance makes money cheap. Wherefore the
Fat Man can’t get the interest ne used to do—and
serves him right.

If the official report of N.S.W. Comptroller of
Prisons is to be relied on, a startling number of
demented or insane people are sent to gaol as
criminals. Here are half-a-dozen typical cases :
(1) A miner, imprisoned for 12 months, on failing: to
find sureties for wife-desertion ; previously insane ; symp-
toms detected in two days; general paralysis of the in-
sane ; sent to an asylum. (2) Painter, 21 days for injur-
in or property I dementia detected on reception , kept m
gaol till expiry of sentence. (3) Cook, five years for
maliciously wounding ; dementia ; detected on reception ,
still in prison. (4) Seaman, four months for vagrancy ;
epileptic ; detected on reception ; kept in prison till encl
of term, and then discharged to police court. (5) Married
woman, six months for riotous behaviour; delusional
mania ; detected on reception ; passed on to an asylum in
ten weeks. (6) Miner, three months for stealing ; epilep-
tic ; detected on reception, symptoms of insanity showing
in six days ; served full sentence in gaol.
These are from the records of one year, and
there are some scoresof such cases. Obviously heaps
of these convictions for crimes ought not to stand
in the records against these unfortunates at all;
yet they do stand, and moreover are used against
the unfortunates if they happen to fall into the
hands of the police again—perhaps for the same
old mental sickness.

The Midland Railway Go. of Westralia wants
badly to sell out to the State. It got a magnifi-
cent land-grant for building a small, cheap rail-
way, and, in addition, the State lent it at a very
low interest half the money to build its railway.
But the co. wouldn’t risk its own money in its
own spec., and what money it couldn’t borrow
from the State it borrowed elsewhere at such
ruinous interest that it promptly got into trouble
and stopped payment. Then there were two
alternatives in front. One was to sell or lease
its huge land-grant at low rates, and' thus
create settlement and make business for
the railway. The other was to block settlement,
in the hope that the State would rather buy it out
than see a great portion of the best part of W.A.
kept a howling wilderness. The Midland Co. has
been trying the latter device. The Crown tenants
who were on the land when it was granted to the
Midland Co. are being refused new leases, and are
offered a yearly tenure at three times the old
rental, and some of them are Being simply thrown
out. As might be expected, these people are
weeping at Bigjohn’s door, beseeching him to
buy out the company “at any cost.” And it is
only ten years since W.A. was wild with joy at
the thought of how its Midland swindle-cat was
going to promote settlement, and make the wil-
derness blossom like the rose, and start a new era
of happiness and prosperity.
As a further proof of the evils of Protection,
Pulsford’s new paper, Our Country, strikes this
beautifully irrelevant argument:
The champion debt-creator of N.S.W. is our esteemed
friend, George Dibbs. During the time in the eighties
when that gentleman was at the Treasury he placed three
successive loans of £5,500,000 in each of three successive
years, bringing the debt up, with other little loans, to 41
millions at the close of 1886.
How beautifully accurate! Dibbs left the
Treasury at the end of ’B5. He was Treasurer
when only two of the 5| million loans were floated.
He was a Foreign-trader then—had been one
all his life—and avowedly remained one till the
early nineties —he was Treasurer in a Foreign-
trade Ministry, and he went on the tear in a true
Foreign-trade fashion. Even the faintest accusa-
tion of Protectionism wasn’t charged against the
Ministry until Jennings took the Treasury, and
his 5 per cent, revenue duties didn’t last long.
Also Our Country quite fails to notice that these
same Foreign-traders, Dibbs and Co., borrowed
£6,000,000 in 1883. That little crowd of Foreign-
trade politicians raised the N.S.W. public debt in
four years from 18f millions to 41 millions. And
the Parkes Foreign-trade Government boosted it
up in five years more to 53 millions. What a
time N.S.W. had in these bygone days, and how
the grand old Foreign-trade party did “go it” !
By the way, Dibbs, after his very brief spell as a
Protectionist, is again a Foreign-trader. He
lately denounced Federation on the ground that
it would deprive N.S.W. of the blessings of Free-
trade. The only respectable part of Dibbs’s finan-
cial career was while he was a Protectionist.
“F.C.” revives an old idea :
If they can spare the time, some of Melbourne’s public
bodies--the City Council, or the Board of Health, or the
awful ditto of Works—might do worse than put up a
small, air-tight chamber, to accommodate one or two
persons, with a supply of charcoal handy. The present
sinfully-wasteful way of dealing with derelicts is a gross
reflection on the Victorian’s commercial acumen. Con-
sider : Threepenn’orth of charcoal, 10s. for a State funeral
and half-a-crown as a death-certificate fee to the charcoal
official—l2s. 9d. the lot; whereas under the present ex-
travagant system, the bill shows Is. for body-recovery,
20s. for jury-fees, a guinea medical-fee, 10s. burial-fee,
besides polluting the Yarra. The plan would also greatly
simplify the process of dealing with those sick and aged
poor persons whom a lavish state now sends to an expensive
gaol for six months or so, repeating the dose as occasion
requires, until they finally uie of old age or too much
hominy. Look at the enormous amount of trouble and
expense to which Elizabeth Nurey, aged 32, put Victoria
the other day. Elizabeth was ill and destitute, and had
no where to go, so she took poison. A constable happened
to notice that she didn’t look any too fit, so he took her
to the hospital, where she was pumped out. The usual
course after such an occurrence is a remand, then six
months’ gaol to recover, then 12 months’ more if she
hasn’t got a job in the meantime. A day or two later an
old woman was found, after midnight, slowly freezing to
death on the steps of an Elizabeth-street warehouse. She
was homeless, and so destitute that she couldn’t buy
even the cheapest poison, so just lay down to die where
she was. She was duly accommodated with six months
gaol. The week previous two old men went to sleep in
the Yarra instead of in their beds, as all right-thinking
old men should do, and their remains had to be fished out
and identified and post-mortemed and inquested, and
finally planted ; and all this costs a lot of money, and gives
extra work to the police when they should be loitering
about easily. Wherefore, I cordially recommend the
lethal chamber, as specified—cheap, simple, and reliable ;
one trial will satisfy the hardest case, he will never use
any other. And a community that has stood the gaoling
of its old and poor so long and unconcernedly needn’t be
squeamish over such a painless, efficacious, and, above all,
inexpensive method as this.

He Shouted for Help.
It was not for pleasure that Mr. Wilson concluded
to take a walk in his garden ; it was rather an ex-
periment than an act of recreation. And, griev-
ous to relate, the result was against him. The
fact is, he had hardly covered a hundred feet of
ground before he stopped, gave a choking gasp,
and then sang out for help. His wife and two
sons came to the rescue, and got him indoors as
best they were able. And that ended his going
alone for six months or more.
By trade Mr. Wilson is a carpenter, one of the
most useful, peaceful and respectable of all the
forms of industry. He has lived and worked for
a long time at Given Terrace, Paddington, Bris-
bane, Queensland, and lives there still. About
four years ago—or it will be by the time this gets
into print—Mr. Wilson began to feel himself much
less of a man than he used to be ; lie was breaking
down.
The first thing he noticed was that when he set
out to walk a fairish distance, which he would have
done once with a kit of tools on his back without
minding it—I say, when he set out to tramp this,
he found his legs were weak, and he often had to
stop for breath. And he kept on getting worse.
Such a state of things was almost as bad for a car-
penter as it would have been for a postman.
Both these vocations demand good legs and good
wind.
On being consulted, the doctor said, “ Mr.
Wilson, your heart is so weak it can scarcely pump
the blood through your body, and your whole sys-
tem is out of order. There is no chance of your
getting sound again, and the sooner you lay aside
your saws and hammers the longer you are likely
to live.”
These were plain words, to be sure, but not
words which a patient would feel like paying out
money to listen to. All the same, friend Wilson
did as the doctor said, because he had no choice.
He couldn’t work, and so, naturally, he didn’t.
His chisels grew dull, but not so dull as their
owner. He left off making chips and shavings,
and went in for drugs and regrets —a bad land-
slide for him.
After about half a year of this sort of thing,
Mr. Wilson made up his mind to find out for him-
self if he was in fact so poor a stick of human
timber as the medical man had declared him to
be; hence the experimental walk in the garden
already described. For six months more he was
like a ship in a dry dock, of no use to himself or
anybody else. The doctor had measured up the
carpenter’s complaint to an eighth of an inch, but
as for curing it, why, that he made no pretence of
doing.
“ About this time,” says Mr. Wilson in a letter
dated Sept. 22nd, 1899, “Mr. Frank Percival
Peacock, of Manning Street, South Brisbane,
urged me to try Mother Seigel’s Syrup; he said
he was sure it would help me. I didn’t think so,
but I tried it. To my surprise and delight, it
enabled me to get about in three weeks, and in six
wee/is I went back to work ; and have had splendid
health ever since.
“ As I am 61 years old it wasn’t the rebound of
youthful elasticity that saved me; it was Mother
Seigel’s Syrup, and nothing else. I am known to
nearly all the people of this neighbourhood, who
can vouch for the truth of my statement.”—
H. Wilson.
Mr. Wilson’s ailment was of the digestion—the
heart and lung troubles being functional symp-
toms of that. When the stomach was made right
he picked up his saw.

Works Minister O’Sullivan’s ambition is
the State Premiership of N.S.W.
According to ex-Premier Reid, not more
than 15 or 20 members of N.S.W. Leg. Assembly
will be candidates for the Federal Parliament.
Melbourne papers fiercely oppose Best’s
Bill to fix the maximum for election expenses at
£lOO. You can’t abolish bribery without seriously
damaging the advertising interest.
Premier Lyne’s latest motto : Unity of
the Federation with the Empire.” Not even
Federal relationship, but unitv. The wolf in
the fable advocated unity, but ASsop Lyne makes
the lamb amble up and beg unity—on his usual
terms.
The Wbstralian Government of Bigjohn
and Co., now in its tenth year of office, survived
Illingworth’s rdotion of censure, the first one that
has struck it in all its long career, by 22 votes to
16. Bigjohn’s downfall isn’t due till next year’s
General Election.
The Victorian Australian Natives’ Associa-
tion wants the Commonwealth Proclamation to
take place on 26th Jan. next (because, you see,
the A.N.A. holds its annual celebration on that
date). But then Victoria wasn’t “lagged” out
here—and everybody isn’t in its position.
Tasmanian politician Hartnoll quite seri-
ously proposed, the other day, that every Tas.
soldier and volunteer should have a free railway
pass while he remained in the service. The idea
wasn’t entertained. If it had been Tasmania
would have had plenty of volunteers and mightv
little railway revenue.
The only Federal certainties in Victoria
are George Turner for St. Kilda and Alfred
Deakin for Ballarat—or Ballaarat, as it is pedan-
tically spelt in the Govt. Bill. M'Eacham thinks
he has a good chance for Melbourne City, but
Fink may score. Dr. Quick should win in Ben-
digo, but there will be a fight for the seat. Dr.
MTnerney (Warden of the University Senate)
will most likely pull it off in Moira, as one of the
Murray constituencies is called, if he works hard.
The rest is chaos.

Officially admitted that men are actually
being engaged by Q. Govt, to lecture in S’Africa,
with a view to inducing Boer farmers to emi-
grate to Bananaland ! Here we have “loyal”
citizens expected to receive into their midst a
people whom the Jingo papers have all along

S.A. Leg. Council so mutilated the House-
hold Suffrage Bill that Attorney-General Gordon
has declined to recognise it as a Govt, measure.
The Tory majority has now altered the measure
to confer the suffrage on the wives of the pro-
pertied minority which possesses the vote at
present. This is adding insult to injury with a
vengeance !
The U.S. Govt., it is cabled, has ap-
proached the Powers in favor of peace. This is
“ very excellent good ” ; but it is decidedly funny
to gather from the very next item that the Yankee
missionary societies are pressing the President
not to withdraw any U.S. troops from China.
It’s the spiritual arm, as usual, that wants most
to “ smite and spare not ! ”
Lord Lamington, the offensive Colonial
Office protege who is titular Governor of Queens-
land, and who made himself remarkable by
meddling with Federation, is credited with pub-
licly advocating more religious instruction in Q.
State-schools. Judging by all The Bulletin
hears, a little religious instruction in Q. Govt.
House is much more desirable.
Ex-Premier Kingston spends nearly every
Parliamentary day as an interested spectator of
S.A. Assembly debates. When Solomon tabled
his adverse motion, Kingston remarked, with fire
in his eye, that he would give all he possessed to
be a participator in the battle. Though out of
the Assembly, the ex-Premier distinguished him-
self by energetically “ whipping ” for the Holder
Ministry.
A healthy sign of the times is that the
blustering councillor John M‘Mahon was beaten
at the municipal election of Fitzroy. This loud-
lunged Jingo was one of the chief persecutors of
plucky engineer Seitz, of the Board of Works,
M‘Mahon being chairman of the Officers and
Servants Committee. His defeat ejects him from
the Board of Works. In refusing to re-elect him
the democrats of Fitzroy do themselves credit.
The miserable little Bill for reforming
Sydney Municipal Council is going to pieces in
N.S. W. Upper House. The Lvne Govt, said that
if the franchise was granted to all adult residents
it would give the Council a chance to reject the
Bill as too sweeping, so it gave a vote to the
householder and the lodger, but none to the house-
holder’s grown-up sons living with him. Then
the Council said that it was a disgraceful
inequality to give the son who lived in his father’s
house no vote, while the son who lived in some
other father’s house had one, so it cut out the
lodger vote, and put the franchise back very much
on the old unreformed basis. And it was the
half-heartedness and cowardice of the LyneGovt.
which supplied good logical excuse for the Council’s
attitude.
The latest Tory hoax is to the effect
that the Victorian banks are going to refuse
accommodation to all manufacturers whose
trades are brought under the Factories
Act, on the ground that these manufac-
turers are bound to be ruined and the
banks don’t wish to share their disasters.
Considering the kind of people (not under
any Factories Act) that the banks lent
money to a few years ago, and the conse-
quences thereof, their capacity to foresee
what kind of people are going to be
ruined seems rather limited.
The impossibility of the sects agree-
ing over appliances and means for intro-
ducing Scripture lessons into Vic. State-
schools is demonstrated by a recent utter-
ance of A.rchbishop Carr’s on the result of
the combined effort of the pietists to pro-
duce suitable religious pills for children.
“ At present the Catholic children in the
State-schools are starved,” said the R.C.
magnate, “but if these lessons are intro-
duced they will be poisoned.” These great
and good men will never agree as to which
is the most suitable poison to feed chil-
dren on.
M.L. Railways Minister Ward has
extended the great deadhead system by
granting free passes over all lines to mem-
bers’ wives during session. The whole
family-tree is now practicall v provided for,
as concessions are granted to sons and
daughters of legislators when visiting the
seat of Govt.
* ! Tasmania’s property-elected.Upper
House is even more notorious than that
of Victoria for the regularity with
whicli it throws out everything’ at all
resembling democratic legislation.
This session it got three chances and
seized them all. The Assembly sent
up a Workmen’s Compensation-for-
Accidents Bill, a Bill to limit dis-
traint for rent, and a Bill to limit
the garnisheeing of wages, and they
all came hurtling down the stairs of
the Chamber of Property. A Bill to
abolish the Meat Tax kept them com-
pany, but it matters little. The Federal
Parliament will fix its eye on the Meat Tax next
year.
Speaking in N.S.W. Leg. Assembly re the,
despatch of the China contingent, ex-Premier
Reid remarked that the men had been committed
to going upon the proposal of two officers; and
that, when the project was first mentioned to the
men, not one held up his hand to go. But
when twitted with being cowards and so on,
they volunteered to go, in order to keep up
the honor of the brigade. And 95 per cent of
those who went for the purpose named were, he
understood, married, and some of them had been
receiving £4 and £5 a week in their respective
callings. These things were all set forth by The
Bulletin at the time; but what punishment
should be awarded G. H. Reid for giving such
treasonable statements the imprimatur of his
political standing ?

URCHIN: “ Halves , Mister! I saw him first.”
The three Lynes : At first he hadn’t given
the question of dual seats “ very serious considera-
tion,” but compromised with his conscience by
remarking the matter should be deferred till the

Q. Minister for Education Drake should
now begin to realise what he lost by hurriedly
snatching that polluted portfolio. The fallen idol
would have been a sure thing for a seat in the
Federal Parliament in the Democratic interest,
and almost a “monte” for some position or other
in the Federal Administration. He might not
have got a portfolio, but his services to P'edera-
tion would have demanded some recognition.
But he defiled his hands, and is dead, dead, dead.
The chief plotters against the Holder
Ministry at present are Premier-for-a-week Solo-
mon and once-Treasurer Rounsevell, who happens
to be a colleague of Holder in the representation
of the Burra. Play ford for the present is watch-
ing and waiting. Ex-Premier Kingston considers
the recent no-confidence debate a most irregular
proceeding in that it is the first for 20 years with
which he has had no prominent connection on one
side or the other. It is suggested that some
public-spirited Assembly member should resign
so that Kingston may gain a seat in that body.
A remarkable instance of the well-ascer-
tained variability of Sydney Telegraph appears
in the fact that it now joins with its enemies in
denouncing ex-Treasurer Reid as a financial im-
postor, who never, never, never had a surplus at
all. Yet, all through his brilliant financial panto-
mime, that paper held him up as a noble man of
figures whose statistics were immaculate, and
blithered and blasted See, Lyne, M‘Millan, and
Wise, who said every time that they didn’t
believe there was no sich a surplus. And all
for revenge at his final attitude on Federation—
after many attitudes !
Victoria’s general election is but two
months off, and so far there is no Liberal organi-
sation for the selection of candidates and the
prevention of clashing; nor have any of the
alleged leaders put forth a programme or raised
an election cry. Yet if such as Peacock or Tren-
with were to start now with a promise to force
through the Upper House a popularly-elected
State-Constitution measure, with a suggestion of
the abolition of said House, he would sweep the
polls. Turner, Shiels and M‘Lean would find them-
selves leaders of mere remnants. And the then
real leader of the Vic. Democratic Party would be
sure of a place in the first Federal Ministry.
The Lyne (N.S.W.) Government promises
to tackle four large questions this session:
Woman’s Suffrage, Old-Age Pensions, the estab-
lishment of a State Land Bank, and the resump-
tion of large estates for Closer Settlement. Reid
had a look-in at the last three of these, and
dropped them all. Lyne doesn’t drop things
with the glorious agility of Reid, but he will
probably half-do them, and do that half only
moderately, unless Wise takes the contract out of
his hand;}. Since Lyne’s weak-hearted botching
of the Sydney municipal law, he lias dropped
to zero in public estimation. There was an easy
chance there to do so much, and he did half of
next to nothing.
Affer months of wrangling and scheming
Adelaide corporation and other municipal bodies
are still at loggerheads over their tramways. The
city body has now abandoned the Bingham tram-
monopoly scheme, but stronsrly opposes a Munici-
pal Trust or State ownership. It may be said to
have sunk into a state of cantankerous inaction.
Some time ago Premier Holder announced that,
unless the local bodies promptly agreed on a
scheme, the State would step in. The local bodies
are further from agreement than ever, and in view
of the fact that Adelaide Corporation deposits its
hoof on every proposal save its own company-
monopoly idea, there is no visible reason for delay-
ing State purchase any longer.
Queensland Govt, perpetrates a cruel joke
when it lands raw English new-chums in the
middle of Q. sugar district, where they are forced
to compete with kanakas, Hindoos and Syrians,
very often, too, on lower wages than the heathen.
It is, however, comforting to know that Philp has
cabled Tozer that Q. Govt, purposes spending
£.10,000 on immigration next year.
For the first three Parliaments of N.S.W.
under Responsible Govt. M’s.P. had no free rail-
way passes. It was not till the fourth session
, of the fourth Parliament that the conces-
pv sion was claimed. The argument in favor
of tiie claim was that the rail-
roads only penetrated a little -
way into the country, and that
as coaches and other vehicles
were frequently blocked on
one account or another, mem-
bers would more certainly
reach their places in Parlia-
ment by taking trains. This-
was buttressed with the fact
that members of those days
were not paid. But small as
the concession then was, a sub-
stantial part of the Assembly
protested against passes, as
they were certain to be used
more for business than poli-
tical purposes. In a thin House
the free railway pass was car-
ried by 19 to 13.
Price, M.L.A., sounds the
depths of eternal first principles
in the notice of motion he has
placed on N.S.W. Parliamentary
business-paper. The motion reads—
“It is inequitable to charge tolls on punts and
permit traffic on bridges to go free.” No doubt.
In Westralia the influential offender ap-
parently fines himself at his own discretion. Perth
Ice Go., which has many of the magnates of the-
province among its shareholders, got into trouble
lately over a charge of defrauding the State out
of large sums in railway freight. The statutory
fine of £lO a ton on the goods in question would
probably have run into some thousands, but the
Ice Co., with all the profound coolness of its own
product, fixed its own fine at £lOO, coupled with a
denial of all liability and a reservation of the right
to dispute the claim for arrears of freight. The
offer was accepted. Yet, if a humbler offender,
who had been sentenced to the “cat,” claimed the
right to flog himself, W.A. justice would probably
be quite surprised.
Mainly about Y. L. Solomon, S.A. Oppo-
sition leader: —
Solomon, who has been in cold opposition off and on in
S.A. Assembly for nearly seven years, and a}>ain chal-
lenged the Holder Govt, last week, is an energetic, excit-
able and Slieiky-looking Israelite with an intense ambition
for office. He was Premier last Dec. of the only Tory
Ministry S.A. has seen for seven years, and he officially
lasted just five days. Solomon originally owned and
edited N.T. Times, published at Pt. Darwin, then em-
barked in politics and has represented the Territory—the
largest Parliamentary constituency on earth —for the last
nine years. He is generally understood to be wealthy,
discreet Westralian land speculations having turned out
well. As a leader he is much inferior to another Opposi-
tionist, Play ford, and the latter is probably the only S.A.
politician outside the Holder Ministry who could com-
mand a working majority in the present Parliament.
S.A. G ivt. lias introduced a Bill pro-
viding for the election of a popular convention of
AO members to be selected by the adults of the
State voting as one electorate, members to be paid
£1 Is. daily during office, their duties being to
frame a new Constitution, to draft a Civil List,
and to decide on the electoral district boundaries.
When the Constitution has been framed there is to •
be an adjournment of from 30 to (JO days, and then
it is to be finally adopted by the Convention, after
which it is to be submitted to a popular referen-
dum for approval and then, if ratified, sent to the
Queen for assent. An alternative proposal by
Grainger M.P. is to have a joint sitting of the
present Council (24) and Assembly (54) to frame-
the Constitution.
Labor-member M'Donald raised an in-
teresting question in Q. Assembly by asking
whether the private railway bills being introduced
by Govt, should not be considered as private bills,
and submitted to a select committee before pre-
sentation to Parliament. The action of Premier
Philp in this matter looks very strange. Private
individuals or companies desiring to pass private
bills through the House of Commons have some-
times to pay as much as £50,000 in fees for lawyers,
Parliamentary agents, wii nesses, etc., and in every
Parliament in the world the cost of drafting, pre-
senting and all other charges are paid by the
petitioners. The generous Philp, however, is
introducing a batch of private railway proposals
as public bills, and using the administrative and
legislative machinery of the country to save money
for foreign concession-mongers.
The only strong point ex-Premier Reid
took in his criticism in the debate on the N.S.W.
temporary Supply Bill was the action of the
Govt, in allowing two newspaper-reporters to go,
at State expense, with the 200 men selected for
service in China, in the capacity of “assistant
paymasters.” It was a farce, he said, to send
three officials to attend to' the pecuniary matters
in connection with a handful of men. Premier
Lyne “fell in” at once. “You know very well I
only allowed them two.” “Surely,” persisted
Reid, “ there was a paymaster. An assistant pay-
master must assist someone? No? Oh, I see,
they are to assist each other. How sensible ! ”
Then he went on to accuse the Govt, of imposing -
unnecessary expense upon N.S.W. Whereupon
Premier Lyne announced that he needn’t , trouble
—Great Britain paid all their expenses. Reid
then congratulated the Ministry on having got
so much of the bill on to the shoulders of Great
Britain, and said he would like to be present when
the Imperial authorities discovered that the loyal
Premier of loyal N.S.W. had saddled the British
taxpayer with the maintenance of the representa-
tives of the two leading N.S.W. daily papers,
which were quite able to pay their men them-
selves.

DOCTORS DIFFER,
VITADATIO
CURES.
HYDATIDS.
A New Arrival from New Zealand.
The Square, Palmerston North, N.Z.,
Mr. Palmer. 25th. April, 1899.
Dear Sir,—The case of M’Minn is a genuine one; the
doctor was in a fix about it, but said an operation was
absolutely necessary. I myself diagnosed it “ hydatids,”
and assured the father that Vitadatio would in less than
two days prove me to be correct. You have the father’s
testimony to that effect.—Yours faithfully,
JOHN HEPWORTH, Eclectic Chemist.
Palmerston North, N.Z.,
Mr. Palmer (VITADATIO), 25th. April, 1899.
Dear Sir, —On Bth. August, 1898, my son, aged 12, com-
plained of a swelling on his right side ; on examination
by a doctor it was pronounced to be “ hydatids on the
liver,” and an operation was ordered to take place im-
mediately for the removal of the growth. Meeting Mr.
Hepworth, eclectic chemist, of this town, to whom I
mentioned the trouble, he recommended me try a bottle
of Vitadatio before having resource to an operation, which
would be necessarily attended with more or less risk. I
procured a bottle, and within an hour after the first dose
an improvement in the system was perceptible, and
within a period of two or three weeks he was perfectly
well, and has remained so, enjoying excellent health
without any return of the former trouble. I obtained for
altogether four bottles, and the trial proved most
satisfactory indeed ; far beyond my anticipations.
Witness :J. Hepworth. ALEX. M'MINN, Journalist.

And at Melbourne, Adelaide, Perth, Brisbane, and Christchurch, (Lie.
Dickson, of Queensland, has the
ineffable cheek to complain that N.S.W.
and Victoria are dilatory in deciding to
compensate Somers Vine’s Papuan land-
grab syndicate for its expenses in trying
to steal a very large share of the Posses-
sion. According to the agreement under
which Papua is controlled, Queensland,
N.S.W., and Victoria find most of the
money, and are to be consulted on all
important matters. The two latter were
not consulted on the proposal to hand
oyer the most valuable part of the pro-
vince to a syndicate for next to nothing
per acre. Even the Q. Parliament didn’t
know of it. The steal was illegally and
secretly engineered by the syndicate, the
Papuan Governor, and one or two mem-
bers of the Q. Government, and it cost
the two Southern provinces much labor
and cash to get the barefaced grab an-
nulled. And now Dickson insolently
says that their reluctance to pay the syn-
dicate for expenses incurred in trying to
grossly defraud them “ has led to an
unfavourable opinion in England of Aus-
tralian integrity.'’ Has it, really ? The
Bulletin hopes that any N.S.W. or Vic.
Ministry which agrees to dole out one red
oent to the hungry Somers Vine and Co.
will have to face a vote of want of con-
fidence.
Victorian ex-Premier Turner made
nearest approach to being a born fool
when , he jeered at the windy Shiels for
charging all last financial year’s war-
expenditure to that year, instead of de-
fraying it out of loans.|The loud-mouthed
Jingoes of Victoria yelled themselves
hoarse over their anxiety to “ make
sacrifices for the Empire,” and seeing
that most of them didn’t fight personally,
the only way they can make sacrifices
is by paying personally. And the only
way they can pay personally is by pay-
ing now, while they are still alive and
solvent. Shiels’ mistake lay in not
levying a special war-tax, and in letting
old-age pensions go overboard in order to cover
the war bill. But his conduct in settling that bill
right off was wholly commendable.
The alternative scheme for naming the
N.S.W. Federal electorates put forward by the
Piddington Select Commitee is sadly wanting in
originality. Fifteen titles already applied to seats
in the State Legislature have been appropriated—
viz., Cook, Botany, Lang, Wentworth, Illawarra,
Bathurst, Goulburn, Monaro, Robertson, New-
castle, Murrumbidgee, Hume, The Clarence, The
Richmond, and Broken Hill. Only two of the
commissioners’ names are retained—Maroubra and
Kurringgai. If the new names are adopted by
Parliament confusion must result in calling the
Parramatta and the Petersham districts “Parkes”
and Wentworth respectively. Both Parkes and
Wentworth are well-known towns, and are miles
away from the Federal electorates named. The
new names recommended are Sydney, Sydney
South, The Hunter, The Darling, New England,
Liverpool Plains, and Canoblas, the only native
name. The commissioners’ list is much the better
allowing for the strangeness of the new appella-
tions.

A Victorian charmer holds the record of
having been the mother of 15 children (all now
dead), with none of the fathers of which she has
yet faced the altar.
You can sit down in your arm-chair by
your fireside, with your nice wife, and take out
your G.B.B. Briar pipe and say : “Ah! A good
wife, a good dinner, a good arm-chair, a good fire
—and now my C.B.B. Briar crowns my happi-
ness.”
The Acetylene Miners’ Lamps, introduced by
the Acetylene Gas Co. of Australia, Ltd., have proved a
great success and are now largely used in the mines
throughout the colony. The company has lately been
rushed with orders for lighting up gold-mining dredges
owing to the many advantages offered by the light for
that purpose. It would be well for mine and dredge
owners to ascertain full information as to cost, etc.,"from
the Head Office of the Company, 36 Pitt-street, Sydney.*
At a Vic. woman’s franchise meeting, a
long, slim-nosed sister burst into eloquence :—
“ And now, ladies, as to the way most men do vote. On
election days they will cease work and start for the hust-
ings in their best clothes.' As they go along the street
they will drink alternately with the canvassers of each
candidate, pledging their votes with each drink. Thus, a
lot of them never reach the hustings—because they are
not able. That accounts for there being only about half
the number of votes polled that are on the rolls ! Those
of this class that do arrive at the hustings generally see
twice the number of names on the ballot-paper that there
really are. And that accounts for the large number of
informal votes at every election ! Couldn’t we do better
than that?” (Loud cheers.)
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm lias a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they Will
be honorably treated.' H. Newfnaii' is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

At Poverty Point.
There is an actor still living who came on once in
the city of —— to play the Inquisitor in “ Gondo-
liers.” He was mentally concealed behind a large quan-
tity of beer, but he got on all right till he tried to sing the
lines : “ And bishops in their shovel-hats were plentiful
as tabby cats.” What he did say was : “ And bishops in
their shovel eats were plentiful as tabby hats.” The audi-
ence laughed, and instead of going straight on he un-
wisely went back and tried again. This time the result
was : “ And bishops in their tabby hats were plentiful as
shovel cats.” The audience howled, and the obfuscated
one felt that he must do or die, so he reeled off these
seven versions in quick succession : “ And tabby bishops
in their hats were plentiful as shovel cats ; ” “ And shovels
in their bishop cats were tabbiful as plenty hats; ”
“ And tabbies in their catful shoves were bishop-
ful as plenty hats;” “And plenty bishops
in their cats were tabbiful as shovel hats;” “And
hatful bishops in their cats were tabbiful as plenty shoves ”;
“And shovels in their bishful cats were shoppiful as
—hie—plenty hats.” Just here the curtain came down,
but even after that a thick voice was heard saying some-
thing from behind about how “ Tabby hattops in their
bish were plentyshove as full of cats,” or words to that
effect. The result suggested something new in benefits.
Fancy an adv't. reading thus : “ Melbourne Hospital.—
With a view to lifting the debt off this deserving institu-
tion a great benefit performance of 1 Hamlet ’ will be
given at the Princess on Saturday night. All the leading
artists now in the city will take part. By way of lending
novelty to a drama which has become somewhat familiar
in the course of years all the gentlemen of the company
will be Drunk. It is expected that some perfectly new
lights will be cast on the text by accident. The ticket-
taker will be sober, but the lime-light man, scene-shifters
and stage-carpenters will all be paralytic. Seats may be
booked with a specially-intoxicated gentleman at ’s
music-shop. The local brewers have already taken up
two rows of stalls at 20s. each.”
He was a stage-manager famed in two provinces for the
volume and vigor of his slanguage, and she was a member
of the new chorus that was being hurriedly anathema-
tised into shape. He had worried and cursed himself into
a fever, more especially over the shy-looking maiden with
the tender eyes and the pale yellow hair, and she had
borne it uncomplainingly until, stung by her imperturba-
bility, the s.m. excelled himself in a tremendous burst of
eloquence for her especial benefit. Then suddenly did the
lady drop her back-hair, and let loose from her ruby lips
a torrent of such concentrated scorn as fairly took his
breath away. She didn’t swear at all. In a quiet, even
voice she told him that he was unfit to herd with dogs
and lepers ; that Judas Iscariot would shudder to be seen
in his company ; that his robust age was owing to the
.Tact, that Death was afraid to strike him for fear of being
dirtied by the contact, and his good health arose from the
circumstance that no disease dared come near him lest it
should be infected by something worse than itself. With
an angelic smile on her face, she told him that even hogs
would get off his grave in haste, and putrefaction would
pass him by in alarm. For a couple of minutes he stood
paralysed ;' then, as the lady was retiring triumphant, he
. yelled, “ Fetch that girl back ! ” She was brought back.
'“ Here,” cried he, “Take my job ' take my salary ! I’m
only fit for the blanky ballet! ”
A distinguished actor coming out from England does
not create so great a sensation now as in the olden time.
When G. V. Brooke landed at Saridridge (Vic.), in ’54, a
drag, drawn by six gaily-decorated horses, conveyed him
to the city ; and the line of route was crowded by shout-
ing multitudes of the sort that, in our day , reserve their
joyful bellowing for a rich Governor, who is supposed to
come among them to spend money. Even the fraud
Bandmann was wildly cheered the first time ha_poked his
well-oiled ringlets out of the manager’s box of Melb. Royal.
A lot of “unthinking thinkers who thought that they
thought,” believed that, because he was the countryman
of Goethe, Schlegel and Tieck he would present a perfect
Hamlet.
“ Old Jane ” : Re Helen Fawcett’s remark that G. V.
Brooke was incapable of bringing mind into the study of
a part. There is such a factor as instinct, which serves
genius as a royal road. A mental analysis may be neces-
sary to him whose perception does not instantly seize
upon the truth ; result, artistic mechanism. But what
people term inspiration produces that realism which no
mere intellectual effort can achieve. Brooke —common-
place and even dull off the stage—had a gift of intuition
which served him in place of mind-study. So had the
elder Kean ;so Booth ;so little- Robson. Also, they all
got drunk when the fit was over, and they had returned
to normal conditions. As to Brooke’s having- been a
failure in London, writer can remember when he took
that village by storm. But it didn’t last, for reasons not
necessary to particularise. Anyhow, the English stage
has not since seen the equal of Brooke as a tragedian, nor
in certain lines of comedy—lrish especially.
When Sarah Bernhardt was performing in Melbourne a
few years ago, the principal of a fashionable ladies’ board-
ing school took 12 of her pupils to the theatre one even-
ing in order that they might receive an irreproachable
French lesson. To the surprise of most people, the play
selected for the improvement of the girls was “ Camille.”
It did not matter, however, because they obviously never
understood a word of the rapid dialogue.
“J.A.C.”: The late Lance “ Lenton ” (with whom I
was intimately acquainted) was a son of the late Dr.
Keen, of Hobart, Tas. (Dr. Keen’s erstwhile Tas. partner,
Dr. Crooke, is still in active practice in Sydney at the
venerable age of about 90 years), and began his theatrical
career by playing the drum in the orchestra with
“Wizard Oil” Frank Weston’s minstrels, after which he
was assistant to Chapman, conjuror, at St. George’s Hall,
Melb. As he was an excellent mimic, and had intuitively
picked up a knowledge of dancing, he next got an engage-
ment at “Charlie” Wright’s, Colosseum, Melb., where
he did nigger apd Dutch song-and-dance business. Next
he was harlequin in a pantomime, and later on joined
Hegarty’s Variety Co. in Sydney. His first essay in
dramatic business was with Coppin and Greville. He also
-showed considerable literary talent, and wrote some very
smart parodies. He was a lineal descendant of Sir
Lancelot Shadwell, Vice-Chancellor of England in 1827,
and twice Commissioner of the Great Seal. Lance Lenton
was a true Bohemian and a good fellow. R.I.P. !
“ Nolo ”: The native drama has not yet made much head-
way in Australia, though 25 years ago W. M. Akhurst was
very successful in Melb. in the composition of some musi-
cal burlesques of the old H. J. Byron school. Garnet
Walch followed in his steps with inferior results ; and
from time to time local dramatic authors have tried their
hands at adaptation of novels. “ Robbery Under Arms,”
and “ For the Term of his Natural Life” hold the stage
with Alfred Dampier’s Co., but they are not of a particu-
larly high merit. George Darrell’s melodramas will
be chiefly remembered in the future on account of his
claim that he was the first to suggest, in “ The Forlorn
Hope,” the despatch of the Soudan contingent. It would
be difficult to point out a really good original play that
has been written by an Australian author, unless Haddon
Chambers is to be counted as one. The man of genius,
with a stirring drama.racy of the soil, has yet to arrive.
Brough has figured as lessee, or sub-lessee, of five show-
places in Melbourne. Firstly, the Opera House, where he
and Boucicault had the late H. R. Harwood for their
“associate-manager.” Then the old Bijou ; next the
•Hibernian Hall, for two or three weeks after the theatre
was burnt; then the new Bijou ; and, of late years, the
Princess’s occasionally. Williamson, as a member of the
Firm and “ on his own,” has touched six places—Royal,
old Princess’s, Bijou, new Princess’s, Athenaeum Hall, and
Her Majesty’s (late Alexandra). Musgrove, who has been
epneerned in all the J.C. W. specs, except Her Majesty’s,
is an e'x-lessee of the Opera House. Rickards has taken a
turn at St. George’s Hall, old Opera House, Gaiety-, Bijou,
and the Princess’s, and is now building a new Opera
House (or Tivoli, as it will prdbably be called). Bland
Holt has tried the Royal, the Alexandra, and the Prin-
cess’s ; and George Rignold is credited with seasons at the
old Opera House, the Royal, and the Princess’s.
Ohas. Arnold, a Swiss by birth, studied law in America
before he took to the stage. He is often spoken of as an
“ only-one-line-of-characler ” man, but, as a matter of
fact, Hans the Boatman went through the mill years ago,
playing dialect parts in drama, principal parts in comic
opera, and any parts he could get in Shakespeare and the
“legitimate.” He owns up to having been manager in
front of the house, and utility actor for a big stock co.
25 y-ears ago, and sometime before he struck the “ Hans”
reef he was running his own show. In the character Of
man-of-the-world, Arnold shines brightest.
“G. 8.”: Ayvay back in the ’so’s there existed in
Hobart an amateur dramatic club consisting mostly- of
shipwrights. On one occasion they put up “ The Lady of
Lyons,” and the man that played Claude—coining on with
a bellow of “ Give me joy, dear mother, I’ve won the
prize ’’—tripped in the ragged carpet, shot head-foremost
into the orchestra, knocked over the- leader, and broke
his fiddle ; whereupon said leader punched Olaude on the
eye. Claude’s friends in the pit rushed to the rescue;
the orchestra backed its man, and for a few minutes
Sheol reigned. Peace being at length restored, the play
proceeded, and by the time Claude came on to do the
wooing biz. he had an eye on him like a pickled walnut.
And mighty was the guffaw that went up to the roof
when Claude returned from the wars, with a lip, also,
like a well-filled sausage, and strove to hide those marks
of strife with his military cloak. The farce that followed
the “Lady” was dry hash by comparison.
They called each other “cully” with that familiar per-
sistence dear to the average “ pro.” Lona' out of a job
and heavily in arrear with board, they finally “ flew the
coop,” and the long-suffering landlady was again “ left.”
Some time after two other “ pros.” came to look at the
rooms with a view to residence. Being satisfied as to
their suitability, one said to the other, “ This’ll do, cully,
eh ? ” But the landlady intimated that she had had
“ two of the Cully family before and wouldn’t stand any
more of ’em.”
“Via”: Re “The Only Way.” Why has Dickens’
“ Great Expectations ” never been staged ? The dramatic
stumbling-blocks seem smaller than in any other of his
works. Wonder it was not done years back. For ex-
ponents, the original B. and B. co. would have got a
bull’s-eye every time. Imagine Mrs. B. as Miss Havisham,
Brough as the loutish Joe Gargery, Dot B. as the priggish
Pip, Tith. as Jaggers, Cecil Ward as the convict Magwich,
and Emma Temple as Stella.
A mean dodge is worked by some quasi-society people
to secure the boxes at theatres without paying the usual
fee. Turning up late thej' claim seats in the front row of
the circle, already occupied, and, when told that theirs
are in another place, they immediately proceed to make a
scene, and aver in loud and ungrammatical sentences
that they intend “seeing about it” in the morning. To
avoid trouble, the management will generally shove the
party into a box ; and it is a peculiar coincidence that
scenes never occur if the box happens to be occupied.
Jefferson made a competence out of Rip Van Winkle,
his favorite character, though Salem Scudder in “ The
Octoroon” was his best part. While he was one of the
most natural actors that ever trod the boards, he
could not compete against first-rate English performers
in such parts as Dr. Pangloss and Tony Lumpkin.
The Americans are patriotic in their dealings with
dramatic favorites. When Louise Pomeroy was in Aus-
tralia she told her friends that she always did her best in
her own country when following on the tracks of Adelaide
Neilson. The English actress having had a splendid
reception in the town, the people said, “ We must roll up
for Pomeroy and take care that bur native talent is just
as well recognised.” Australian patriotism is not yet
developed to that extent.
A voice from a N.S.W. country town: —“Visited the
local Oddfellows’ Hall t’other night to see Cinematograph
with alleged pictures taken at Ladysmith. One depicted
a shell bursting in a stable, and man leading out two fat
horses with spanking harness on—they looked as though
they had just been groomed for the brewer’s dray. Of
course it was a palpable fake, but the long-haired Ananias,
in his introductory remarks, told the audience how the
poor, starved brutes were rescued for food. And, as a
sample of what the public will stand, he wound up like
this : ‘ You people in the country perhaps wonder how
these pictures are obtained. Well, I’ll tell you. We
secure a few mules and attach the cine, on their backs
and send them away broadcast. Some return, some get
shot ; those that return are examined, and any good pic-
tures, such as battles, fires, &c., are preserved and
brought to Australia and shown as you have seen them
to-night.’ And the audience sat still and said as - one
man, ‘Well, fancy that!’ That lecturer had got his
story upside-down. It isn’t asses that take the cinema-
tographe war-pictures. It is asses who pay to see them.’
Dear Bulletin. —Probably a jealous mummer started
the yarn that Irving’s .eccentricities of pronunciation
were suggested to him by manager Bateman “ when he
made his first London appearance at the Lyceum ” as
a means of exciting controversy and calling attention to
the actor. Irving’s first London appearance was made
year’s before Bateman (a firm believer in his powers) gave
him the chance to play Mathias in “ The Bells.” Also,
the Irvingesque tricks of speech and gait had been
peculiar to the man in previous performances under other
London managers. He was an actor of note when he
took the part of Mr. Ghevenix, M.P., in “ Uncle Dick’s
Darling” at the Gaiety. He was almost famous by the
time he had finished playing Digby Grant in “ The Two
Roses,” at the Vaudeville, under the triumvirate manage-
ment of Henry Montague, David James and Tom Thorne.
And in both of those well-remembered successes Irving
walked and talked on the lines which took Bateman’s
fancy and gave that astute Yankee the idea of trying how
far the mannerist would go in a weird; character part.
To say. that Irving was “ faked ” into fame is ridiculous—
but, then a small mummer’s attempts to explain away
the triumphs of big mnmmers are almost invariably
foolish. —E.F.
“ Camille,” as “ La Dame Aux Camelias,” was first pro-
duced at the little Theatre des Varibt6s, Paris, in 1850,
with Madame Doche as Camille, and Fechter, a native of
Whitechapel, as Armand. That was the making of
Fechter, and awhile later he created in London the twin
characters of the Dei Franchi, in “Corsican Brothers.”
The study of English was not hard to him, but like most
Frenchmen, he never could wipe away the accent, his
talk sounding very like that of Bandmann—the German,
but not so bad. Yet Fechter, who played the part in a
flaxen wig, made an overwhelming hit with his Hamlet,
in London, influencing every Hamlet that followed.
Montgomery took the cue largely from him, and maybe
this was the reason why Montgomery’s Hamlet never
caught on so in London. He couldn’t well Fechterise
there, as in Australia.
A London critic draws attention to the fact that the
“ Pirates of Penzance ” opens on 29th February, a bitterly
cold season in England, and yet the girls wear summer
frocks and are about to paddle in the sea. He wants to
know how Gilbert came to make such a discrepancy.
While he is about it, he might also ask Gilbert how he
came to make the worse discrepancy of having any
pirates at Penzance at all. This writer arises to assure
the public in a deepl.v-serious spirit that there are no
pirates at Penzance except the one that sells you beer in
the local pub., and the pirate at the local branch bank,
and the buccaneer at the railway-station.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The “ Oxberry ” Spring meeting at Clarendon
(N.S.W.), Saturday, drew a profit-bringing crowd. Though
Mo. (as he always does) finished a bit in hand, he didn’t
fet quite the scoop that came his way at Rosehill previous
aturday. Accuracy of oft-repeated phrase, “ Horses for
courses,” was further verified when Lochiel-Lola mare
Jenny Moore won Flying Handicap (6fur.) easily in 1.15 J,
and, later, got home in Welter. Crack jocks F. Kuhn (in
Flying) and Steve Callinan were up. Same nag gathered
in a double at Autumn meeting of same club. Last Sat.
she started for first event at sevens—Lybian, Cast Iron
and Expectation 11. being better favorites, but none of
them had a hope from flag-fall. Jenny was top of the
market (3 to 1 agst) in Welter, and took the money com-
fortably by two lengths from unlucky Maranoa, who had
previously run second to Wooton’s Scientist. Cyanide
had most followers in Spring Handicap (ljmls. 100yds.).
Rock of Ages, St. Clare, Hi Hi, Butternut and Nevermore
were others legitimately supported. Butternut was in
front from end to end, but only squeezed home by a neck,
through sheer gameness, after a hot tussle with Rock of
Ages. Cyanide’s third doesn’t make her prospects in
Epsom or Metrop. look too rosy. Hi Hi—heavily backed
by owner (Metallician Beales) for to-day’s big mile, Tues-
day’s Metrop., and the double —shaped worse than the
favorite, as did others (Water-Color, the Bananalander
Malula, and Pelissier) fancied for coming big plums.
Butternut, who is by Eiridspord (imp.) from Buttercup
by Panic, and was bred by Mr. R. O. Allan, started 16
times last season and only won once.

Ampier hasn’t done anything here to warrant present
position in betting, and t’other day was badly beaten by

Remarkable ill-luck has befallen “ Turanville ”
stud this season. Only two mares have foaled up to date,
and in these cases Splendora’s twin foals to the speedy
Ruenalf died immediately after birth ; while the death of
Myril’s foal to the same sire was followed by that of the
mare herself. The lottery of blood-stock breeding !
* * * •
“T.S.” writes: S.A. law made another exhibition
of itself over its latest attempt to put down the illegal
bookmaker. The police employed agents, who went
round and made bets with the unlawful bag-carriers, and
then they prosecuted the long-nosed brigade wholesale.
But the defending lawyer asked these witnesses if they
intended to collect the money supposing they won, and
they replied that they didn’t—probably lest they should
be fined or imprisoned for illegally betting in a public
place. Then it was gravely submitted that as the person
making the bet didn’t intend to collect the money if he
won, it wasn’t a real, complete, bona-fide betting trans-
action, and the law agreed with this view and sent Moses
about his business. Next time, probably, the defence
will be that the bookie was only a welsher and didn’t
mean to pay the money if he lost, and therefore it
wasn’t a real, complete, bona-fide betting transaction,
and Moses will be sent about his business again. Also,
next time the informant will doubtless be careful to say
that he did intend to collect the money if he won, where-
upon he will be prosecuted for illegally betting in a pub-
lic place, and for conspiring to incite an innocent Moses
to do an unlawful action, the same being the laying of a
disgracefully short price on an animal that was stiff.
If the S.A.' Legislature is in real earnest I desire to
suggest a way out. Let a list be made of the names or
professed names of everj r known bookmaker and a law be
passed making it heavily punishable for any one of them
to be found at large in the province after a certain date.
Then if more bookies arise put their names in a similar
law next session, and keep on doing it. The law can
almost always come out on top if it is sufficiently in
earnest. The trouble is that it is hardly ever sufficiently
in earnest.
* * * »
The “unlawful-betting” prosecution has brought
out an interesting law-point at Adelaide. Paris Nesbit,
who defended, hit back with an information charging the
prosecuting constable with infringing the Act by making
a wager with one of his clients. Crown solicitor produced
the Governor’s pardon for his man. Nesbit argued that
the pardon was ineffective inasmuch as the constable had
pleaded “ not guilty,” and the charge had not yet been
proved against him. Held—following English authority
—that “ the King may pardon any crime before attainder
or conviction,” and “ if a penalty be given to him who
will sue for it, the King, before suit, may pardon the whole
suit.”
» * * •
Marvellous how pug. Cribb draws. Saturday
night Golden Gate Club (Sydney) was full to bulging-out
point. Tariff ranged from 10s. down to 35., hence sur-
plus over purse (£75) must have materially added to pro-
moter’s banking account. The Maorilander was let loose
at Melbourne Tot Higgins a second time, and made short
work of him. Tot shaped as if he wanted it brief and
merry, for instead of standing off and watching for, or
making, openings (as the cleverer boxer of any two would
do), he hurled himself at the M.L. man —made the fight
as Otto liked it—with the inevitable ending—the thing
was over inside 4mins. of actual slugging, and Cribb owned
another scalp, together with attendant coin. ’Twas a
wild and woolly go from the jump—all swing, rush, and
crash. In first round neither got anything really solid
home, though both landed somewhere often. Getting to
it again, Cribb set a recent gash over Higgins’ left eye
going, making Tot a gory spectacle, but only for a few
seconds, as, a little later, he caught one on the point
that sent him to the boards, where he stayed just a
moment too long to claim any further interest in the
proceedings. Writer has seen many bruisers who’d have
got over that punch inside half the time, but perhaps not
if they were tackling men who had beaten them unmis-
takably before, as was the case with Higgins.
* • « •
“Jab”: The way the second meeting ’tween
Cribb and Higgins (Sat. night) was boomed by some of the
papers would be amusing if ’twere not so sickening.
E. News of said date printed that the two put up an
interesting battle before, but owing to Higgins not being
in the best of condition his seconds were compelled to sky
the towel. “ To-night both will be in best of condition and
battle should be a good one with exciting situations from
start to finish.” Star told a hungering public that
“These two warriors met before and it was one of the
best ever seen in Sydney, (a large order). The Melbourne
man not being in good condition was forced to give in.
This time they are both real well; it should be a great
battle, one that will bristle with exciting situations from
beginning to end.” Surely both pars emanated from one
source? The truth, of course, is that the previous battle
was anything but enthrallingly interesting, for Higgins
kept on the run the whole time, and instead of his seconds
throwing up the sponge he took the bit in his teeth and
insisted upon doing it himself—wanted to do it two
rounds before, in fact, though in first class trim and good
enough to go another 10 rounds had he owned the spunk
and been lucky enough to dodge a knock-out; but Cribb
scared him badly. Small wonder Sydney is getting rather
tired of a game boomed in this misleading fashion,
* • * *
M.L. bruiser Otto Cribb and Mick Dunn were
matched this week for N.S.W. middle-weight supremacy
(which Dunn holds); £lOO in stakes, and a purse of a
similar amount given by Gaiety A.C. (Sydney). Fight has
been fixed for Oct. 1. Otto will find Dunn the toughest
he has yet faced, and can look forward to being set a
merry jig round the G.A.C.’s 20ft. square throughout the
20 rounds stipulated, unless Dunn outs him previously;
for, as Otto couldn’t catch Sturgeon, writer doesn’t see
how he’s going to corner wily Mick. But that’s to be
seen. Cribb is coming on at every go. Erstwhile Cap-
tain’s Flat miner Dan O’Connell is waiting for a shy at
Otto and will have it whether latter wins or loses with
Dunn.

* * * •
Cable last week told that world’s champion
bruiser Jim Jeffries had refused to fight Fitzsimmons
Aug. 31, and latter notified his “ permanent retirement.”
Fact of Fitz. insisting upon date named was due to the
law prohibiting fighting in N’York taking effect from next
day. According to latest Yankee files Jeffries’ right arm
was in plaster, so that he could hardly face the music at
such short notice. Nobody’ll look upon Bob’s announced
retirement seriously ; it’s about the third time he’s been
taken that way. Old Bill Beach is the only champ, who
ever did the retiring act properly.
**' » *
Coming ’Stralian champion man of thew “ Sam-
son,” now in M.L., met local crack Newton at Auckland
recently. Despite Samson being challenger, Newton
went on first with sheer lifts of from 56 to 2001 b. above
head—all done rather easily. Samson couldn’t follow
some, but subsequently came along with his own lot, and
most of ’em beat Newton. Referee here declared scores
equal. Newton now set a tough go ; while prostrate he
raised 2001 b. from back of head, stood erect, and resumed
recumbent position again. Samson cried ago here.
Referee’s verdict went to Newton. Samson contends he
didn’t get a square deal—his challenge was for Newton to
perform same feats as he (Samson) did nightly in Auck-
land.
» * * *
Olympian Games in Paris didn’t impress Stan.
Rowley. He says in a letter that “to treat these events
as world’s championships is a farce. Most of the com-
petitors take them as a huge joke. Thought of having
come from Australia to compete makes one feel ridiculous.
The tracks are (bar none I’ve ever seen) the worst in the
world ; there’s a dust footpath running right across them
at 30yds. Starter was a Frenchman. Foreigners be-
haved like jumping-jacks on their marks, and invariably
got yards the better of the gun. Americans and myself
decided to do likewise, with the result that in every heat
you’d see one man beat the report badly, and tear along
syds. in front of the others. No foreigner got placed in
100 metres first round, and, as Yankees and myself were
left in the second round, we clamored for an English
starter —and got one.”
• • • *
Latest particulars anent 100 metres world’s
supremacy at Paris Olympian games are that the four left
in final were Yankees Duffy, Tewkesbury and Jarvis and
’Stralian Stan. Rowley. Duffy fell at 30yds. (where the
dust-path crossed the track), but didn’t look like win-
ning. Jarvis breasted the worsted by a foot from Tewkes-
bury who was 6in. in front of Rowley. Latter is confi-
dent that if distance had been 120yds. instead of 100
metres (110yds.) he would have settled Tewkesbury and
probably Jarvis. Yale ’Varsity trainer said if he had
Rowley in hand for a while he’d make him top o’ the
heap. Appears now Rowley was third, not second in 60
metres—a foot behind Tewkesbury whom Kraenzlein beat
by 6in.
• * * *
“ Spike ”: There not much ’Ome about England
for Australian premier sprinter, Stan. Rowley, who writes
by last week’s mail: “ English A. A. A. have not up to
now shown me one little bit of courtesy ; in fact, I have
only seen the sec. when I’ve casually visited a sports
meeting. The only letter he wrote me was one saying I
owed 10s. entrance-fees for English A.A.A. championship
meeting. I only got one ticket for championship gather-
ing and that was kept back till day before.” Nice, hos-
pitable people, those down-under “ Amachaws.” Leaving
Rowley religiously alone (a stranger in a strange land) is
bad enough in all conscience, but billing him for entrance-
fees is piling it on thick. Wonder they didn’t make the
gentlemanly Stan, pay at the gate 1

Latest mailed news re lengthy wrangle between
’Stralian scullers Towns and Wray over English supre-
macy is that Wray gave way and agreed to the Hunter

• * * . *
Fact of ’Stralians Wray and Towns going to row
for English championship Monday, recalls that Saturday
last was 14th anniversary of the big Sculling Carnival
promoted and engineered by London News op the World
W. J. Innes. Beach and Matterson were Australian reps.,
Teemer, Wallace Ross and Lee U.S., and Bubear and
Perkins British. Dapto (N.S.W.) warrior Beach gathered
in first money (£1334), Teemer second, and Matterson
third. Canucks Gaudaur and Hanlan were on the spot,
but wouldn’t enter; while Peter Kemp (N.S.W.) had to
drop out through lack of condition.
