Sprint wonders Fred Lane and Jack Hellings are expected
back from London, also Leo (whilom champion of Aus-
tralasia), who was among the defenders of Mafeking.
Followers of the sport are looking forward to some hot
tussles, as ’tis anticipated that “Mud” Bishop, Dick
Cavill, and Bob Craig will set the returning cracks a
lively go in all premierships from 100yds. up.
* * * - *
Arthur Eales, of N.S.W., goes to England
next February, to shoot against the best there. He says
he had great confidence in his match with Massey, that
he was dead-sure he’d last, because he trained properly.
His training was 10, 12, 18 miles a day in the field with
the gun, shooting everything that got up. Field shoot-
ing, he says, is the game to make you quick and sure
with the second barrel, because if two birds get up, you
have to be quick with the second barrel to get both
birds. He’ll shoot Massey again if Massey likes —at the
Gun Club, Botany—but he won’t go to Yass, where
Massey wants him to go. He says it would be just as
reasonable for him (Eales) to insist on Massey going to
Morpeth to shoot.
• •
First interprovincial baseball match ’tween Vic.
and N.S.W. at Sydney Cricket Ground only drew a fair
crowd (rain probably made some difference) but interest
was sustained throughout. It isn’t at all unlikely that
the Yankee craze will take on directly that ’Stralians
become versed in its fine points. Laver, M‘Allister,
Ingleton and Kemp, who toured U.S. as members of that
much-joked-about Vic. baseball team, pulled the market-
garden province through. Vic. started well and early. Home
players’ combination in stealing bases was effective at
times and in final innings they made a good recovery, but
too late to be of service. Boehm and Iredale did fine
work, while Jones and Diamond—on first and third bases
—gave t’other province’s reps, a set back often. Vic.
pitchers—Laver and Ellis—opened the eyes of onlookers
as well as bats. Laver’s “ drop balls ” were very trouble-
some. At one stage scores were 9 —nil in favor of visitors.
Sixth innings’ results showed Vic. 11, N.S.VV. 6. Nothing
more was notched on either side till eighth and ninth
innings when home men added 3 to their total. Play
ceased : Vic. 8 innings 11 runs ; N.S.W., 9 innings 9 runs.
Other two matches of rubber were to be decided Wednes-
day and Saturday this week.
• » • *
Football struggle (Saturday) on Brisbane Cricket
Ground—N.S.W. u. Q. —went to latter by Bto nil. Score
was accomplished in initial half and within smin. of spell.
Spragg secured first try and converted, and Henry got the
second. Q. forwards did great work, forcing the game by
a series of brilliant rushes throughout which Colton and
Carew shone. Backs also shaped well, especially
M‘Cowan. Rain marred attendance (which only numbered
2000) considerably and affected play a deal. N.S.W. pass-
ing was of a high order, despite ball being very wet and
slippery. Herein Halloran, Blaney, Smith and M‘Mahon
made themselves conspicuous while the effectiveness of
Joyce and Judd’s play among forwards was very notice-
able.
• * * *
Saturday’s makes 21st football match ’tween
N.S.W. and Q. —decided in Brisbane. First was played
17 years ago. Mother province has won 10, Q. 9, and two
have been drawn. Of last five, Q. got away with three,
N.S.W. one, and the fifth was drawn.
* • * *
Most important Rugby match in Sydney last
Saturday was played at Agricultural Society’s ground
’tween Northern (Newcastle) and Metropolitan branches.
Visitors came out atop by 14 to 9, and deserved the vic-
tory. Backs defended well, while forwards brought a
heap of power to hear in the scrums. Game was some-
what loose, but nevertheless interesting.
* » * *
N.S.W. great public schools’ football premier-
ship went to Newington College—with seven wins and
one defeat. Points scored totalltd 163, only 17 being
compiled by opposing sides. Sydney Grammar and King’s
Schools tied for second—five wins and three losses each.

Re a possibly misleading par. in issue of
25/8/’OO, The Bulletin is now reliably informed that crack

cricket and sports business in Adelaide.
• * ♦ •
Cabled that Yorkshire has come out top in
English county cricket. The Tykes owe their success to all-
round play rather than to the possession of cracks. To
illustrate, their top batsman, Hirst, was only 12th on the
averages, the next, Taylor, being as far down as 23rd.
Rhodes, however is top in bowling, and Haigh third.
Lord Hawke has now captained the team for 13 years.
* « • ■<
A. E. Trott was nowhere in the English bow-
ling averages when last mail left, although it has since
been cabled that he has captured 200 wickets.
* * * *
W. G. Grace took part in one of the Gentlemen
v. Players matches this season, and made top score. But,
of course, he has now ceased to rank as an indispensable.
He gets more and more bulky with advancing years, and
loses a lot of runs by his slowness ’tween wickets.
• * * »
Not a bad game being an English pro. cricketer.
Mold’s benefit last month panned out £2500. The Lanca-
shire bowler is 35 years old, and must be going off now.
He has never been to Australia.
* * * *
C. B. Fry made a new cricket record last month
in the match Sussex v. Surrey by hitting up 125 and 229.
The only player who has gone near this was Stoddart, who
several years ago made 195 and 124 at Lords.
* * * *
Cyclists will be interested to learn that not a
single tube is allowed to leave the Dunlop Company’s
factory until it has been subjected to exhaustive tests.
After the tubes have been tested in shallow pans of water
they are taken out, slightly inflated, and hung in rows on
racks for a few days, and then again finally examined
before leaving the premises.
The Maoriland law-courts have taken the
Styche sensational-novel story seriously enough to
commit Styche for trial on a charge of attempt-
ing to procure the murder of his wife, and yet the
cheap and unreal Wilkie-Collinsism of the story
so far impressed them that they let the accused
out on moderate bail. At the preliminary hearing
the chief evidence was that of the doctor who was
offered by some anonymous correspondent the
billet of family murderer to the Styche household,
which favorable opening first presented itself in a
type-written letter to this effect:
Christchurch, June 4, 1930. —Dear Doctor, —The writer
of this letter will not give you her name, but simply tell
you that she is a woman. What lam going to write to
you I depend on your keeping a secret to the end of your
days, and I will do the same. I know a woman who is
continually ailing off and on, but she hates doctors, and
consequently has not consulted one for a very long time,
and I know that her husband has often wanted to call one
in, but she objects, and I once advised him to call you in,
and I think I could persuade him to do so if I go carefully
and cautiously to work, as he told me once that his wife
told him that she would just as soon die as be continu-
ally ailing as she is. What I wish co tell you is this : If
you are called in to attend this woman, and she doesn’t
recover, I will pay you two hundred pounds. Bear in
mind, you will never know who I am, so you will not be
able to get me into trouble over this, if you wish to, and
it is not likely that I will ever peach on you, as, if I
did, I should get myself into trouble as well; so you
quite understand that you have only to play your
cards well, and be able to give a certificate of death to
make the £2OO your own, and if the woman dies I will
post you the money in bank notes, but not before she
has been dead (say) three months, as I shall have to sell
some of my property to raise that sum, but I will be true
to my word, and do ic, and you must be true to yours, or
have' nothing to do with it. I might tell you that the
woman has no children dependent on her. Of course, I
must make myself secure, so will not give you the chance
of finding out who I am by writing to me, so, if you are
ready to make £2OO easily, put an advertisement in the
Press as follows“ Wanted to buy, a good dog-cart.
Apply Medico, Press office.” If you agree, put the ad-
vertisement in the paper either on Saturday, June 9, or
on June 16 or 23.—Unknown.
The medico didn’t reply, and another letter arrived
as follows: —
Dear Doctor, —I did not see your advertisement in
Saturday’s Press, and I hope you are not lacking in pluck
enough to make £2OO so easily, so I will look in the Press
every day between now and Saturday, June 23, for it, so
don’t be afraid. I will be true to my word, and pay up if
the event happens. You may never get such an easy
chance of making £2OO, and with no risk whatever. I
know that the woman’s husband thinks she will not re-
cover from her present illness, so he would not think that
anything was wrong when it happens. I have had a
little experience in nursing, and my idea of how you
should act is to tell the woman she must go to bed for a
week or two ; you can then give her medicine that will
not make her improve, and then call one evening and give
her something that includes the power to send her to
sleep for ever. You may, of course, know a better way than
this, but in any case you may rely on me for secrecy for life,
and to be true to my word. Perhaps you will think I am
terrible, but I am desperate, so for God’s sake do what I
ask. If you do not advertise between now and June 23 I
will conclude you do not want to make the money, so I
will then sell up and clear out of the country. In case
you have lost or burnt the advertisement, it is this :
“Wanted to buy, a good dog-cart; apply Medico, Press
Office.” Insert it in the Press. I will not write to you
again, but if I have occasion to address an envelope to
you it will contain nothing but this £2OO in notes. ' I am
to you and to all, for ever, Unknown.
This time the doctor advertised for the dog-cart,
and two days later he received a letter from
Styche offering to sell him a piebald horse, and
stating that Mrs. S. was ill and would call in next
day to consult him. The letter was not an answer
to the anonymous “ Medicus ” about the dog-cart;
it was a direct offer to sell the doctor a horse, and
it stated that the writer had heard, per medium of
the baker, that the doctor had a craving for such
an animal. A month later a letter from Styche
complained that Mrs. S. was getting no better.
Later still another anonymous type-written com-
munication incited the doctor to hurry up and
earn his £2OO. There was no visible connection
between “ Unknown ” about the dog-cart and
Styche about the horse, but by piecing
all these things together, and by the aid
of a mechanist who gave it as his fixed
opinion that the type-written letters were
done on a certain ancient and battered machine
in Styche’s office, the law became sufficiently
interested to run Styche in. At the same
time, the man who expected any doctor
to commit murder on the chance of receiv-
ing £2OO from an unknown party after three
months’ delay, was such an impracticable dreamer
that he could hardly be taken seriously, and it
seemed rather as if he were practising with a view
to becoming dramatic author to Billolloway.
Even if he was real, and if he tackled the crime
himself with an axe, such a mentally-unfinished
person was almost certain to miss his aim and
only wreck a picture, or a cat, or a ceiling. The
whole thing read so like the Incriminating Docu-
ment in a bad Bill Hollowayism that the court
had to sit on a thistle to make quite sure it was
awake, and even then it was less positive than it
could have wished.
The Bulletin has received the following
from the attorneys of R. Bell and Co. :
Some recent paragraphs in your paper in reference to
the manufacture of matches in Maoriland demand some
protest, and a brief statement of the facts may interest
you. In your issue of Ist inst. you state that Messrs.
R. Bell and Co. received a Government subsidy of £l5OO
“to start the match-making industry in Maoriland.’
This is untrue, and palpably ridiculous. Messrs. R. Bell
and Co.’s factory in Maoriland was started in July, 1895,
under precisely the same duties as the Dunedin factory,
which had been working for some time. The statements
in regard to Messrs. Bell’s treatment of factory girls are
grossly unfair, as the girls brought from London were not
intended to, nor did they, displace any local girls. The
former are paid exactly the name rate as the latter, and it
is a fact that the wages paid by Messrs. Bell and Co. com-
pare most favorably with those paid for any factory-work
in the province, and that far more than a living-wage can
be made by any ordinarily industrious girl. The fact
that all hands are on piecework, and that many of the
girls employed have for years made good wages, speaks
for itself. The idle and incompetent will never make
good wages at anything. The factory in question is one
of the finest of its kind in the world, and no possible
objection can be taken to the industry if the facts are
studied. Messrs. Bell and Co. have no “sweating policy ”
and no “ cheap labor traditions,” and the English records
will show that their factories are deemed as nearly as pos-
sible perfect in regard to the comfort of their employes.
Knowing your love of fairplay, and that you will always
support properly-conducted local industry, you can at
any time, if you desire it, obtain proof that the damaging
statements complained of are uncalled-for, and unworthy
of a fair-minded Protectionist paper.

T. C. C. SAUNDERS, Accountant.
London, 11th July, 1900.
We beg to report that we have verified the cash, investments, bills of exchange, and other assets
in London, and have had access to the accounts sent to London from the various branches in the
colonies, and having examined the same, and the books and accounts of the Loudon office, we are of
opinion that the above balance-sheet is a full and fair balance-sheet, properly drawn up so as to ex-
hibit a true and correct view of the state of the company’s affairs as shown by the books of the
company.
WHINNEY, SMITH and WHINNEY
(Chartered Accountants), Auditors.
During the early stages of the South
African war Premier Salisbury laid the hand of a
true British gentleman on his waistcoat, and de-
clared on his honor that Britain sought no increase
of territory. Then she took the Orange State and
is obviously going to take the Transvaal. Russia
said the same thing re China, and has already
laid hands on a piece of Manchuria. And British
papers are deeply reproachful about the unrelia-
bility of the Muscovite person.

The Impossible.
Is the creation of ignorance; it is the excuse
of the man who cannot understand, and
wherever education comes, there the dogpna of
impossibility dies. “It is impossible,” said our
fathers, more ignorant than their sons, because
earlier in the world’s history than we, “toharness
the lightning—to float iron—to make iron wheels
grip an iron road —to command the sun —to be
despots of the wind ’’—and to-day we attempt air-
ships ; we photograph colors; we drive loco-
motives eighty miles an hour ; we flash a thought
from meridian to meridian with little less than
a thought’s speed ; we collect the atoms of a
thunderbolt in a carbon point, and produce the
only light that can penetrate to the depths of our
old-time ignorance. Medicine triumphs over the
knife —the surgeon-butcher abdicates to the
physician. We solve, we do not destroy. Some
medical men even now say that it is impossible to
cure Bright’s disease of the kidneys. The pro-
prietors of Warner’s Safe Cure beg to differ, and
in the face of the facts of the many cures of
Bright’s disease through the use of Warner’s Safe
Cure, who will venture to contradict ? Read the
following from a physician of established repute,
being a sample of many more on file of similar
tenor.
Dr. Robert Shaw, L.S.D., M. 8., Rockhampton,
Q., says: “I have much pleasure in stating that!
prescribed Warner’s Safe Cure very frequently
whilst acting as Medical Officer on the Cairns-
Herberton Railway contract, and obtained more
satisfactory results from it than any formulae of
my own prescribing.”*
The Bulletin is reminded—with refer-
ence to some recent remarks about Tom Playford
and coloured labor—that it was Playford’s Govern-
ment which, in ’BB, got together the Sydney
Chinese Conference ; and is assured that Playford
has ever since been steadfast in his antagonism
to both yellow labor and black.
Editor Bulletin. —Have the goodness to
give our emphatic denial to “Cuss’s” statement
(B. 14/7/’00) that we are losing sheep on Yandama
at the rate of $5O per week through wild dogs.—
Richard Dawes, for R. B. Dawes and Co. [The
information in question was supplied, and printed,
under the signature of a correspondent who has
hitherto proved reliable, but its accuracy, under
the circumstances, was not vouched for.—Ed. B.]
Reported by the daily press that the two
outlawed black Governors are, at time of writing,
“doing pretty much as they like,” and setting the
law at nought, and making a good many thousand
people shake in their shoes. Isn’t the Governor
disgrace just about balancing the “ glory ” gained
by the Cornstalks in Africa ? It is all very good
to talk about the heroism of the man who has gone
to the Transvaal; he is too far away to be in-
spected. It is the painful want of heroism in the
man who didn’t go that is the trouble.
The Guide-Book to Japan, issued by the
Nippon Yusen Kaisha (Japanese Mail S.S. Co.),
is a neatly got-up and beautifully-printed pub-
lication, containing every possible information for
the use of intending passengers, and abounding in
capital photographs of what is most noteworthy
to be seen, not only in “ Rising-Sun Land ” itself,
but also upon the pleasant voyage thither.*
Amidst the whirl of the Melb. Poster Ball
several ladies were charmingly conspicuous by
reason of a wealth of glittering hair “a-hanging
down their backs.” For this gracious display of
“woman’s crowning glory,” E. Holland, proprietor
of the “ Marvellous Hair Restorer,” was respon-
sible. Holland’s Restorer, by the way, has
caught-on in Europe, America and England.*
Allan and Co. were an easy first in the
prize competition at the Melb. Poster Ball. Their
set was conspicuous for its artistic simplicity.
Four charming ladies, clad in bars of music, repre-
sented a harmony in white satin. Said silvery
harmony, though not audible, delighted all the
senses by its sweet visibility. This happy design
was the inspiration of clever young Tait, one of
the firm’s directors.*
In hair-dressing, wig-making, &c., Mr.
W. H. Wigzell, of 110 William-street, Sydney,
holds a first position. He makes a speciality of
hair-working, importing the hair from London,
Germany, and France, and he employs a large
and able staff in making it up to suit each
customer’s requirements. He has Ladies requisites
in infinite variety, and any lady in the country,
who wants her needs attended to with every care
and despatch, should write to Mr. Wigzell.*
There has been a great and disastrous
hurricane at Mafeking. Some of the Baden-
Powell advertising-gas hustling round, no doubt.
N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency Co. reports
that London advises an advance of 6d. per quarter for
wheat at Mark Lane, and cargoes have been placed at a
corresponding hardening of rates. So far the local mar-
ket is as flabby as ever. Shipping freights and exes, pre-
vent large operations, and millers do not speculate beyond
actual requirements. The co. reports some quotations •
Prime samples, 2s. 9d. to 2s. 9Jd. ; Good, 2s. Bd. to
2s. BJd. ; Pinched and chickwheat, 2s. 3d. to 2s. 6d. per
bushel. Coming harvest still seems likely to break all
previous records.

London Outlook has discovered a quality in Lord
“ Fauntleroy ” Hopetoun hitherto quite unsuspected by
his gushing Melb. admirers. According to the. print in
question, the coming G.G. frivols as an amateur in comic
opera. The paper does not say in what department the
lordling performs, but, remembering the general tendency
of the British peerage, The Bulletin has a horrible sus-
picion that the first Governor of the Commonwealth is an
Earl who feels that he has a natural call to the Ballet.
Lovely balderdash from S.M. Herald re music at the
Sydney King Humbert commemoration.: “ The full
orchestra rises at times, however, to just such an
anguished outburst of rageful and mutinous sorrow as
the present senseless crime might excite.”
“ S.S.” : The fortnightly free concerts started by Mayor
M'Eacharn at Melb. Town Hall, and run at Smalcolm’s own
expense, an immense success, according to the
appreciative daily press. Certainly they have drawn
great crowds, and they have been no end of a boon, if it
was Mister Mayor’s intention to provide cheap shows for
that section of the Melb. public which can best afford to
pay to see acrobatic musicians hammer the piano, and
hear songsters hunting top notes round the rafters. But
if Smalcolm’s intention was to give a sort of musical eight
o’clock rush to those people who cannot pay for a shil-
ling’s worth of music without going short of a shilling’s
•worth of something more substantial, he would be edified
to see these latter held back in the streets by fat police-
men whilst the “ higher orders ” troop in on the glorious
nod and jump all the accommodation.
Hundreds, not to say thousands, were turned away
from the doors of Her Majesty’s, Melb., on the Nance
O’Neil farewell night. And those who were not turned
away got the most barnstorming entertainment of the
season for their trouble. Nance as Partjienia, Nance as
Bill Sikes’s girl (not the gory scene), and Nance in her
amateurish attempt to play old Meg Merrilees simply made
the uninfatuated smile. As the “Jewess,” a character
she played in Sydney before the press booming had begun
to work the public, Nance shouted with much success.
Her “ curse ’’-scene seemed to give the audience a genuine
knock—the first and last suspicion of a genuine knock
that Melbourne experienced during the season. As a
hearty uproar, accompanied by an energetic display of
not inappropriate gesture, Nance’s curse was all right,
though it gave little idea of the full possibilities of the
scene. Miss Bateman, of yore, could shake a multitude
of people out of their seats with that same curse. “ Leah ”
Bateman—an actress of no great importance in any other
part—was convincing, as well as strong, when she cursed,
so much so that the late Wills (author of “Charles the
First ”) wrote a- “ curse ” play specially for her. Nance is
never convincing. There is nothing at the back of her
voice. But she has great physical strength and self-
confidence. She lets herself “ go.” And if she serves to
encourage other girls to stretch out their arms and roar,
Nance will have some claim to our gratitude.
Now we have the inevitable rumor that the “ aston-
ishing of Miss Nance O’Neil in Melbourne (but
why ‘ astonir .ig’ if she were only half as good as the
stereo’d press appears to think she is?) has encour-
aged two or three of the greatest American actors to
contemplate an Australian tour.” A male actor is a
very different matter to a 15-stone Nance, anyhow, so
if the contemplative persons are men, The Bulletin
would advise them to think of Nat Goodwin, and pause.
And if there are any lady “stars” making arrange-
ments to come along and show this country the true
meaning of histrionic art, The Bulletin would advise
them to pause likewise. Melbourne lost its head over
Amy Castles, the Bendigo girl, but when a gifted young
Australian artist in Maud M'Carthy came along from
London with her violin, to walk in the tracks of Amy’s
phenomenal success, she was ignored. The public had
just begun to realise its previous foolishness, and had
Maud been an angel singing songs of Heaven to her own
harp accompaniment the public, at that time, would not
have rushed the show. They were then suffering a re-
covery from their Castles illusion. A gifted American
actress might strike Australia just when it was recovering
from it’s O’Neil illusion.
“ Seesaw Melb. Age critic, re Nance O’Neil’s “fare-
well ” appearance in Melbourne, congratulates himself on
his early prophecy that Nance “ is destined to recogni-
tion in the world’s centres as having been born to the
imperial purple of the stage.”. ’Also, he thinks “it will
be held an honor to Melbourne to have been the scene of
her first startling triumph.” All of which is very in-
teresting to people who have watched the manufacture of
the Nance Boom, and the credulity of the Melbourne
public. The Age critic states indirectly that the large,
loud lady had not been a startler previous to her Mel-
bourne season. What a satire on the Age para-
graph of June 2 : —“ Miss Nance O’Neil, the celebrated
American emotional actress, will make her first
appearance in Melbourne this evening. Miss O’Neil is
one of the recognised leaders of the American stage.”
Somehow the Age and all the other newspapers whose
“ critics ” and interviewers have talked about Nance’s
imperial purpleism and forthcoming conquest of Europe,
&c., &c., have given us nothing about the fact that Nance
was matineed in London on June 27th last year. Did
they (the Melb. critics) know of this important fact before
their personal acquaintance with Nance had begun, and
if they didn't know of that expedition to London, did
Nance refer to it in the interviews? And if she did why
didn’t we hear something about it ?
J. W. Sweeney, popular and clever Sydney actor and
general good fellow, proposes, in conjunction with English
theatrical-manager G. J. Staples, to tour N.S.W. with a
dramatic co., strong in numbers and not weak in talent.
The home province finished with, the co.’s next “dart”
will be N.Q.
A noted fiddler, James Storragh, known as the “last
minstrel of Bungaree,” died recently in Ballarat hospital.
For years he was the recognised musician of the local
Irish settlement, which recalls the jibe of 0. E. (“ Mint-
sauce ”) Jones: “ Electors of Ballarat and Savages of
Bungaree! ”
Died at Inverell the other day Florence Hunter, who,
until she married a year or two ago, was, as Florence
Fowler, one of S. Australia’s most popular semi-profes-
sional singers.
When Dorothy Vane came to Australia—it’s two years
ago now—she told her interviewer that she did not like
men, “ although,” added Dorothy, “ men are very use-
ful.” Later on she talked about her own sex. “ I love
women,” said Dorothy, “ but best of all, I love a dear
little baby.”
“S.S.”; The most marked characteristic of Nance
O’Neil’s audiences at Melb. Majesty’s was the unwavering
constancy of the gallery. Biz. ran thin now and again in
other places, but the galleiy never once failed the majestic
O’Neil, and although the rarity of the gaudy bouquet that
is humped up by the cynical, conductor has been noted as
a peculiarity of the season, the gallery frequently dumped
its little pile of salad at the feet of the star with a swish
and a thud suggestive of anarchistic outrages, and the lob
of a muffled bomb. The affectionate regard of the gods
has been instrumental in determining Williamson to ex-
tend the accommodation of “ high Olympus.”
“Edson”; “Snorter”, in correcting me re Nance
O’Neil’s record of heavy parts points out Mrs. Bernard
Beere’s heavy run, but he overlooks the fact that the
American was dealing with some parts quite new to her—
Hedda Gabler and Tess were then in hand—whereas Mrs.
8.-B. played only stock characters with which she had
long been familiar.
W. E. Hammond, late of the Maoriland Observer and
N.Z. Sporting and Dramatic Review, is in Sydney as
business representative for the Ancelon Dramatic Co.,
which combination expects to shortly go on tour again.
It has been doing well in Maoriland for some time past.

Tasmania’s Great Western Railway syndi-
cate continues its humorous career. After doing
nothing for five years it put on 20 men to dig a
hole —tliis was when the time allowed for starting
work was just expiring and Parliament refused
further renewals. Government intimated that 20
men digging a hole wasn’t really starting work.
Syndicate replied that if it got a year’s extension
of time it would put on 200 men, and proceed with
the floating of the co. immediately. Govt,
noted that there was no guarantee that the 200
men who were to be put on wouldn’t be taken off
again immediately the extension of time was
granted. It also noted that there was still no
sign of anything except the 20 men mooning
around and digging an occasional hole. So rela-
tions between it and the humorous land-grant
railway syndicate continue strained.


Died recently a West Coast (Tas.) identity
—“Jack,” the dog who used to run with the N.E.
Dundas tram. He was fighting with another dog
and both were run over and killed. “Jack” had
run from Zeehan to Deep Lead (18 miles) and
back daily with the tram ever since it started.

The man Arnold, lately extradited from
California to England in connection with some big
business-frauds, had “laid-out” to raise the
question of the legality of his extradition under
the law of every State through which he should
be taken in his expected journey eastward to the
Atlantic sea-board ; in which case—even if he had
“gone-down” upon the legal point every time —
the delay would have been something prodigious.
The English ’tecs, however, are going to “stiffen ”
that game by simply taking their prisoner back
“ west-about,” via Australia.
A correspondent of Glasgow Herald re
present troubles in China ,
The Chinese are about the most tolerant people in the
world, and have no word against any form of religion as
such. What they dread is political action masking under
the form of religion. The R.O. religion in China is an
absolute imperium in imperio. Its converts extort money
from and torture their neighbors in every province in
China. They are supported in their defiance of Chinese
authority by the R.C. priests, who again rely upon the
ever-ready assistance of the French Minister in Pekin.
This unscrupulous action of the R.C. Church in China is
the real cause of the “ Boxer” movement, which was first
of all a “ mutual protection society.” The R.C. bishops
and priests have for many years arrogated to themselves
the style, status, ar.d authority of high officials of the
Chinese Empire. Hence the wise decision of the Govt,
to deny any such political status to Protestant mission-
aries who desire to follow the example of R.C’s.

The Q. Factories and Shops Report is an
interesting document. It bristles with tales of
insanitary conditions ; of puny girls and puny
youths working incredible overtime, of hapless
female, apprentices who sweat away at 2s. lOd. a
week through summer and winter, of waitresses
who regularly work from 8 a.m. to 9.30 p.m., and
up to 11.30 p.m. on the Saturday. European
females to the number of 15 are employed in
Asiatic factories ; thousands of hours’ overtime,
without compensation, are put in by pallid
youngsters ; a considerable percentage of dress-
making employes earn no wages at all. A magnifi-
cent triumph, however, is achieved by the sys-
tem, inasmuch as slop trousers are manufactured
at ss. a dozen. Prosecutions for breaches of the
Act are practically non-existent
“Banjo ” Paterson, in Melbourne Argus,
narrates how a Maoriland detachment, newly
arrived and eager for blood, went into action:
They were sent to cut off a retreating body of
Boers. Limping painfully in the rear of his com-
rades, a wounded Boer was seen traversing the
path from the kopje. This unfortunate became of
special interest to the Maorilanders. One and all
took aim at him; still he limped on slowly. Pi’e-
sently he disappeared around the neck of the hill,
unstruck by the bullets of the braves from
the land of the Moa. What a picture ! The
efforts of a wounded bird or beast to escape appeal
to an honest man’s sympathies. But this crippled
Boer, toiling behind his compatriots in full sight
of the enemy, and daring their efforts to finish
him, seems to have suggested no other impulse to
the Maorilanders but a desire to shoot him in par-
ticular. Brave work!
“ J.A.C.” gossips to The Bulletin about
the late King Humbert and his father:—
During an extended residence in Italy I had special
facilities for learning a good deal about the reigning
family. The late king, then Crown Prince, was not quite
so popular, owing to a certain stiffness of manner, as his
brother, Amadeus, ex-king of Spain, but he was respected
as a man of great personal courage, having, at the battle
of Custozza, resisted for a long time, with two regiments
of infantry, the charges of the brilliant Austrian cavalry,
and being himself seriously wounded. His beautiful wife
—then Principessa Margherita—was simply idolised. She
mixed with the people in an unconventional way, and
was a great theatre-goer. I have seen the .young men
of Milan, once when she was leaving the theatre on
a damp night, spread their coats on the ground, Walter
Raleigh fashion, for her to walk on. When Humbert
ascended the throne he displayed a solicitude for the wel-
fare of the masses which had not been expected of him.
During the Naples cholera epidemic he went everywhere
amongst the stricken, and at a time of great distress
among the poor of Italy, he devoted a very large part of
his income to helping the destitute. Our own Royal
family might do worse than take a leaf out of his book in
connection with the present famine in India. The Italian
socialists say, however —and not without show of reason—
that, had Humbert exercised his prerogative during the
’9B Milan riots, he might have saved much bloodshed.
The principal cause of the riots in question was
oppressive taxation, arising from the necessity of
maintaining an enormous standing army, in' ac-
cordance with the terms of the Triple Alliance.
Humbert, on the whole, however, was a worthy son
of Re Oalantuomo. The latter term, by the way,
means “ honest man,” and is not to be confounded with
uomo (jalante (a Lothario), an error made once by a S.M.
Herald critic in noticing a performance of “Ma Mie
Rosette.” Victor Emmanuel was of a most striking per-
sonality—with his herculean build, swarthy skin, rugged,
good-natured face, and portentous moustache. When
the railway connecting the Mediterranean sea-board with
Lombardy and Piedmont was in course of construction
and the tunnelling through the Alps had to come to a
standstill for lack of funds, Victor Emmanuel, recognising
the importance of the undertaking, sold a valuable estate
of his to provide the wherewithal. He afterwards refused
to participate in the resulting profits ; but the property
was bought back for him by public subscription. Victor
E. was also brave to rashness, and at the battle of Palestra
he pushed so far forward that he was on the point
of being captured by the Austrians, when a regi-
ment of French Zouaves rushed to his aid, and,
surrounding him, asked him to retire. He said,
“ There is fighting enough for us all here ;
let me have my share ” ; and he insisted on pressing for-
ward with them, whereupon they elected him, on the
spot, honorary corporal of the corps. He spent all his
spare time in shooting excursions, never taking any food
with him but bread and raw onions, which he carried in
the pocket of his shooting-jacket. Another peculiarity
of his was that he would never, if he could avoid it, speak
anything but Piedmontese patois. Although he was ex-
communicated when he deprived the Pope of the tem-
poral power, yet, when he died, Pius IX. had requiem
Masses said for him in Rome—a liberal example, which
Cardinal Manning (like most proselytes, a bigot) refused
to follow in London.

“D. 8.11. One of the most successful and
economical ways of evading the labour-conditions on a
mining lease is, for two friends to start an action-at-law.
Two smarties have managed this quite a long while in
connection with a well-known and promising N.S.W.
mine, and are likely to continue till someone comes along
to buy. There is not a miner in the locality who dares to
petition against this same for fear of not getting a “ job ”
when work does start. This mine has virtually not been
worked for years, and it is just about time that the
Department took a hand in the matter.
* * * *
Editor Bulletin.—When Cullinga gold-field
was visited by the local Warden some time back, he
marked out several leases, very few of which have been
since worked. The pegging out of leases in a lot of cases is a
mere blind, as those taking them up do not intend to
work them. The warden also cancelled some leases that
had been taken up eight years ago ! Surely Govt, could
do something in the way of removing the pegs. After
the 14 days for fulfilling labour conditions have expired
faw prospectors care about putting a pick into ground
that is pegged on private property, knowing the difficulty
of getting it from the former applicant. Another appa-
rently strange thing was the granting of “ suspension of
labour ” to a claim employing two hands; no notice of
application having been posted.—Fuze. *
Suggested in Maoriland that Govt, should ap-
point competent experts to report on any mining claims
applied-for, in order to safeguard the gullible public.
Only the other week present writer met a one-time hotel
porter attired in frockcoat and topper. He said he was a
“ M. E.” When this scribe remarked that he had cheek he
only laughed and said : “ Well, I’m as good as most of
’em, and the shareholders seem quite satisfied.”
* * * *
The third annual report of M.L. Hartley and
Riley Dredging 00. contains some wonderful reading.
There are 7000 shares in the co., £1 fully paid-up, of
which 500 remained unallotted, leaving 6500 to share divs.
The dredge has this year won £52,459 of gold, an average
return of £lOOO per week (or 2500 z.). Of this, £lll7 has
been paid Govt, as income-tax, and as on all divs. Govt,
also gets 6d. in the £ it is time Seddon thought of further
encouraging the dredging industry in the way of railway
freights, &c., as the difficulty of getting plant forward is
responsible for the non-starting of many half-completed
dredges. Divs. on the £1 fully-paid-up shares had been, for
this year, at the rate of £6 17s. 6d. per share. The shares
are held by 322 persons. As showing the business done on
’Change in these shares, 23,740 shares have been trans-
ferred, bought and sold. At 2J per cent, the brokers
shared £5OOO, transfer value being £182,309 4s. 7d. The
Govt, also gets £lOOO in stamp-duty. An example of
what one successful dredge can accomplish in circulating
money. The dredge-master reports prospects ob-
tained show that the ground in the claim will provide
payable work for years to come, and has every appear-
ance of being as good as that already worked.
* * * *
During the past six months, 21 Otago dredges,
with £152,876 capital, paid in divs. £42,118. Hartley and
Riley heads the list with £16,250; Electric next with
£5200 and Junction Electric £4550.
A correspondent tells The Bulletin that on
M.L. West Coast, owing to the dredging-boom, carpenters
are offered 18s. per day and travelling expenses, while in
Central Otago offers of 14s. per day meet with no response.
Forty teams and wagons are regularly employed in cart-
ing dredging-material between Lawrence and Rosburgh
(40 miles), in Central Otago.
“Shareholder”: Quartz King Lansell, by re-
stricting development work to one shift, is quietly but
surely becoming the sole owner of the valuable machinery,
huge claim, and rich prospects of “ The Prince of Wales
and Bonshaw C 0.,” BaHarat. Holding two-thirds of the
shares (although not on the board), he practically
muzzles the directory, which, while enjoying
continuous board fees for several years, seems
strangely indifferent to the immense benefit the
speedy development of such a large area would be to
Ballarat. Meanwhile the regular monthly calls of 6d.
wear out the. patience and pockets of smaller holders, who,
seeing more than half of the call absorbed by surface and
machinery care, board fees, office management, &c., &c.,
would briskly and cheerfully respond to heavier calls if a
more vigorous underground policy were pursued.
* * • *
Dear Bulletin. —Here in Tarcoola, S.A., the
warden holds that one camel represents 21 men (the brutes
can be hired at 10s. per week each) and one horse, 2 men
—provided the animals assist in the working of the lease.
People are wanting to know whether if a miner kept cows
and cocks and hens the3 T would be considered in the work-
ing of his claim. There is no mention whatever made
either in the Act or Regulations of other than manual
labor. A prominent M.L.C. and a publican together
hold an enormous area of the field, and up to date only
employ about 35 men, though according to the regula-
tions they should have 4 men to every 20 acres—which
would mean work for more than 100 men. It staggers
those who have been mining in the other
provinces to find State officials here living and messing in
the same camp with a surveyor (who acts for city people),
speculators and representatives of sharebrokers and
mining syndicates. This isn’t much calculated to inspire
confidence in those who have to deal with the mining
department. There may be nothing wrong, hut the thing
is most unseemly. The country is pegged for about 10
miles in length and an average of 3 in width, but at time
of writing only 3 leases are fully manned ! How long will
it take at this rate to develop the mineral wealth that
undoubtedly exists? In many cases also leases have been
granted months ago, yet not a pick has been put into the
ground. According 'to regulations, pegging and ad-
vertising intention to apply &c., must be simultaneous
and it is then usually 8 weeks before the lease is granted
—when the claim is supposed to be fully manned.
Nevertheless, though all the at present known gold-
bearing leases were pegged early this year the lessees are
still evading the labor covenants. Again, there are scores
of 20-acre blocks merely pegged—and neither advertising
nor application conditions complied with, and it is a
howling scandal that parties coming here fully equipped
and with bona-fide intention of prospecting and working
the ground, should be blocked by such an impudent
evasion of the law.
* * • *
“Old Miner” writes from Herberton that the
prospects of Chillagoe are not cheering ; that after two
years’ development work he was somewhat surprised at
the smallness of the ore-dumps and the limited amount
of ore exposed. He describes the Chillagoe formation as
only surface ore bunches scattered over 20 miles of coun-
try. A much greater authority assures the writer that
the lateral copper formations of Chillagoe are so numer-
ous that the great expenditure in railway-building and
mining is warranted —that the public won’t “ go down ”
even if the mines don’t.

Murrin Murrin copper-mine, Westralia. “ Pros-
pector ” writes that the discoverer was David Lambie, one
of the oldest prospectors of Mt. Malcolm district. In ’94
he took up a mineral lease, and after spending £3500 m
sinking and driving, it was jumped through the men
Lambie had it under offer to leaving it unworked for three
days (Lambie being laid up in Kalgoorlie). Then the
Warden granted the once-known Lambie Reward copper-
mine to present owner—Taylor. Poor Lambie ! Lambie
seems to be a most unlucky name in Australia.

Mt. Hope (N.S.W.): The Melb. people who
took over and have apparently been left with the Central
Mt. Hope Freehold mine evidently mean business, and
have already a great number of men employed in prepara-
tory work. They have also secured an option on the Mt.
Hope mine itself, the tribute of which has just expned.
In continuing the tributors’lsoft. level a fine body of
prill ore has been cut, and though it may be but a bunch
it shows considerable development, and as there has been
very little work done between the 150 ft. and 340 ft. levels
there is plenty room for ore. With a railway from Con-
doblin or Oobar Mt. Hope would become an impoitant
mining centre. Without a railway, Heaven help •
The New Sulphide Reduction Process is to be
merged into the British Sulphides Smelting Co. Capital,
half-a-million ; a working-capital of £lOO,OOO to be raised
by issuing shares carrying a fixed div. of 10 percent.
Much was said of this process last year and of the great
things it was going to do with sulphide ores, no matter
hpw base. Mt. Reid mine, Tasmania, was to become a big
thing under its influence. Something seems to have gone
wrong with the works. Although it has been running for
nearly three years nothing has been advanced to prove
that it can be worked on a practical scale.
♦ * * *
The quantity of sulphides now treated per week
at the “ Barrier” compares favorably with the output of
any silver-lead mining field in the world. The difficulty
in treatment of sulphides is being successfully grappled
with mainly owing to improved systems of mechanical
“dressing.” In this the Luhrig Vanner plays a very
prominent part. A large number of these machines are
now in use there, and fresh orders are still being given to
the agents, Messrs. W. and J. Lempriere, Melbourne. The
South Mine people are remodelling their mill on up-to-
date lines, and will install twelve or more Luhrig Vanners
of the 1900 pattern. The British 00. is also erecting
more of these superior machines.*

By the way, The Bulletin has probably for-
gotten to mention that the Soldier’s Hill Diamond Mining
Co. paid a div. of 3d. per share last year, and hopes to
repeat the brilliant achievement pretty soon. The co. is
getting diamonds in a small way. Balance-sheet for the
year ending May 31st reports that 4412 J carats of dia-
monds, value £3023, have been raised thusfar, also tin (14
tons odd) worth £lOB6, and gold to the modest amount of
£l3 13s. Bd. Say £4123 in all. The cost of producing this
lot was about £3849, so the 3d. div. (£600) was paid
mostly from a 2s. 6d. call on contributing shares. And
the nominal capital of the mine stands at £47,250.

* * * *
N.S.W. Mines Minister Fegan has a bill well
through the Assembly enacting an eight-hour day for
miners ; but, as on a couple of former tries, trouble is
feared from the “ other place.” And yet the Bill is so
tame a little pet that it will feed out of the hand. There
are only three things in it: a workman must not be in a
mine more than eight hours in the 24 ; the time must be
computed from bank to bank (in other words, the incom-
ing shift must relieve the outgoing men at the shaft and
not at the working face) ; and anybody who works longer
hours is liable to a£2 fine. The only contentious scrap
of this relates to the place for changing shifts —at the sur-
face or at the working face. That- is an old bone of
contention; but the miners got the sweet end of it years
ago, and now the almost universal rule is to change at the
shafts’ mouth unless there is a dangerous place or an un-
exploded charge which it is necessary for the incoming
men to know of. The Bill, therefore, even as to this lets
matters stand just where they are ; it takes the well-
established and smooth working conditions of an industry
and seeks to put the stamp of law upon them in the
interests of industrial peace. The owner who knows
what mining is is quite satisfied that an eight hours’ shift is
a fair thing, so the proposed law will not affect him at
all; the man it will affect, if anybody, is the new-chum
English manager sent out to the blawsted colonies to
make divs. for a J. Bull 00. And it won’t affect him
unless he tries to do something that custom has con-
demned long since.

Associated Gold Mines (W.A.): Chairman Lan-
dau admitted that last year’s directors’ report, based on the
manager’s, gave no indication of the disasters that were
so soon to follow. Manager’s careful estimate of ore in
sight and blocked out was 739,296 tons and the average
assays loz. 16dwts. per ton. He ventured to say that if
the Board had not sent a special mission to the mine the
whole of the co.’s money would have been squandered
and reconstruction would have become inevitable.
Financial position, six weeks ago, £240,000 in cash.
Frank Gardiner did not get on the Board—he surrounded
his nomination with too many conditions. Landau sym-
pathised with the idea of prosecuting servants of the co.
who had grosslv deceived shareholders and was willing to
head a fund with £5OO for this purpose, but did not care
to use his co.’s money in this direction. But if it is not
the co.’s business, whose is it ? Waddington denied that
the directors had received any private information and
used it for their own benefit in speculating in shares,
and declared that the directors as a rule knew
less about the mine than men in the street.
It was common knowledge that there were spies
in every mine in the world in the pay of London
speculators, and directors were often blamed for not pub-
lishing information which they did not possess. Having
been through the Are, he hoped the experience he had
gained would be of use in preventing a recurrence of
local mismanagement. The meeting accepted Hewitson’s
appointment with acclamation, and the chairman said the
property was being worked without loss. The mine rose
over £lOO,OOO in value the day after the meeting. It had
another good rise when the mill shut down, so it cannot
now be working without loss. Meeting terminated with-
out a vote of thanks to the “ Bears ” for keeping up the
price of shares in a mine which would not have half its
market-valuation without them.

Hannan’s Oroya: Another reconstruction-
capital increased to £250,000 by the creation of 50,000
new shares. Proposed, to erect a 100-ton-a-ciay sulphide
plant. This will cost between £40,000 and £50,000. And
the estimate gives some idea of the expense of treating
Kalgoorlie sulphides. The problem is now supposed to be
solved hut with many mines out there the sulphide
problem will be to find the money. Manager’s estimate
of ore in sight on North block 22,085 tons, and on South
block 26,500 tons ; estimated value, £143,000. Mill shut
down as the oxidised ore available cannot be treated
without loss. The chairman’s appeal to shareholders to
take up new shares was remarkable. He says that had
the new scrip been issued some time ago buyers would
have had to pav £3 10s. a share for them while now they
could get them at 305., thus giving shareholders who had
bought at £3 or £4 a share a splendid opportunity of
bringing down their average price. Possibly a fine thing
for shareholders, but a missed chance for the co.

There was a big missionary jubilation
(Anglican) in Brisbane t’other day. Sundry in-
fluential big-wigs testified as to the value of
missions in Papua ; among the testimonies afore-
said, was that of Gov. Le Hunte. Perhaps the
proudest boast made at the meeting was that of a
rev. dignitary, who told with pride that, no matter
how licentious the traders, they were still so gen-
tlemanly that they observed the tacit understand-
ing that there should be “no immorality under
the shadow of the mission ! ” Note herein several
beautiful im lications. Firstly, the missionary-
advocate admits that the trader is of licentious
bent; and, secondly, that he finds numbers of
Papuan ladies by no means averse to his endear-
ments. Thirdly, one is led to infer that mis-
sionary influence is only powerfu I enough to ban
immoral proceedings from under the sacred
“shadow of aforesaid mission.” Lastly, it was
not asserted that the Christianised girls are more
virtuous than the heathen ; it was only said that
their chastity is invulnerable—in the neighborhood
of certain buildings. Altogether, it doesn’t look
like a very big boast to make when you’ve weighed
the pros and cons, but it loomed up wonderfully
in the columns of Brisbane papers, and was re-
garded by the orthodox as a set-o!f to the late
massacres of Christians in China, .fust ask any
old hand who has come in contact with the “ con-
verted ” aboriginal in N.Q., or rubbed noses with
a Sunday-school kanaka in the sugar-districts,
what he 'thinks of the regenerated heathen, and
he’ll give you his opinion in a style that would
drive a whole army of clergymen to drown their
sorrows in the accursed drink.

Sydney Evening News is moving for a
new trial of the libel-action brought against it by
a policeman, to whom a monumental jury awarded
£6O damages, carrying many hundreds of costs.
Major Holmes (secretary of Sydney
Water and Sewerage Board) isn’t going to get his
salary, as such, for the time he was away “swash-
ing ” as an officer in S’Africa. The Board pro-
mised him said salary, but Premier Lyne says that,
as the Board had no right whatever to make such
a promise on its “own,” he sees no reason why in
this respect an exception should be made of the
Major.
The will of Miss Elizabeth Jenkins, one
of two eccentric old maiden sisters who, for many
years, lived at Long Reef, Narrabeen district,
Sydney, will shortly be haggled over by a big legal
brigade in N.S.W. Supreme Court. The Misses
Jenkins, neither of whom died under 80, were
daughters of an Imperial soldier who got a large
and valuable land-grant at Narrabeen in the old
days, comprising Long Reef, one of the loveliest
coast-sites in all Australia. In their old age they
got in tow of the Salvation Army, to which they
made large gifts; and now that they are dead it is
found that all the Jenkins estate, valued at per-
haps £150,000, has been bequeathed to the Harmy
aforesaid. A nephew of theirs is kicking—avers
that his aunt Elizabeth was silly and the subject
of undue influence. Hence the lawsuit.
In Sydney Supreme Court last week a
Sydney resident was sued by the mother of
a girl for damages for loss of her services,
owing to the girl having, as was alleged, been
seduced by said resident while in his em-
ployment. Want, Q.C., raised the technical point
that, as the girl was in defendant’s service
at the time of the alleged seduction —which, by
the way, defendant entirely denies—the mother
could not have lost her services, and had no
interest in them. The Chief Justice considered
the point fatal, and the mother was nonsuited.
It is a solemn fact that N.S.W. law only allows
damages for seduction on the ground of the loss
of the seduced girl’s personal services to her
parent, and yet on that point the damages may be
absolutely unlimited. Therefore the rich parent
who can afford to keep his daughter at home, even
though she renders no services there, can get, say,
£5OOO damages on the alleged ground that he was
deprived of £5OOO worth of household assistance
from her during the period when she was incapaci-
tated—perhaps £250 worth of household assistance
per week. And the poor man whose daughter
has to go out to earn her own living can’t get
damages for her seduction because he didn’t
lose her services—it was her employer who
lost them. And yet she may give a great
part of her wages to her parents, and
therefore her seduction may make a real loss.
According to the law of N.S.W. as expounded by
J. Want and the Chief Justice, if a man can afford
to hire a girl for a fortnight as his servant at ss. a
week he can seduce her with impunity, so far as
damages are concerned. And if he is too poor to
hire her as his servant, and seduces her without
that formality, he is liable for unlimited damages.
The poor man is struck both ways, and the richer
man crawls out both ways. Apropos, a few weeks
ago a Cornstalk mother who was affluent enough to
keep her daughter at home got £l5OO damages for
loss of services. Jack Want was her lawyer, and
the moving picture he drew of how the finger of
scorn would be pointed at her all her days as an
illegitimate grandmother (if the expression is
correct) was calculated to draw tears from an iron
post.
From figures recently to hand, it would
seem that N.S.W. oranges, of a quality which
realised locally only 2s. 6d. profit per case, have
been sold in London at an average profit of Bs. 6d.
The exporter, Mr. Bennett, of Hawkesbury dis-
trict, who made the shipment merely with the
view of “filling the breach” and keeping the
trade alive, has thus met with the pecuniary
reward his public spirit deserved.

Enteric fever is very fashionable just now,
even the pheasants in England being decimated
by the disease. The pheasants that gamekeepers
rear by hand for the entertainment of shooting
parties are very susceptible to enteric, and they
probably prefer to die that way.
Maori description of the effects of lyddite :
By cripes, the shell, he the boy to kill the Poa (Boer).
The time he burst, as soon you hear the smell, you dead.
One flaw in N.S.W. Municipalities Act is
that there is no provision for formally winding-up
hopeless municipalities. Deserted Silverton, the
early depbt for the Barrier silver-field, has for
years past been uncertain whether it is a munici-
pality or not. It knew for certain that there was
not any Mayor or aldermen dr council clerk ; but
yet it was assured that the Municipal Council
was still the owner of some buildings and the
trustee of the common and a lot of other things.
By a round-about process this anomaly is, how-
ever, now being ended; though Government
officials say they don’t know how the thing can be
disposed of satisfactorily.
“ Pukaki ” tries to figure things out : —
Kipling lately told “ Banjo ” Paterson that it would be
very foolish for Australia to separate from Britain, as “it
would be a dead snip for Germany.” Kipling is given to
hasty generalisations, and this statement of his wants
looking into. The Boer population with which Britain is
at war numbers about 150,000. The population of Aus-
tralia is some 3,500,000. It has taken 250,000 soldiers,
“ the finest in the world,” to conquer the Boers. If an
Australian is one-tenth as good a fighting man as a Boer
it would, on this basis, take 600,000 German soldiers to
conquer Australia. Then we are told that the most mar-
vellous thing in the war has been, not so much what the
British did when they got there, as how they got
there. It is said to have been a marvel in the way of
transport. No other nation could have performed the
feat. Now, Australia is twice as far from Germany as
S’Africa from England. The difficulties of transport
would be at least doubled. And Germany has not half
the facilities for transport that Britain has. Therefore, if
the Australian is only one-tenth as good for fighting pur-
poses as the Boer, the conquest of Australia by Germany
still looks impossible.

Business, Robbery, etc.
Some curiosities in life-assurance medical exam-
ination. A recently-retired ex-chief of a Govt, department
applied to a N.S.W. life-office for a loan on the security of
his pension. A policy was necessary, but the examining
doctor declined to recommend the life. Heart was sound,
lungs ditto, kidneys seriously affected. The rejected
candidate thereupon tried an American office, and the
result of the overhaul was that the examiner found heart
right, kidneys ditto, lungs affected. Still not satis-
fied, the pensioner proposed to a third office, with the
further result that kidneys and lungs were found right,
but heart seriously affected. In sheer desperation, appli-
cant consulted another medical officer attached to office
No. 1, with the astounding result, that lungs, heart, and
kidneys were declared to be in perfect health. Thi* reads
like a snake-yarn, but it is absolute unvarnished truth.
• • # ,
“ Been There ”: Business agency fine work.
A places his hotel in your hands for sale ; you advertise ;
a possible buyer comes along ; you let him have a look at
the business, and then tap him for 10 per cent; deposit
(your own commission is only 5 per cent, and ex’s). Then,
during ensuing negotiations, you arouse buyer’s sus-
picions, and, by judicious hints, make him think the deal
not a good one. If he “ jihs-out ” you forfeit his deposit,
and tell the seller he never paid one. Keep seller and buyer
apart all the time ; and, even if seller should come to know
of the deposit, halve it with him, and you still have the
business on hand to make 5 per cent, out of. By working
things thus, bluffing the buyer off by talking of action for
“specific performance of contract,” and holding on to
the forfeited deposits, it is possible to live without selling
many businesses.

What’s in a name ? A Melbourne tobacco-
twisting damsel lately told me that her smoke-
factory put on the market a certain brand of
cigarette—with enormous unsuccess. “ Hard luck,” said
I. “ Rats ! ” said she. “We just took ’em all back and
put ’em in noo boxes with a noo nyme—and now they’re
sellin’ like mad 1 ”

The cook on one of the large Sydney-coming
liners landed the other day 4 hogsheads of fat, of which
3 were delivered at restaurants, but the 4th into the base-
ment of a city warehouse. Here careless handling stove-
in the top of the cask, and lo ! a false head containing
6in. of fat—the rest of the cask being filled with cig. .
and tobacco.

“L.R.”: I know a young man whose father
lately died, leaving everything to his widow. The widow
next day died of shock, leaving everything to the
son. Will probate-duty have to be paid twice —first on
the father’s estate and then on the mother’s (who had
nothing to leave except what her husband left)? [Yes ;
probate-duty will have to be paid twice.—Ed. B.]
* * » »
Dear Bulletin. —Re medical attendance on
Westralian mines. At a certain N.S.W. mine there is
posted at the office a notice that anyone refusing to pay
his contribution to the medical fund will be sacked. The
money is stopped at the office, and amounts (at 9d. per
head of 200 men or so) to £7 10s. a week. A nice little
sinecure, because nine-tenths of the men will have no-
thing to do with the doctor (who was employed here once
before). Why don’t the men object in a body ? Because
they would all be marked men—to have their places
gradually taken by others. A large percentage of them
are married men with families, to whom the sack wohld
mean ruin, the mine being the only one in the district
which employs any labor to speak of.—Yakker.

“ Ped.” : Recently took my watch to alleged
jeweller in N.S. W. country town to be cleaned, and 3 days
after getting it back it stopped dead ; couldn’t make it go
anyhow. Examined it, and made the interesting dis-
covery that the “innards” had been lifted bodily, and
replaced with worn-out works of some more ancient
ticker.
* * » *
As a keen, hard-headed business man the
average successful advt.-canvasser is hard to beat. But
many canvassers have been badly beaten in Maoriland.
Two men started a manufacturing biz., and represented
by a few preliminary prepaid advts. that their prepara-
tion had a huge sale there and abroad. The man who
was supposed to be the financial partner attended solely
to the (alleged) manufacturing department, and left the
advertising solely to the other fellow. The latter, by
delicate innuendo, gave the hordes of advt.-searchers to
know that a share of the commission might induce him to
work the “ financial ” partner to contract for a page or so.
Result, contracts all round from £lO to £lOO, and at
least £ISQ (half-corns.) from the poor devils of canvassers—
who, in many cases, had to borrow the bribes, which they,
of course, will have to doubly repay to those they repre-
sent. The firm’s suspension of payment is to last inde-
finitely.
* « * *
N.S.W. Supremo Court recently decided that
the publication in a trade journal of particulars of a duly-
registered bill-of-sale is privileged, because, according to
Darley, C.J., “ it is absolutely essential in a mercantile
community such as this that persons should know to
whom they can safely give credit. The Bills of Sale Act,
which provided that records should be kept in the offices
of the Supreme Court, and that they should be open for
public inspection, was specially designed to secure the
widest publicity of such matters.”

A Queensland man recently entered for a
bazaar-raffle, which was to be decided in one throw, and,
having thrown the “possible” (18) when there were but
two or three more chances to be tried, he came away
assured that he had won. On going round next day,
however, to claim the prize, he was surprised to hear,
from a sweet innocent in book-muslin, that Father O’Raf-
ferty had thrown 20! Who says the age of miracles is
past ?
* * » » •
Wanted badly, a law to prohibit shopkeepers
from acting as directors of trust associations. In a cer-
tain province, lately, a retail-trader, on two separate occa-
sions, garnisheed the trust co. of which he is a director
for the amount held in trust for a debtor. Admitting his
right to collect debts by any legitimate means, has not
this particular merchant a much better “ look-in ” than
his fellow-tradesmen?

“R.A.” : Note in one issue of a Melb. daily 11
different offers of “ Stradivarius ” and “Cremona”
violins, all “ genuine,” at prices from 255. to £3 IDs., bow
and case included. Yet Pentridge hasn’t had a pawn-
broking guest for quite an age.
Mr. J. Joynton Smith, of the Hotel Arcadia,
Sydney, to a Sydney daily on the tied-hotel question
“ The unfortunate publican who once gets into the hands
of a brewer, for no matter how small a sum, has no pos-
sible chance whatever of escaping from his captor. I
shall mention, as an example, one instance of many which
have come under my notice. A certain publican became
indebted to a brewer to the extent of £lso—a trifling
amount to a man of business—and in consequence of this
his house was tied, not for six months or 12 months, but
for the term of his existing lease, ‘or any extension or
renewal thereof,’ which means, practically, for all time.
It is also well-known that brewers frequently put salaried
dummies into hotels which they find it difficult
to let, and it is owing in a measure to this prac-
tice that disorderly houses flourish. ... In Victoria a
duty of Bd. per lb. is charged on hops, while on sugar it is
6s. per cvvt., or if it is to be refined in that State ss. 9d.
per cwt. ; whereas in N.S.W. refined sugar is admitted at
4s. per cwt., and raw at 35., hops being free. Notwith-
standing the great difference in favor of N.S.W., the
great and powerful local brewing ring here exacts £3 12s.
per hogshead from the ‘ tied ’ houses, or about
17s more than the current market price in Vic-
toria. Also, it is an'open secret that houses which
are free can purchase their beer fully 10s. per hogs-
head cheaper than their less fortunate rivals—say at
about £3 2s.—which shows the great anomaly of the
‘ tied’house question. Though a barrel is supposed to
contain 54 gallons, the most a publican can get out of it
is 50 gals. ; and when he has to retail, it at 3d. a pin., the
o-ross return to him is about £5, leaving a profit oi only
£1 Bs. Out of that he has expenses to meet, such as rent,
servants, counter-luncheons, rates, taxes, lighting, &c.
The average publican is only human, and when he nnas
that he cannot pay his .way legitimately, owing to the
exorbitant prices which he is charged for his purchases,
he resorts to adulteration, &c. Therefore, the sooner the
present state of affairs is remedied—and that can only be
brought about by most drastic legislation—the better it
will be for the public health and the general conduct of
the trade. So long as * tied ’ houses continue to exist,
adulterated spirits will be sold.”
» • * •
“Jack Shay ” to The Bulletin :
Dear Bulletin.— You print (30/6/’00) : “ The man who
expects the run of a bottle of high-class spirits, with
mineral water, for sixpence, is a fool.” I—having myself
served a term “ at the bar” —say the publican who does
not give a man decent whisky and m.w. for 6d. is a rogue;
and I show cause. The ordinary bottle of whisky, or
other spirits, costs the trader from 3s. 3d. to 4s. 3d., said
bottle averaging 14 or 16 drinks. I have seen 10 drinks
only half-empty a bottle. Then, one dozen m.w., Is. ;
average cost of whisky, 3s. 9d. (lower where bulk whisky
is sold from decanters) ; total, 4s. 9d., as against Bs. for
16 drinks. Again, therefore, the publican who can’t be
honest on a margin of 3s. 3d., and over, is a rogue 1 Re
draught spirits : You can buy o.p. whisky at 245. per gal.
Breaking it down about one-sixth gives you a whisky
worth about 21s. per gal., so at the Bs. for two-thirds of a
quart average, your gal. of whisky brings you in £2 Bs.
Watch how ready even the best bars are to dump down
their draught decanters in default of your asking for
some special brand. By faking, profits can be increased ;
while “ untied ” publicans, who buy in bond and speculate
in bond-certificates, holding large stocks bought cheap—
well, to them, 6d. spirits with m.w. is a veritable gold-
mine.

A M.L. up-country Mayor recently hit on a new
way of collecting arrears of rates. Halfway through his
term he resigned. As faction ran high in the place,
everyone wanted to vote for the new Mayor ; and, as no
one could vote who was a defaulter on the rate-roll, .the
arrears' came in with a rush.
• * * *
A person calling himself “ Colonel ” landed
at Auckland, from ’Frisco, a few years ago, and lived in
great style, giving many champagne-lunches, and getting
into clubs and society. A confederate, starting from
Invercargill, had meanwhile sold cheap to every club and
leading hotel in the province numbers of packs of playing-
cards, of good quality, but all marked. When the pear
was ripe, the Col. began his tour. He entertained and
played cards right through from north to south and back
to Auckland, ending up by taking down a party of very
keen bookies, and making in all at least four figures, ft
was long after the “Col.” had left that a Wellington
hotelkeeper found the cards were “ trick.”

Interesting advt. of a London tailor in a remote
suburban paper—the Surrey Herald and Middlesex
News “ Free Railway Ticket to London. In order to
introduce our famous * Geelong Tweeds ’ and ‘ Empire
Serges ’ to parties living in the country, we will pay, 3rd
class return fare to London (providing the distance tra-
velled is not more than 30 miles) of every purchaser of
one of our celebrated 30s. Suits (made to measure), style
and fit guaranteed. Geelong cloths take their name from
the town in Australia where they are manufactured ; they
have long been recognised throughout our colonies as
being unrivalled for beautiful texture and having no
equal for hard wear, and we are quite sure a trial will
convince everyone of their marvellous value and dura-
bility.—So & Co., Economical City Tailors, :
street, E.C. (near Blacltfriars Bridge).” The so-
called “ Geelong tw r eed ” at 30s. the suit is purest British
shoddy, of course, but the fact of a Cockney tailor using
the term as an advt. catch-line show's that Australia is
getting on as a manufacturing country. The name of its
wares begins to be worth stealing.

Legislation for the suppression of bogus bidders
at stock auctions is suggested in Maoriland. Numerous
A. and P. associations written to on the subject refused
to support a measure disturbing the existing pleasant
arrangement, but a big petition is nevertheless being pre-
pared, requesting State interference. It will, however,
take a stiff Act to knock out the bogus head-wap ger.
* * • •
Various Acts of Parliament exempt “bedding”
from distress and execution, but Shylock-landlords and
their admirers interpret this word as not including “ bed-
steads,” and have instructed their bailiffs accordingly.
These harpies will now be distressed to hear that the
English Queen’s Bench Division has decided that the
“ bedding” privileged from distress includes a bedstead
used by a tenant as part of his sleeping accommodation.

Two wood-carters battling Little Bourke-street,
Melbourne, with a load, tackled a Chow and received the
shrewd reply “ No feah: load too muchee likee house
insi.”
* • • •
A Sydney van-man was one day engaged by a
person who took him to an empty house and loaded up a
36-gal. cask, with which they drove around from hotel to
hotel, till evening came and the vanman had about 15s.
due to him. But the “traveller” now confided to him
that the cask contained about £7 10s. worth of illicit
whisky, which he had been trying to sell. He also gave
the vanman a good nip from his sample-bottle, and told
him to take the cask home in the meantime and meet
him (the “ commercial ”) on the morrow, when a couple of
quid would be forthcoming if a deal were made. Half
the next day, accordingly, the vanman waited vainly for
the other one—then he took the cask home and stored it
as security. But as the owner never turned up the van-
man rested content with possession, until, having - a big
shifting job on a wet day, he took his mates around to
have a nip of prime stuff, and a bottle to take home with
them. They tapped the cask, and drew off a large jug
full of—water ! and upon opening the head they found
a tin tube, fixed against the spile, and containing about a
quart of the crathur. The vanman sold the cask for 55.,
and is waiting anxiously to have 355. in either money or
stoush should he ever meet his gay deceiver.
» * * *
This apparently-simple confidence game was re-
cently played off in Townsville : Three thil sty gentlemen
enter a pub. and call for drinks, which Brown pays for with
gold coin. “Let me see—was that half-a-sovereign?”
asks the shouter on receiving 9s. 3d. change. The half-
sov. is handed back at his request, whereupon he adds 9d.
to the 9s. 3d., hunches the change and half-sov. together,
and asks the barman to hand him a pound-note in ex-
change for the lot. The barman, slightly confused, does
so, and discovers his error when the financiers have de-
parted. Rumpus and police.

“Theo. B.” : If I owned a controlling interest
in a railway or telegraph-line, and wanted to get a real
A 1 advertising scheme worked out cheaply, 1 would pro-
pose to extend said line to the South Pole or Mars or the
moon, and instigate my fellow-shareholders to slang-
whang for and against the proposal in letters to the press.
My venture would thus get scores of columns of capital
advts., giving statistics and facts,and showing that the affair
was so very gilt edged that no possible extension could
hurt it. The value of this ad. merely at space-rates
would be anything from £lOOO to £l5OO, but otherwise it
would be simply incalculable! Worked about every 7
years, this plan would boost my concern ahead of all
rivals, and pull the collective newspaper leg to an enor-
mous extent.
