proportion. Error discovered, and old woman applies
for refund. Commissioners refuse, because applica-
tion was n't made in t'me-limit.


Great Weakness and Anaemia
An Interesting Case
And a Perfect Recovery.
The Case of
MRS. SARAH DILLON.
(by our own reporter.)
As our reporter was one day pursuing his
Usual mission, involving the collection of
items of interest to the reading section of
the community, he happened to hear of the
remarkable recovery from weakness and
continuous headaches of Mrs. Sarah Dillon,
who was then residing at 5 Norton-street,
Surry Hills. He accordingly called upon
that lady, who cheerfully offered to give
him all the information he desired.
“ My case must have been a particularly
interesting one to the doctors who attended
Upon me,” began Mrs. Dillon, “for the very
simple reason that they were of the opinion
that I was beyond the scope of medical
treatment. I can assure you it is exceed-
ingly dispiriting to be told by your physician
that your condition is the natural result of
old age creeping on, and anything he
prescribed for you could not possibly do any
good. And it is especially so for a woman
like myself, who had previously enjoyed the
best of health. I had never before that
time known what it was to have a day’s
sickness, excepting as a child, when I met
with an accident to my hand, which soon
got all right again; but I clo not call that
sickness. And then to be suddenly
conscious of an ailment which was gradually
drawing all the vitality from me, and to be
told that nothing could be recommended by
my doctor to cure me, you can easily
imagine how despondent I became.”
“What were the symptoms of your
malady, Mrs. Dillon ? ”
“Well, I cannot very easily describe them
at the commencement of my illness, because
they were altogether too complicated. 1
knew that my blood was not in good order
on account of the alteration in the colour of
my complexion. My lips assumed an un-
healthy paleness, and my eyes became dull
and heavy. I cannot say that Isuffered a great
amount of pain, although very frequently I
would become possessed of a sickly feeling
which left me languid and extremely weak.
It would have given me a great amount of
relief if I could have slept when I retired to
bed at night; but even that luxury was
denied me, for I would turn in feeling so
jaded and worn out that I felt quite sure of
getting a little repose; but I was invariably
disappointed. As a result, it was almost an
impossibility to get up in the mornings, and
when I did manage to do so, after much
perseverance, I was too weak and exhausted
to stand. During the day I would be
afflicted with a feeling of a drowsy languor,
as though everything taking place around
me was no interest or importance; and the
next development in my failing health was
something entirely new. I had never
before known what it was to faint; but now
it was a frequent occurrence for me to go
right off without any apparent cause, and
when I regained consciousness I would be in
a depressed state of mind for a long time.
It was enough to frighten any woman at my
time of life to experience such threatening
indications of a general break-up of the
system; and when I tell you that my
appetite had abandoned me altogether, you
can understand how dejected I was. The
most tempting food failed to make me eat,
and the robust appearance I had so long
been proud of gave way to a condition of
emaciation. The sight of food was a source
of trouble to myself, as my inability to eat
was an additional reminder that I was
gradually wasting away. And the hopeless-
ness with which 1 thought of the future was
brought about through the doctors’ opinion
that there was nothing in the way of
medicine which could possibly restore me to
health. The idea of being in a state of such
utter abandonment occasioned me no end
ofworry and anxiety, which, I’m sure you will
agree, was quite natural. No matter how ill
a person may be, I think they can always
keep up their spirits to a certain extent if
they are following out a course of treatment
which will encourage them to "believe that
their health may ultimately be regained.
But in my ease I was apparently left without
a chance. However, I made my mind up
that it would be foolish to continue in such
a state of misery without making an effort
in some direction, because I did not think
anything in the way of physic could have the
effect of making me worse, my condition
then being so forlorn. By a stroke of good
luck I secured the right remedy at the first
attempt.”
“How were you so fortunate?” enquired
the reporter.
“ It was through listening to the advice of
a lady friend of mine, wlio had benefited
through the same medium, that I was so
favoured by Dame Fortune. She strongly
recommended me to try Clements Tonic,
assuring me that if I did so there would bo a
decided change for the better in a very short
space of time. It was with a certain
amount of doubt and misgiving that I con-
sented to give it a trial, although it was a
pleasure to think that there was something
which might have the effect of recuperating
my wasted system. I was really too
dejected to place confidence in any medicine;
but I sent for a bottle of Clements Tonic by
way of experiment, and, would you believe
tne ? I felt myself improving after the first
few dose*. Day by day my appetite gained
ground, and I was soon able to sit down
and enjoy a comfortable meal. The old,
worn out, despondent feeling of weakness
entirely left me, and the vigorous blood
that, imw ran through my veins had such a
stimulating effect upon my spirits that I
soon became quite cheerful again. The
racking headaches from which I had
suffered were but a memory of the past; and
I can tell you they used to be very severe
indeed. They never attack me now,
however, for I continued taking Clements
Tonic till my vitality was thoroughly
restored, and my health is now as good as
ever. I think in a country like this, where
the heat is so oppressive in summer,
Clements Tonic is a grand thing to have in
the house, and I always have it on hand
during the hot weather to take as a stimu-
lant. I believe that is why my strength
keeps up so well.”
“Do you give your consent to the
publication of this interview?”
“With the utmost pleasure. I owe my
recovery exclusively to Clements Tonic, and
now I always recommend it to my friends.”
statutory: declaration.
I, Sarah Dillon, of 5 Norton street, Surry Hills,
Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, rlo
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully
read the annexed document, consisting of four folios
and consecutively numbered from one to four, and
that it contains and is a true and faithful account
of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also
contains my full permission to publish in any way
my statements—which I give voluntarily, without
receiving any payment; and I make this solemn de-
claration conscientiously believing the same to be
true, and by virtue of the provisions of an Act made
and passed in the ninth year of the reign of her pre cnt
Majesty, intituled “An Act for the more effectual
abolition of Oaths and Affirmations taken and made
in the various Departments of the Government of
New South Wales, and to substitute Declarations
in lieu thereof, and for the suppression of voluntary
and extra-judicial Oaths and Affidavits.”

“And Nations Wept!”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Universal grief is expressed at the tragic end of the late
King.” —The Press — everywhere.
The children wept in the fetid slum and fought
for the empty bowl;
We gave them crumbs in a careless mood and
dealt them a careless dole,
But Peace and Calm and the dreams of balm lay
sweet on our sated soul.
We heard a wail from a land afar of Misery dark
and drear;
The distant howl of the Hunger Wolf, it fell on a
heedless ear.
For We were fatted and we were fed —and never a
Want was near !
There came a cry from a stainhd field of a myriad
men in pain ;
There rang thedire of a fusilade that flashed from
the ranks of Cain,
And the bells they* rang, and the people sang and
told of a*thousand slain.
There fell a shot in an olden town, and the blood
of a prince was shed;
A gasp, a groan, and a parting moan, and the
crowned life was sped :
And the Nations cried in the morning-tide to know
that a King was dead /
Q.
F.
A Night in a Pocket.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thb everlasting rain fell from the tops of the
towering mountain ash (which is not an ash) to
the soil-seeking limb-roots of the native fig-trees
(which have nothing to do with figs), thence to the
lawyer-vines, and thence to the sting-trees and the
unfortunate travellers. Rain in drops, sheets,
showers, waves ; rain in feather and in powder;
and, when no rain, then an atmospheric saturation
—the wraith of fog—and emulsion.
Along the narrow track the packers’ horses
struggled, treading only in the great furrows of
the miry volcanic soil—the furrows that earned
for the squdgey alley through the scrub the name
of “ the potato field ” —the furrows that, flank-
deep as they were, were luxury compared to the
treacherous slimy crowns between them. Easy
enough for a leg-break here. The soil fell away with
traffic from the entanglement of roots and left un-
speakable traps, but the horses struggled and
schemed as only a packer’s horse can, balancing,
their two-hundred weight of load —stream tin,
corn, anything—swinging themselves down the
walls with the careless exactitude of cats, giving
their buttocks to the steepness and somewhile
sliding on their quarters.
Billy King’s small party first followed, and later
preceded, the packers, and like most men who go
ahead had the worst of the deal, for there were
places where great trees had blocked the narrow
track. So Billy King, sometime something else,
and now a mining - promoter of wonderful
and spasmodic luck relieved by cataclysmal
failures, and John Majendie his mate, and the
others of Billy King’s party, cut a new road in the
wall of fern and lawyer-vine and hazel, using great
scrub-knives which occasionally fell away from
the spineless sting-tree and gave that delightful
vegetable to the hands of the operator. After
these frequent road-makings, Majendie and King
walked ahead towing their grunting horses.
“We ought to reach that pocket before
sundown, ” said Billy. Majendie thought similarly
and stopped to curse his horse.
“Everything’s a fraud,” said Billy again.
“ Distance is a fraud ; we should have reached that
pocket two hours ago. Here’s about 40 miles of
holes calling itself a track, and there’s that sting-
tree that looks so green and innocent —like the
little boy who died —and yet it killed a horse
yesterday and gave me particular rats to-day.”
A scrub-turkey started before them, made his
run and got his way on, mounted drunkenly into
the humid air and dived into the green surf of the
scrub top. A wallaby stopped prospecting for
ticks and made into the close undergrowth.
“Fraudsagain,” continued King “ just like you
and me. That scrub-turkey looks as pretty as a
cookery-book, but outside of his bones he’s all
feathers and inside he’s all worms —like a jay.
And that wallaby is a flourishing colony of ticks
and he’s been scratching ’em on to the track so
that our horses will pick ’em up. All of ’em
crawlers, all of ’em 1 ”
“ ‘Pediculi’ is the polite word,” murmured the
demure Majendie.
“I like that fine christening-party air of yours.
Why that word ‘Pediculi’ is a fraud, and all this
is a fraud like most prospectuses and experts.
Think of ’em ! There are some of ’em who reverse
the swindle by giving an indiscriminate negative
to everything they report on. In the majority of
cases they’re bound to be right, and that’s what
makes the fraud worse.”
“I knew one of ’em,” said Majendie; “he went
to see some mines in the West and he damned
twenty of ’em and lauded the last one unto the
third generation. And, my grief ! the twenty are
paying divs. now and the twenty-first is playing
Icliabod on its stampers.”
“ Verily and of a trewth,” said Billy nasally
“there be but two righteous men in Gomorrah,
and them’s us.”
There came a break in the scrub wall and they
saw patches of sky, the air lost its balsamic
moisture, they heard the tinkling of water on
metallic pebbles, the impervious scrub broke into
belts ana patches, making a running fight with
the open country and dying hard. The potato- .
fields ended. The interlaced roots were succeeded
by tussocks of silver and kangaroo grasses; horses
and men became new again, and strode quickly into
a small circle of open billowed grass-land—an
exercise-yard in the gaol of the scrub.
When 60 or 70 miles of scrub are cleared
the traveller may yet see from that pocket
the reflection of the Pacific Ocean; he may view
Bellenden Ker and Bartle Frere and the mountains
that hide Cairns and Trinity Bay. Certain
learned men who can take up a chance stone and
tell you when it was made, and who its father was,
and are able to describe how a red-hot mud spring
was overwhelmed and crushed and indurated, and
became something else, will tell you that Australia
—millions of years before man invented theology—■
reached into the sea hundreds of miles further east
than it does now. They will tell you that the
Barrier Reef, which is coral, superimposed on
granite, was at one time a chain of mountains in
the mainland, and that it sprung a leak, or was
scuttled by Titans, or something, and sank, taking
some thousands of square miles with it. On the
Russell River at this moment men are hydraulic-ing
for gold old river-beds that have been lifted to
mountain tops—Boo feet below, the river runs its
new course. In the great convulsion that abased
the mighty mountains from their seat and exalted
the humble and meek river-beds, the volcanic
deposits that are now scrub-lands rose and
swamped the plains. But in spots and patches
the plain-lands rose with the volcanic flow and
formed these silver-grassed pockets, so that Billy
King and Majendie and many others might have
the pocket for their camp.
They loosed the horses, ate their own meal of
tinned mutton and bread, and then sprawled and
smoked in the long grass which the heavy dews
had begun already to drench.
The big, electric, overflowing moon had left the
scrub in darkness, as if it only shone for Tecoma
Pocket, and as the moon was very large and
Tecoma Pocket is very small, the rays saturated
the night and the light appeared to drip into the
darkness of the scrub. At the scrub-foot the fire-
flies barred the blackness with sparks.
Billy King sat up cursing, and counted the
leeches as he detached them from his legs.
“Sixteen, seventeen, eighteen, nineteen; that’s
nineteen thousand quid to be added to the
Vendor’s cut.”
“ But it’s up to two millions now, is’nt it?” en-
quired Majendie lazily. “ It was the grass-seed
at Chillagoe last year that put it to a million and
a half. I charged a tanner for every speaigrass
seed I found in my pants, and I lost count of ’em
at forty millions; but if I never get’em it does me
proud to write ’em down and gloat.”
“ But what w’d you do with ’em if you did get
’em ? ”
“Well, fust of all I’d buy up all the police-
force and a few judges, and then I’d take my
enemies and hang ’em in front of the Exchange;
make ’em do the Cromwell trick, and poison the
air they once ruled. I might half-roast a few of
’em before the hanging, but I’m not sure. After
that I’d buy you a kingdom, Jacky, and a spare
crown for Sundays, and I’d make life pleasant for
some old friends I know.”
“ And what would you do for yourself ? ”
“ I’d do as I liked. If it liked me to be a pirate
—I mean the old honest sort of pirate—instead of
being the furtive highwayman on paper that I am,
I’d be a pirate, and pay the bill when settling-day
came. And for the rest I’d buy a bale of copy-
paper and write such verse that the shade of
Shakespeare would be sorry it didn’t collaborate
with me.”
After moonrise other travellers made the pocket.
There was a young Britisher (engaged in sugar-
planting) named Tottenham, who came to Australia
for experience, and found an overdraft and the
fact of his own grit—who lived by the grace of the
Colonial Sugar Company and in spite of his
mannerisms, which moved people as much to
laughing ridicule as the resultant fights with him
moved them to grief. He knew King, and having
freeted him in the wonderful tongue he called
Inglish, loosed his horses, took his rug from the
pack and joined the party at the fire.
There was Sampson, an agent at Cairns —
a man who sold everything sugar - farms,
mines, town - lots, pianos, and some alleged
pictures, the fearful and wonderful productions
of a local artist. There was Smith, the Labor-can-
didate (for just then it was the silly season in the
land) and the great white man who lives by signs
and fetishes like his black relation became swollen
with the importance of his vote ; and finally there
was a very beanpole of a Queenslander —Nazarenio
in his ignorance of a razor, and as a result his face
was almost hidden in something which was
not hair, but a tissue like silk or thistle-down. He
was a packer. Year in and year out he tailed his
sagging string of mules through the eternal
slush and over the limitless plains—travelling al-
most naked —knowing little more joy in life than
his wretched cattle, which were moving sores.
He stopped his weary beasts near King’s fire, un-
loaded them silently, not even cursing at the
brittle pack-straps and rotten gear, removed the
muzzles that had all day kept the animals in agony
but had protected their loads, asked for hot water
from King's fire, made his tea, and ate some livid
cow-beef, so hard that he made his one feeble joke
again—saying that “ the meat had been cut off the
horn.”
They asked him to their fire and he accepted
silently, for his long, lonely work had made him
dumb nearly always. But, as they talked on to
his exclusion, he felt more at home, and. at last
this long, ragged packer took his Familiar from
his saddle-pouch, and breathed into it all his
longings and the misery he could not feel to the
full. A mouth-organ, making the expressionless
articulate—
“ Whack-fa-101-ri-tooral-addity
Billy, get out of the bo’sun’s way.”
Newson —one of King’s men—had been singing
ribaldry, and while the packer played “ After the
Ball ” and other heavenly things, Newson had not
been stayed.
King spoke in an undertone, but the packer,
entranced with his own music, could not have
heard him shouting.
“Look at that chap,” said Billy. “He walks
those fraudulent tracks for ever and ever. The
gear of his harness is so spent that every time he
buckles a strap he splits a nail. His clothes are
rotten, his gear’s rotten, the tracks are rotten, the
tucker’s rotten—he’s as badly off as Sefton’s party
when they crossed York Peninsula. There’s
nothing before him for ever and ever and ever but
the potato-furrows, and the muzzling of horses to
prevent them eating their loads, and all the end-
less cruelty to them that he can’t help. And yet
he’s contented, and when he blows into that bit of
tin—he’s happy. And I grumble, and you grum-
ble, Jacky ; and Sampson grumbles, and there’s a
lot of skunks in-Melbourne who weep when the
market doesn’t jump with their swindle. Truly,
the packer-man is better than any of us—worth all
of us—his twenty shillings is worth a quid.”
“Such troubles as his,” said the Labor-candi-
date, “is just what the Labor-party is intended
for. Vote for me, and I’ll amelyerate his condi-
tion.”
Tottenham smiled. Majendie fell off his tus-
sock, and laughed himself helpless; and into
Sampson’s blue eyes came the light of battle.
“ It’s this black-labor that makes packers
poor,” continued the Labor-candidate. “If we had
no black-labor, there’d be plenty of other work for
a white man than packing.”
Sampson waxed shrill. “Man, man, man !” he
said, “are ye out of your egg? Have ye
senses and brains ? If there were no black-
labor here there’d be no Labor-candidates possible,
because the country ’ud be in the hands of the
aboriginals again. The Kanaka is the bullock-
waggon that must go before the locomotive.
When the country’s cleared and in crop the white
man can weed the cane with a motor-oar and a
cultivator. But ye don’t know. Ye’re all
wasters. Ye don’t know!” For Sampson was
full of the Tired Gospel of the North, which is

What have J done for it? I have rubbed it with

Tottenham weighed-in. “I have severwal
hundwed akars of land which would cost me £25
per akar to oleah. I lease those severwal hun-
dwed akars to a—er —Chow ! First three yeahs
he pays me nothing, fourth yeah he pays me ten
shillings per akar; fifth yeah,fifteen; sixth yeah,
one pound; seventh year, one-pound-five. So
that in seven years he has paid me ten shillings
per akar per year and he has cleahed my land.
And if he wasn’t so allowed to cleah my land I
wouldn’t —aw —cleah it at all.”
The Labor-candidate made a further attempt at
justification.
“ But the black-labor is slavery,” he said.
Sampson blazed again ; “ Why, ye don’t know
anything. They’re cared for and fed and paid
wages and they’re helping a great country to the
settlement it must have before the white man oan
take proper hold.”
“And, anyway,” said Billy King, “what is
slavery and what is freedom ? I’d rather be a
slave with the slavery of the horse—well-fed, well-
groomed, well-stabled and no whip, than I’d have
the liberty of a vote and the right to howl about
my freedom, if the vote and the howl were accom-
panied by an empty belly.”
“ But shouldn’t a democracy give every man an
equality of opportunity?” said the Labor-candidate.
Sampson, vitriolically: “ And doesn’t every man
in Australia have equality of opportunities ? Grit
enough and brains enough and the world belongs
to any man.”
“ But the world should belong to the People.”
“So it does—to some of ’em,” said Majendie—
“a few kings and queens and a few Rockefellers
and Rothschilds.”
“Well, I’m against you there,” said King.
“ Monarchy is a superstition.”
The Labor-candidate was roseate with congratu-
lations.
“As a stoodent of political science,” said he,
“you must be a Republican.”
“ It isn’t that,” Billy answered —looking at the
smoke rings from his pipe. “If I lived in a Re-
public, and I made the forty millions that I’ve
decided to wind-up with prior to becoming the
new Shakespeare- —”
“ Colossal cheek ! ” said Majendie.
“Damn front!” muttered Tottenham, regard-
ing Billy lovingly. ' 1
“Prior to becoming the second Shakespeare,”
repeated Billy ; “in a republic I should beg, bribe,
and steal my way to the Presidency—be just and
generous at the' same time to the people until they
thought me greater even than I think myself—
found an army who should be my mercenaries, buy
up all the principal newspapers ; and then, one
fine evening, put the Ministry in gaol—which they
would probably deserve anyhow—and proclaim
myself King.” ■
“As a stoodent of scientific politics,” said
Sampson, “you would do as you dam-well-
pleased.”
The Labor-candidate called Billy an enemy
of the people, but otherwise lie held his peace.
From the other fire came Newsoms new
ribaldry—
“ When first she got the rilm
Her blood began to smoke.”
But Sampson rose and cursed him into silence,
for the packer had exhausted his music-hall
idiocies and was playing “ Abide with me ” very
softly ; so softly that by-and-by even Sampson
forgot his middleman’s Toryism, and the
candidate forgot the slavery and his campaign,
and all differences of shibboleth faded into
nothingness. The argumentatives were as dumb
as parrots in a thunderstorm. Not four men sat
there, but four children—while the ragged packer
played on the foolish little mouth-organ that rest-
ful music which made the instrument not so foolish
after all. The weary, martyred horses stopped
cropping the rank grass, and, probably forgetting
their sores for a moment, looked at the humans —
for whom they did so much and who returned
them so little —with some of a human yearning.
And the eventide fell fast and the darkness
deepened as the big, shimmering, tropical moon
went to its early setting.
The packer rose, replaced his Familiar in the
saddle-pouch, and kicked the face of a salt-
hungry horse that was vainly nosing .round
the salt bags in the loading. The animal grunted
its pain, but only moved a few paces, too weary
for flight and ready to die for a lick. The packer
went to his unwashed blankets and slept—
snoring as weakly as his whiskers grew.
“Jacky Majendie, Tottenham and Sampson,”
said Billy King, making his bed as the moon
foundered in the scrub-tops—“ after that Johnny’s
playing on the dotty mouth-organ, I feel that I
forgive everybody. I won’t hang one broker.
I’ll merely make ’em apply for shares in my next
‘ cat,’ even if I have to use a revolver.”
Some great bastard-ash fell in the heart of the
scrub-lands, and the pocket echoed the crash.
The heavy, damp darkness closed on them like
curtains, and they slept. _
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
An Irishman was fined 10s. for drunken-
ness. At once another Patrick of same initials
advertised that he had no connection with the
drunken Patrick. Now Patrick, the drunkard,
advertises: “I, «T. Patrick, fined 10s. for drunken-
ness, beg to thank J. Patrick, of Ballyhooley
Lodge, for notifying that I am in no way con-
nected with his family.”
Letter received by Dunedin (M.L.) Bene-
volent Institution Trustees from an inmate of the
Institution who applied for the position as wards-
man
As you intend to advertise for a warder at £65 a year, I
respectfully apply for the situation, and can give a first-
class reference. I hold the responsible position of head-
warder in No. 3 ward. If appointed I will give £2O a year
out of my salary to some able-bodied inmate, who would
do all the dirty work, which I would superintend, and see
it done properly. With reference to my medical exper-
ience, I wish to state that I was under Surgeon-general
Stokes, of the College of Surgeons, Dublin, in 1854, where
I met with an accident from a collision with a policeman’s
baton, which knocked out a portion of my brains. Dr.
Stokes filled up the gap with a portion of the brains of a
mad doctor, who was dying at the time. I got wounded
in the back at the battle of Bull Run, America, in ’63, and
have been in all the hospitals of Maoriland with
broken ribs, broken legs, black eyes, broken head,
and numerous other complaints (external and internal)
too numerous to mention. I got burnt in the great
fire of Chicago. I have been in the Auckland,
Wellington, Christchurch, Timaru, and Dunedin hospitals,
and in each I was given up, there being no hopes for my
recovery, my temperature being over 66. In all these
hospitals I cured myself, thereby saving a most valuable
life, my own prescription—brandy and milk 24 times a
day. Gentlemen, I refer you to the medical staffs of the
above hospitals, who will satisfy you as to the above
statements. Gentlemen, I had the honor of shaking
hands with His Excellency Lord Ranfurly and the Right
Hon. R. Seddon, Premier of Maoriland, which I hope
will be sufficient guarantee of my respectability. Should
Ibe appointed, I would require £5 in advance,to buy.an
outfit. I cannot call on you personally, as lam a.prisoner
(by order) for three months, and not even permitted to go
to church on Sunday. If not appointed, I would thank
you very much if you would grant me a pass for Timaru,
where I could get work.
Gonfessio Amantis.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I gave you my heart. Be it much or little,
The gift that I gave was without reserve ;
And now that I know you, no jot or tittle
I take back from you; Ido not swerve
From the bond once bound, from the vows once
plighted ;
All yours to the end is the gift I gave—
(Would God at the first I had been clear-
sighted !) —
And you are the mistress and I am the slave.
My last illusion has passed for ever;
I have sounded your shallowness through and
through;
But the seal of my thraldom shall vanish never,
While I am I, dear, and you are you !
You’re a pestilent fraud in a comely setting,
Your falseness lacks e’en the grace of art.
But there’s no help for me, and no forgetting,
I’m damned beyond hope; you are queen of my
heart!
A month ago (think !) I never had heard of you,
Never been touched by your siren-spell.
Then mv senses were stirred by some light slight
word of you,
And I met you and fell with you—down to Hell !
So here is the body and soul (triple-cursed) of
me—
Yours to eternity ! Why repine ?
Choose your own way, since you must make the
worst of me.
What will you do with me, mistress mine ?
• Wrecked are my hopes of a life full of joy with
you—
. Toys of your granting are cheaper than dirt.
I will not rank with young asses that toy with you,
Crowding like curs at the tail of your skirt. •
Here is my case : all your favors I leave to you ;
Strike out my name from that pitiful roll.
But, spite of my manhood arid knowledge, I cleave
to you,
Love you and loathe you with all my soul !
TAS.
FRANDK MORTON.
A Bush Funeral.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ever been to a bush funeral ? I went once. Miss
Duncan had been dying for 18 years, and finally
did die on one of the hottest days of the hottest
month of the hottest year on record. Next day is
the very hottest of that awful month, and the
funeral is to leave at nine o’clock. Being a Scotch
funeral, none of the female relations go; so old
Donald Duncan, the father, is sole near mourner.
About ten buggies and twice that number of riders
are scattered around under the trees on the un-
fenced road that the four-roomed slab - house
seems to back-on to.
At last we get a start. Out from the friendly
scrub into the blazing sun, and on through a bare
crab-holey lane, following the waggonette that
does duty as a hearse. Old Donald, with shoulders
drooping, leading on his draught-chestnut; the
rest of us following in free-and-easy groups.
About two miles on, I look back. Only three
buggies in sight, but a cloud of dust on the horizon
in a paddock to the left shows that all the rest
are making for water, to fill jars and water-bags
again to last a few more miles. Next gate some
more riders join us, and three miles on we strike
a house, the last on the track. More water; more
filling of bags; more riders. Some that are
fagged turn back. We go on, only to find we have
lost the funeral, but strike the trail on a sandy
track and hurry to catch-up. At last we see them
camped under trees, all off their horses and lying
down under stringy-bark and ti-tree.
Half an hour’s spell; then on again, across a
white, sandy, blazing plain; no shade; the sun
glaring on the white sand makes us shut our eyes
and trust to luck to go the right way. Our dog
fives up here; he has had enough, and his feet are
listered, so we take him into the buggy. At
last we sight a hill, and at the foot of it—welcome
sight!—fresh' green ti-tree, showing that there is
a spring somewhere handy. Some of the riders
have located it when we get up—fresh, cold,
sparkling water. After we have all drunk they
tell us there is a dead bullock in it; but that is a
mere detail to us to-day. We camp again, scat-
tered over about a mile of country, but the day is
going and we must push on. A couple of miles
more, and we meet a buggy coming back. Finlay
M‘Fee has given in to the heat. He is lying in
the bottom of the buggy, green boughs stuck up
round him, and he is going home, 25 miles.
At last another hill, and we sight the cemetery.
The little parson, locally known as the ‘‘Jockey,'
is waiting. We form in line and follow in soberly.
“ I am the Resurrection and the Life,” drones the
quaint little white-gaitered figure, with the huge
umbrella held over his bare head. Two faded
wreaths are placed on the grave, and we file out,
to discuss sandwiches, and cake, and water, while
we look down on the little town three miles below
us. A pub., one church, a school, a bridge,
ferns, sand, scrub, and rabbits —that’s all.
We turn our backs and start wearily for home;
even yet the sun glares down fiercely. We can
only walk our horses through the deep sand ; and
so, when the stars are out, we get home.
FRAULEIN.
Apparently the Maoriland Government
has decided to throw overboard most of its goou
traditions of honesty and economy, and be a Jingo
and boom and loan-swindle Ministry, pure and
simple. Last year it had a surplus of £500,000,
and transferred £500,000 of this to the Public
Works Fund for railway building and other essen-
tial purposes. For this year it is proposed to
sacrifice £BO,OOO a year by reduced postage, to
take off duties, &c., to-the extent of another
£220,000 a year, to make, in addition, a large re
duction in the tax on mortgages, and to spend
about £200,000 a year extra on military prepara
tions. And as this is calculated to reduce nr
surplus to about nil, the good policy of paying f .
public works in part out of revenue is apparent,
to be abandoned. A £1,000,000 loan is, there
fore, proposed to go on with. So The Bulletin
holds an inquest on the corpse of Seddon’s financial
principles and hands in the verdict: “ Fatally de-
moralised by the war-drunk.”
